When the author of this book completed for you, my sons, this book which is a part of the science of medicine in its entirety and reached the ultimate goal therein in its clarity and explanation, he saw that he should complete it for you with this treatise which is a part of work done by hand.
For manual work is in our country and time utterly lacking, so much so that its science was almost lost and its effect ceased. And what remained of it were only slight diagrams in the books of our predecessors, which hands had corrected and which error and confusion had obscured until its meanings became hidden and its benefit distant.
So I saw it fitting to revive it and to compose this treatise on the path of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation, and to include images of sharp surgical instruments and all other tools of work, as this is from the increase in explanation and what is necessary for it.
And the reason why there is no skilled craftsman with his hand in our time is that the craft of medicine is long, and its possessor should first be trained in anatomy as Galen described it. So that he may understand the benefits of the organs, their forms and temperaments, their connections and separations, and knowledge of the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, origins, and the arteries, veins, pulsating and non-pulsating, and the locations of their exits.
And therefore Hippocrates said that physicians in name are many, but in deed few, especially in manual skill, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable about what we mentioned of anatomy is bound to fall into error that may kill people, as I have witnessed many who imagined themselves in this science and claimed it without knowledge or experience.
This is because I once saw an ignorant physician who incised a cystic tumor on a woman's neck, and he severed some of the arteries in the neck so that the woman bled until she died at his hands.
I saw another physician who had advanced in removing a stone from a man who had been stabbed in the tooth, and the stone was large, so he crumbled it and removed it with a piece of bladder. The man died in about three days, and I had been called to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that this was beyond my capabilities.
I saw another physician who was making a living from medicine among some of the leaders of our country.
It happened to a black boy who had a fracture in his leg near the heel with a wound, so the physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture on the wound with bandages and splints tightly and did not leave the wound any room to breathe.
Then he released him to his desires and left him for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot swelled and he was on the verge of death.
I was called to him, so I quickly untied the bandages and he felt relief and recovered from his pain, but the damage had already become entrenched in the limb and I could not restore it.
The corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and it ulcerated after a few days, greatly increasing the suffering of its owner. This is because if cancer is purely from a black humor, it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a member that can tolerate the removal of all of it.
Therefore, my son, you should know that work done by hand is divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety and work that is followed by harm in most cases.
I have warned in every place that comes from this book about the work that involves risk and danger, so you should beware of it and refuse it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to speak and revile, take care and caution for yourselves, and adorn yourselves with gentleness and deliberation.
And employ the best path leading to safety and a happy outcome, and shun dangerous and difficult diseases.
Purify yourselves from what you fear may lead to doubt in your religion and your worldly affairs, for it is the most lasting safeguard for your honor and the highest attainment in this world and the next for your station.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad disease and become bad physicians."
And this treatise has been divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
On cauterization with fire and cauterization with caustic drugs, a well-arranged chapter from the beginning to the end, and illustrations of instruments and iron cautery tools and everything needed for working by hand.
The second chapter.
On incision, dissection, cupping and bloodletting, surgery, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all well-arranged and with illustrations of instruments.
The third chapter.
On splinting, dislocations, and the treatment of ulcers and the like, all well-arranged from beginning to end, with illustrations of instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
And before we mention how to perform it, it is necessary to state the benefits and harms of cauterization, and in what temperaments it should be used.
I say that speech concerning the manner of cauterization's benefit and harm is a long discourse, a subtle science, and a hidden secret, and many sages have spoken on it.
They differed among themselves, and I have summarized a little of their speech for fear of prolixity.
I say that cupping is generally beneficial for all bad humors, whether with or without material, namely hot and dry humors without material.
As for dry humor with material, they differed on it; some said that cupping is beneficial in it and others said the opposite that cupping is not suitable for a disease that comes from heat and dryness because the nature of fire is hot and dry.
And it is impossible to be cured of a hot dry disease by a hot dry remedy, and he who says the opposite said that cupping with fire may benefit from a hot dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you reach a cooling for the body of man.
And I say what he said because experience has revealed it to me many times, but it should not be imagined by anyone except who has been accustomed and trained in the matter of cupping with extensive training. And he stood upon the difference of people's temperaments and the state of illnesses in themselves and their causes, symptoms, and duration of time.
As for the rest of the temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold and wet diseases; all physicians have agreed on it and did not differ in its benefit.
And know, O sons, that the secret of treatment by cupping with fire and its excellence over cupping with burning medicine is because fire is a single element whose action does not exceed the member that was cauterized and does not harm another connected member except for a slight harm.
Cupping with burning medicine may exceed its action to what is beyond the members and may cause a disease in the member that is difficult to treat and may even kill, but fire, due to its honor and generosity of essence, does not do that except if it was excessive.
This has been made clear to us by experience, through long service and attention to the craft, and by arriving at the truth of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse, and if it were not unbecoming of my book, I would relate to you in this treatise a hidden secret and the manner of its doing in bodies, its negation of diseases, with philosophical, demonstrative speech that would pierce your understandings.
Know, O sons, that they have differed concerning the time that is suitable for cautery and have made the best time to be spring.
And I say that cautery is suitable in every time, for the harm caused by time is surpassed by the benefit that is brought about by cautery. Especially if the cautery is from necessary, intense pains that cannot be delayed, lest what is feared should followâ€”a destruction greater than any internal harm caused by time.
Do not entertain, O sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cautery which cures a disease has no return and make it obligatory.
For the matter is not as they think, for cautery is like a medicine that changes the temperament and dries up the moistures which are the cause of pains arising.
Except that cautery excels medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the illness may return at some time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength and establishment of his illness, and what is prepared in his body from the gathering of superfluities and negligence in acquiring them from foods and suchlike causes.
Except that the illness in which cautery is used be a delicate one and in an organ with little superfluity and moisture. As for the removal of a tooth for pain and the like, it may be that the pain will not return in it, at least.
As for the common saying that cautery is the last resort of medicine, it is a correct statement, but not in the way they understand it, because they believe that no treatment is beneficial by medicine or otherwise after cautery has taken place, and the matter is otherwise.
Rather, the meaning of "cautery is the last resort of medicine" is that when we have used various treatments for a disease and those medicines have not been successful, then we use cautery as a last resort, and it succeeds; therefore, it is said that "cautery is the last resort of medicine." Not according to the meaning that the common people and many ignorant doctors have gone to.
The ancients have mentioned that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they said this because of the temperance of gold and its noble nature, and they said that it does not cause suppuration at the site of the cautery, and this is not true at all.
Because I have tried it and found that it only does so in some bodies and not others, and cautery with it is better and more excellent than iron as they said, except that if you heat the iron in the fire from the gold, it will not become clear to you when it has heated up to the degree that you want for the redness of gold.
And because it quickly cools down, and if you increase the heat on it, it melts in the fire and spills, so the maker falls into trouble from that. Therefore, cautery with iron has become faster and closer to correctness for us to work with, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cautery into sections organized from the head to the foot so that it is easy for the student to find what he wants from it, God willing.
Chapter one.
On cauterizing the head: a single cautery.
This cupping is beneficial for the predominance of moisture and cold over the brain, which are the cause of headache and frequent catarrhs from the head towards the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache and sore throat. And in general for every disease that arises from cold such as paralysis, epilepsy, stroke and the like of these diseases.
The image of this cupping is to order the patient first to vomit with a laxative medicine purified for the head for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, his age and habit.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, then seat him between your hands squatting, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes and where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-oil lamp which has this shape.
Expose from the bone the size of a toothpick head or a caraway seed, then lift your hand, otherwise replace your hand with the same iron or another if it has cooled. Until you see from the bone what I mentioned to you, then take some salt, dissolve it in water and soak a cotton ball in it and place it on the spot and leave it for three days.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in ghee on it and leave it until the crustiness from the fire goes away, then treat it with the fourfold ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they said that the wound, the longer it remains open and discharges pus, the better and more beneficial it is.
And some mentioned that the skin should be burned down to the bone, and hold the iron until part of the thickness of the bone burns, then remove what has been burned from the bone afterwards and treat it.
Others said that cupping should be excessive so as to strongly affect the bone until a piece falls off, like the shape of a small horseshoe or a pellet, and they claimed that steam from the head is released from that spot and the wound is left open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals, and I do not see these two types of burns at all except in some people and by chance. Leaving it with me is best and goodbye if that is the case, for the head weakens when its natural connection is separated, as we have seen in all other organs.
Especially when the patient's head is weak by nature, and the first type of burn is safer and better with me, and I will use it. Do it and be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Burns on the head also.
If a persistent pain occurs throughout the head and this continues in the patient, and poultices, ointments, compresses, and bandages are used, especially if he has been burned with the single burn that we have described and nothing benefits him from that, then look. If the patient's head is strong in nature and not weak, and he feels a severe cold, then apply another burn over it slightly. Then apply a burn to each horn of his head so that the thickness of the skin goes away and is exposed from the bone in the amount we have described. Apply a burn to the back of his head in the place known as the axe, and lighten your hand in this one, do not expose the bone because the patient will experience severe pain from it, unlike the pain of all other burns on the head. I will mention this burn in its place.
The iron with which the horns and back of the head are burned should be finer than the iron with which the middle of the head is burned, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
In non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in the temple of the head with a headache and the pain extends to the eye, let the patient purge with purifying medications for the head and use all the treatment I mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If that does not work, then burn it on two sides. Either with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, it is to take one clove of garlic, peel it and cut off its ends on both sides, then make an incision in the painful area from the temple with a wide spatula until there is a space under the skin that can fit the clove.
Insert it under the skin until it disappears, then tie it tightly with strong bandages and leave it for fifteen hours, then untie it and remove the garlic, leaving the wound for two or three days, then carry on.
Cover it with cotton soaked in clarified butter until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medicines that I have mentioned in the eighteenth article on burning medicines.
As for cauterizing it with iron, it is done as follows: heat the iron which has this shape:
It is called a nail iron because its head is shaped like a nail with some grooves and a small projection in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while turning the iron slightly, burning a depth equal to half the thickness of the skin. Lift your hand so as not to burn the artery below, which would cause bleeding, then soak a cotton ball in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then carry on with the cotton soaked in clarified butter, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, you can cauterize this migraine with the pointed tip of the iron, especially preserving it from cutting the artery in this non-chronic migraine.
Chapter Four.
On Cauterizing Chronic Migraine.
If you have treated the migraine with the aforementioned treatment and the division of diseases, and the treatment has not been effective, and you have seen from the cause what cannot be treated by the aforementioned first cauterization with medicine or fire. You should heat the surgical iron until it glows white, then apply it to the area of pain in a line half a finger long or so, and leave your hand once while tightening it until it cuts the artery and reaches near the bone.
However, you should be careful not to touch the moving jawbone during chewing, as this could burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it, causing paralysis.
And be wary and cautious of bleeding from the artery you have cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for those who are ignorant of what they are doing and are not experienced practitioners and abandon the work first.
The management of bleeding that arises from the artery will be mentioned in its proper place in the book, God willing. If you see from the cause what this cautery cannot treat.
And you saw the patient's body capable of enduring it, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery that we mentioned in the chapter on severing the artery with a double-edged iron, for it is better and more effective than this.
Chapter Five.
Cauterization for Earaches.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other treatments mentioned in the division, but the pain does not subside, heat the iron called "the point," which has this shape.
Then, after heating it, apply dots around the entire ear as it turns or all around them if the pain is in both. Remove the cautery from the root of the ear slightly after learning the location with ink. The cautery should be the size of ten dots in each ear or so, then treat the areas until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization with a hot iron.
The type of cautery that treats diseases caused by phlegm, as mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
Avoid cauterizing the type that occurs from dryness and nerve constriction.
If you have treated this type of cautery with irrigations, poultices and gargles and your treatment has not been successful, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second slightly below the cheekbone, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization opposite the affected side, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The image of the cautery is to make a cautery opposite the upper tip of the ear, slightly below the horn of the head, and another on the cheekbone, its length being the length of the thumb.
Lower the cautery with your hand until it burns half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the iron, which is a type of scalpel that has been advanced in its form, but it is slightly finer than it, as you see. The scalpel should have a slight increase in thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization for chronic stroke.
If the stroke has persisted and you have treated it as we mentioned, and your treatment has not been successful and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize them four times. On every century of its head, a cautery and a cautery in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and a cautery at the back of the head as previously stated.
The description of the cauteries as mentioned above, and a cautery may also be applied to the mouth of the stomach for greater effect, then treated as previously stated.
Chapter Eight.
Cauterization for Forgetfulness.
That which is from phlegm, the patient should first be given large doses of emetics and purifying herbs for the brain.
Then his whole head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, as written in the article on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for it is a type of cautery.
And do this on the rank mentioned there, if he is cured by it, otherwise cauterize three times in the back of his head, lined up from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the space between each cautery the thickness of a finger, then treat the cauteries as previously stated. If you want to increase it and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cautery again.
If you want to increase it further, cauterize the temples and treat them until he is cured. The cautery iron should be olivewood as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
Cauterization for Paralysis and Relaxation of the Whole Body.
You should first proceed with purifying the head with emetics and what we have mentioned, then shave the patient's head, and cauterize in the middle of the head, on each temple, and on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If you need, in cases of relaxation of the body, more than that and the patient can tolerate it and the disease is strong and persistent, cauterize four times on the vertebrae of his back. Heat the cautery until it burns most of the skin, then raise your hand and treat it as previously mentioned so that it heals, God willing, and the iron should be olive.
Chapter Ten.
On Epilepsy.
Only an epileptic whose seizures are caused by phlegm should be cauterized. First, his brain should be purged with large enemas and all the treatments mentioned in the section on purging should be administered if the patient is an adult and able to take medication.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medication, gargles and purified masticatory should be used to purge the brain several days beforehand along with an improved diet.
Then his head should be shaved and one cautery should be applied to the middle of the head as previously described, and another cautery to the back of his head. And on each temple of his head, one cautery should be applied. If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, apply the cauteries mentioned for paralysis and relaxation of the body, extending from the nape of the neck to the lower back. The iron should be olive as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, make the cautery gentler in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On Cauterization for Melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, apply the cauteries we mentioned for paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess leaning towards blackness and the patient's body is moist and corrupt, purge his brain as previously mentioned in the section.
Then shave the patient's head and make a tight cake of linen in the shape of a circle, then lower it onto the center of his head. The patient should sit upright, holding on to each side, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a moderate fire as much as the finger can bear when inserted, then empty it in the middle of its head in the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week with all his good treatment until he recovers, if God wills.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures without holding your hand on the iron, for this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply cotton soaked in butter or chicken fat to it.
Chapter Twelfth.
On cauterizing the descending water in the eye.
If the beginning of descending water in the eye becomes apparent to you by the signs mentioned in the division, hasten and make the patient clean his head and protect it from all moisture and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize a spot in the middle of it, then cauterize two spots on the temples if the beginning of descending water is in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the iron all the veins and arteries under the skin, let the cauteries be long in width as the temples, and prevent the bleeding of blood.
If you see anything of it, cut it on the spot with any treatment possible, and I will come to wisdom in cauterizing the arteries and cutting them while preventing bleeding.
It may also be cauterized under the two bones in the nape of the neck with two strong cauteries.
Chapter Thirteenth.
On cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and constant, and they were previously from the veins and arteries on the outside of the head, from outside the head, and you are certain that this is from cold superfluity, thick and phlegmatic, then cauterize the cautery which I described precisely in the beginning of descending water. Two cauteries in the middle of the head and two on the temples and two in the nape below the bones, and if you need more cauterize one on each side of the tail of the eye at the end of the eyebrow with a small iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On Cauterizing the Nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment does not work, hurry to make the patient fast for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle cautery with an olive iron. Then cauterize it with a nail iron, two above the eyebrows under the hair a little, and beware of the artery, do not cut it. This is an image of the nail iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On Cauterizing Relaxation of the Eyelid.
If an eyelid relaxes from illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cautery using this crescent-shaped iron.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows a little two on each side, keeping away from the temples, and the length of each cautery is along the eyebrow.
Do not overdo your hand with the cautery, but cauterize only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the iron will be as follows.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Cauterizing the Eyelid.
If its eyelashes turn inward and irritate the eye, cautery is done in two types. Either cautery with fire or cautery with a burning medication. As for cauterizing it with fire, you order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone if he is one who plucks them so that they grow long and even.
If it irritates him when they grow, tighten his eyelids with a bandage so that they do not move until they sprout. When they have sprouted and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then draw on the eyelid with ink a mark in the shape of an aspalath leaf.
The sign should begin near the eyelashes, then a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a boy is placed under the eyelid; then this iron shaped like it is heated.
Then it is cauterized on the shape you have taught little by little in many times until the surface of the skin which is like an aspen leaf burns entirely, its appearance being particular and a sign of your work's success being that you see the eyelid become wrinkled and the hair rises from the flesh of the eye.
Then raise your hand at that time and leave it for three days, then carry a cotton swab with ghee on it until the scab comes off; then treat it with ointment so that it heals if God wills, for if something of the hair returns after a while and the eyelid relaxes.
Then cauterize that place again as you did first, and if the hair is in the lower eyelid, pluck it until it returns to its natural position so that it does not pull on the eye; as for cautery with a burning medicine, order the patient to leave off the eyelashes until they lengthen and become even.
Then make a shape of an aspen leaf out of paper, then take from the usual soap and quicklime not yet slaked, one dirhem weight or thereabouts from each, and grind them all together thoroughly, doing this quickly so that it does not dry. Then spread it on the paper you have made in the shape of an aspen leaf and place it on one or both eyelids, putting a cotton swab soaked in egg white underneath the eye, with the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on top of the medicine and press it a little, moving it whenever the patient feels the burning sensation from the medicine because he finds it pleasurable like fire, so as long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger; and when the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And observe, if you see the eyelid has risen as it rises when scorched by fire or cutting, otherwise apply more of the medicine to the spot where it did not take effect and did not blacken until your work is complete and the eye is scorched; then place cotton soaked in butter upon it until the scab comes off.
Then treat it with palm salve or another until it heals, and you should, when working, take the greatest care that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye; for if the eyelid relaxes after a few days and we fear some hair will fall into the eye.
Reapply the medicine to that part of the eyelid which has relaxed, especially as you did at first; then treat it until it heals. This is an image of a fig leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size, so the scorching should be done accordingly. This is not a subtle path to righteousness for one who has practiced this craft.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On the Naasur that Appears in the Eye Socket.
If you have treated the naasur as described in the divisions of diseases and your treatment has not been successful, it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and have a servant hold his head firmly between your hands so that it does not move or shake.
Then place cotton wool soaked in egg white or the saliva of a sparrow on his eyes, and heat the iron which is pictured here.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather from one end, which is where the cautery will be applied. If you wish, it can be pierced at the other end; if you wish, it can be closed off like a pomegranate seed. However, this hollow one is best for your work, God willing.
Then you press the abscess if it is open, and the pus will come out; you dry it. If it is not open, you open it and extract the pus. Then you place the cautery iron on it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
Withdraw your hand a little way from the eye towards the nose so that you do not make a mistake or disturb the patient, and the iron does not fall into the eyeball and spoil it. If you reach the bone in the first cautery, otherwise repeat the cautery a second time if you need to.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in butter and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a sharp, eating medicine so that the bone is exposed, and scrape it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing an abscess, mentioned by some of the ancients, is to deliberately cut into the abscess and then place a slender cone into the same incision; its shape is as follows.
Pour into it a weight of lead equal to a dirham, melted. Hold the cone firmly with your hand so that it does not move at all, so that the molten lead does not flow into the eye. You should place cotton soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
For the lead will burn the abscess and heal it in an astonishing way. If the abscess heals with the cautery and treatment mentioned, that is sufficient. Otherwise, you must resort to using nasal puncturing and redirecting the abscess back into the nasal passage as will be explained in its proper place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the incision of the lip.
A fissure often occurs in the lip called a harelip, especially in the lips of boys, it often happens. If you have treated this fissure with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment was unsuccessful, then heat a small, smooth iron in this manner.
Make its hollow on the sharpness of a knife, then place it, protected by haste, in the fissure itself until the cautery reaches the depth of the fissure, then treat it with cerate so that it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the cauterization of a wound in the mouth.
If a tumor appears in the gum, or palate, or roots of molars, then it dries and bursts, and pus flows from it, forming an ulcer, and you treat it and treatment is unsuccessful, then you should heat an iron to the extent that fits within the ulcer.
Then insert it, protected, into the hole of the ulcer and hold your hand until the iron, protected, reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned from treatment until it heals, God willing.
If the matter is cut off and healed, otherwise it is necessary to examine the place and remove the decayed bone according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On cauterizing teeth and relaxed gums.
If the gums relax due to moisture and the teeth move, and you treat them with medicines and they are unsuccessful, then place the head of the patient in your lap, then heat the iron that will be shown after this, after placing the tube on the tooth.
Insert the iron into it, protected by haste, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then repeat the iron several times as you wish, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
He holds it for an hour and throws it away; the mobile tooth becomes fixed, the relaxed gum tenses, and the corrupt moisture dries up.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Pain in the Tooth.
If the toothache is from cold or there is a worm in it and medicine does not relieve it, cautery is performed in two ways: either with butter or with fire.
As for cautery with butter, take cow butter and boil it in an iron ladle or a shell. Then take cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, dip it into the boiling butter, place it on the aching tooth quickly, and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth. If you wish, you can dip a wick or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then put a heated iron on top of it until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterizing it with fire, take a copper or iron tube, making sure its body is somewhat thick so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the branding iron, which has a pointed shape, and place it on the same tooth, holding your hand until the iron cools down. Repeat this several times.
The pain will go away either that same day or the next. After cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is a picture of the branding iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, to the extent possible. This is a picture of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On Cauterizing Warts.
If warts are from phlegm and cold humors, and they do not mature with medication, and you desire rapid maturation, heat a hollow branding iron, which has this shape, with openings at both ends.
Let the smoke escape when cauterizing from the other end and place it protected on the same tumor once or twice if needed, until it reaches the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, place the iron according to the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter on it until what was burned by the fire is gone, then treat it with ointment and wicks until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If moistures prevail in the trachea, especially if it is accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxatives, then cauterized with a cautery in the hollow of the neck at the base of the pharynx in the lower part.
Be careful not to reach the pharynx with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin, then cauterize another cautery at the joint of the neck in the last cartilage of it vigorously, the iron being nail-shaped as previously described.
Then treat it with what was mentioned before until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-four.
On cauterization for lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moistures and the patient does not have fever or phthisis and the disease is chronic, cauterize two cauteries above the clavicles in the low soft empty spots and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The iron should be nail-shaped as previously described, or if you wish, cauterize with a point iron called "the point" whose shape was previously shown in the cautery for earache and pain. The points should be from thirty to near that number.
Then treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it is healed. It is possible to make an iron with three branches in this shape, and hasten cauterization with it because you cauterize three burns at once with it. This is the image of the iron.
Chapter twenty-five.
In the cauterization of the armpit.
If the head of the humerus has become dislocated due to slippery moistures, or if it has not been fixed when it was returned so that it becomes habitual for it to return and then dislocate at the slightest movement as we have seen, then the jaw should first be returned. Then the patient should lie on his back or on his right side, and then lift the skin inside the armpit upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inwards.
Then heat the iron with two branches, which is pictured like this.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cautery will be four burns. It is also possible to cauterize with an iron with three branches so that the shape of the cautery will then be six burns, and the branches should be on the neck of the humerus.
This is the image of the iron with three branches.
The number may be increased by one so that the cauteries are eight, then apply pounded alum mixed with salt to the cautery and bind the patient at rest and do not move the limb for a time until it becomes strong. If the dislocation is upwards, this rarely happens.
So cauterize above the elbow with one good puncturing cautery or many punctuating cauteries, for the joint will become strong and the moisture will go away and the patient will recover.
Chapter twenty-six.
In the cauterization of the abdomen.
If cold and much moisture occur in the stomach until they expel it from its temperament, and frequent fluxes come to it, and it is treated with kinds of treatment but are not successful, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his legs and hands, and then be cauterized three times. A cautery is made under the spoon of the chest the size of a finger with a nail iron, and two cauteries are made below the sides of the one cautery so that the shape of the cauteries becomes triangular, and they are spaced apart lest they come together when they heal. The depth of the cauteries should be one-third the thickness of the skin, and the shape of the cauteries should be.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, you may cauterize one large cautery in the middle of the stomach in this manner.
The iron with which this single cautery is made should be in the shape of a circle in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by points for those who are frightened of this cautery, and that is to draw points on the stomach to the desired extent with ink, then cauterize them with a pointed iron, then treat it with the treatment previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seventh.
On Cauterization of a Cold Liver.
If pain arises in the liver from cold and moisture or from a strong wind until it has exited from its natural temperament excessively, and the patient is treated with what we have mentioned in the division but it is not successful. Then the patient should lie on his back and three cauteries should be drawn with ink in this shape and exact amount on the liver below the ribs, where a human's elbow ends.
And the distance between each cautery should be the thickness of a finger, and the cautery should be straight along the body, and do not grip tightly with the iron.
Let the amount that you burn of the thickness of the skin be half, no more. And let the patient stand on his feet; if he is not able to stand, let him lie down with his legs extended and arms raised. This is an image of the iron.
And you may cauterize these ulcers with a surgical iron if you have skill and experience in the craft, taking care not to press too hard lest you burn all the skin and reach the abdomen, for the skin there is thin, so be warned.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On Cauterizing a Liver Abscess.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know whether that tumor is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will experience heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be sharp intense pain; and you see that physicians have grown weary trying to treat it, then the patient should lie on his back and a poultice should be applied to the warm spot.
Then heat an iron in the fire; it is an iron resembling a hammer, and this is its image. Cauterize it with a single cautery to burn all the skin and end the cautery in the peritoneum so that all the pus comes out, then treat it with treatment for abscesses until it heals.
This type of cautery should not be used except by one whose training in medicine has been long and who has treated these diseases many times through experience; only then should he undertake such work, and leave it with me if not.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On Cauterizing a Fistula.
Those who came before me mentioned cauterizing a cold fistula in this way: take one of the long dried roots of the zedoary plant, as thick as your finger, dip it into oil, and heat it in fire. Then make one cautery between the connection of the collarbone to the neck and two small ones below the jawbone, slightly inclined towards the side under the chin, and two large ones above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
Also make two cauteries between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backwards, and another in the middle of the chest and one above the stomach, and three cauteries from behind: one between the shoulders and two on either side of the spine below the cautery that is between the hands.
Your hand should not be deepened with the cautery, but rather a branding should be made on the surface of the skin. Some of the ancients have mentioned that some people used to use an iron poker, shaped like a horseshoe, which they would heat and insert between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
The tumor is then extracted as we described in the case of a liver tumor, and with this cautery method there is great danger; either the patient will die immediately or an incurable fistula will develop at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
Cauterization of the Spleen.
If you have treated spleen disease with the remedies mentioned in the division and your treatment has not been successful, then cauterize it in three ways, all of which are correct: one is to make three or four cauteries lined up along the length of the spleen.
Arrange them in the shape of liver cauteries as previously described, with a distance of the thickness of a finger or slightly more between each cautery. The iron should be of the same type as mentioned for liver cauteries, and your hand should not be deepened with the cautery. The patient should be lying on his back.
The second method is to lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends and raise the skin across the width of the body so that the cauteries will lie along the length of the body.
Then insert the two cauterizing irons, well-protected, until the skin is pierced on the other side. Then withdraw the iron so that there are four burns. If you wish to cauterize with the other iron which has three points.
Then treat the burn site after leaving it to drain pus for many days, for this is more effective than all the previous treatments mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On Cauterization for Dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial for ascitic dropsy, especially if you have treated the patient with the types of treatment mentioned in the division and your treatment has not been successful. Then you should cauterize four burns around the navel and one burn on the abdomen.
Another burn on the liver, a burn on the spleen, and two burns behind his back between the shoulder blades - one opposite his chest and the other opposite his abdomen. The depth of the burn should be close to the thickness of the skin. Then leave the burn open to drain pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient's treatment after cauterization with what is necessary so that he benefits from both, and his recovery is hastened. The shape of the iron used to cauterize the back is nail-shaped as previously described, and the one used to cauterize the front is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On Cauterization of the Feet and Legs.
If the feet become swollen in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then they should be cauterized on the back of the foot in the hollow between the little toe and the next toe.
Hold your hand steady with the iron and do not bend it, then lift your hand and do not return it at all, for yellow water will be secreted and the iron will be in this shape.
Then cauterize two burns on each leg, the burn being made with the pointed end of the iron. The burn should be along the length of the leg: one below the knee and the other lower down towards the middle of the leg. There should also be two burns on each thigh.
Leave the fistula open without treatment for a long time so that water may discharge from it, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the fistula of diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and moisture until it weakens the holding and digestive powers that are in the stomach and intestines, and it is treated with various treatments but does not recover, and you see the patient capable of a fistula and strong, then make a large fistula on his stomach. As previously mentioned in the stomach fistula, with a circular iron and four small fistulas around the navel, delicately made with a fine awl-iron, and a fistula on the cotton above the bone, large or two.
If you see the moisture is abundant and the patient can tolerate it, then make a fistula on the groin and one on each buttock, and perhaps add two small fistulas to the stomach near the large one. For this is a successful treatment that does not fail in its benefit if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the fistula of hemorrhoids.
If there are many chronic hemorrhoids in the anus, or one, and they are from thick cold humors or corrupted moisture, and they are treated with what we mentioned in the division but the treatment does not succeed, then make for the patient three fistulas at the bottom of the sacrum under the table a little, triangular.
And a fistula under the navel the size of two fingers, and if you think his stomach has become cold and his food does not digest and you see his face swollen, then make for him a large fistula on the stomach as previously mentioned and one on his liver. And another on his spleen with a fine awl iron, and leave the fistulas open for a while, then treat them until he recovers if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
In the matter of ulcers.
After cutting them, if the warts are cut, take a heated iron resembling a spatula and insert it, still hot, into the very same warts that have been cut, and press your hand until the iron reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described and then treat them with ointments suitable for them until they are healed. And if you cauterize a large wart also on cotton, it is more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the Cauterization of a Fistula That Occurs in the Buttocks and Its Surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to incision and the procedure that we have described in its place, and they refuse it, perhaps they will be cured by cauterization. And if a fistula occurs in one of them, and pus and foul moisture flows from it, then first you should measure it with a thin probe.
Then know the depth of it with the probe, and take a heated iron resembling a spatula and insert it hot into the very same fistula, straight along the path of the fistula and to the depth that the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt humors are burned away once or twice or three times according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein; and if the fistula leads to a bladder tumor or an intestinal tumor.
So be careful of all these places, and only do this if the fistula is in a place of flesh and you have determined that it is not penetrable; then treat the area until it heals. If the area closes and the discharge ceases and remains so for some time, know that it has been perfectly cured.
And if the discharge does not cease, know that it is penetrable or there is a large corrupt bone in its depth or something similar to what will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven
On the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys due to cold or harsh wind and a gathering results from this, it should be cauterized on both sides above the kidney, with a branding iron as previously mentioned, three times in succession, forming a line of three cauterizations for greater benefit.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On cautery of the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and moisture, so that the patient cannot retain urine, cauterize below the perineum on the bladder where pubic hair begins, one cautery to the right of the perineum and another to the left.
The cautery on each side should be the size of a thumb joint, and an additional cautery should be performed below the lower back or two if necessary, using the previously mentioned branding iron.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On cautery of the womb.
If a substance of cold and moisture forms in the womb, preventing conception and corrupting its humors, making menstruation difficult, or if pain occurs during menstruation, three cauteries should be applied around the perineum as described for cautery of the bladder. And a cautery on the cotton below the lower back or two, using a branding iron.
Chapter Forty.
On cautery for hip dislocation.
Erroneous humors may sometimes accumulate around the hip joint, causing it to dislocate. The sign of this is that one leg will be longer than the other when measured against each other and a void will be felt at the site of dislocation. In this case, a semi-circular cautery should be applied directly to the hip joint itself.
After you have learned with the pen about truth as it revolves to fall in the middle of a circle and let the iron which presents its form be in the belly, if this iron does not come to you then take three cauteries with the large olive iron.
Make the depth of the cautery according to the thickness of all the skin and then treat it until it recovers, God willing.
Chapter Forty-One.
In Cauterization of Sciatica.
If pain occurs in the right hip and its cause is cold and moisture, and you have treated the patient as mentioned in the division and the treatment has not been effective and time has passed, then it is necessary for the patient to ease thick humors with hellebore or colocynth and the like.
Then cauterize and direct the cautery in two ways, either with burning medications or with fire. Cautery with fire is done in many ways, one of which is to cauterize on the right hip itself three triangular cauteries like this.
Make their depth appropriate and the distance between each cautery according to the thickness of a finger, using an olive iron. There may be a cautery in the middle on the head of the hip itself, making four cauteries. If you wish, you may cauterize with the circle mentioned previously in the cautery for dislocation of the hip, one cautery.
Let the circle encompass the whole hip and burn all the thickness of the skin, this is the characteristic of cautery in sciatica if the pain is constant to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
But if it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two cauteries on the thigh in the place the patient indicates pain and one cautery above the ankle bone by four fingers to the lateral side slightly, using a thin iron. The depth of the cautery should be according to the thickness of the skin only.
And if he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize it where he points with a point iron three or four times, or more if necessary. And if he indicates pain below the knee toward the leg, cauterize there one small cautery.
And be careful in all your cauteries not to burn a nerve or large artery, lest you thereby inflict on the patient a bad malady or lameness.
I have seen one or two who cauterized above the ankle and pressed hard during the cautery, so that the leg swelled until the swelling reached the foot, and it all became ulcerated and corrupted the whole leg; then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is on both sides, cauterize them in this same manner.
Some of the wise scholars have mentioned that in cauterizing the hip, this is the way to do it: Make a kind of cup out of iron, its diameter half a cubit, and at its mouth make it as thick as the kernel of a date or slightly less. Inside this cup there should be another cup, and a third cup within that.
The space between each pair of cups should be the width of a thumb joint, and the cups should be open on both sides, their height being about one or two thumb joints. A handle made of iron is securely fixed to them, and this is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and the sparks are removed, then it is placed on the hip bone; the patient should be reclining on his sound side and it cauterizes three circular cauteries at one time, then it is left for three days and dressed with butter. The wound is left open for several days.
Then it is treated with ointment until it heals. The author of this book says: This type of cautery is rarely used by us because of its ugliness and frightening appearance, and few are found who can endure it, although it is a good cautery for those who can bear it and are struck in the proper place.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it is to make two cups resembling a throat into which the molten lead from copper or iron will descend.
Their edges should have a height equal to two or three knots, and the space between them should be the thickness of a finger, with the bottom open and the top closed, while their sides are held together in this manner.
Then you lower it onto the affected area of the hip and thigh, with the patient lying on his right side. You firmly hold your hand in place and then pour hot vinegar between the two circles. Hold it for a duration of an hour glass and the patient will endure its sting and burning, as he will feel a pleasure like that of fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then you lift your hand with the instrument and wipe all the vinegar from the hip with fresh water, then leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears. Leave it for a few more days so that pus may flow from it, for this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for him that will hold water, just as you did for the hip. This is the description of hot vinegar, and its effectiveness has been proven in my treatise on the Correction of Medicines.
Take one part each of saltpeter and unquenched lime, crush them together, and place them in a new pot with a single small hole in its bottom, just large enough for a mortar to fit through. Place another glazed pot underneath the bottom of this pot.
Pour fresh water over the saltpeter and lime until it covers them by a finger's breadth, after firmly pressing them together with your hand. Leave the pot until the hot vinegar drips into the glazed pot below, then collect all of that water and pour it onto fresh lime and saltpeter.
Then it becomes very sharp and can be used for many medical procedures and to cauterize all the limbs, because it acts like fire itself. Some medications can also cauterize the hip, such as thafsia, honey of bladderwrack, and lime with soap ground up.
Galen related a story about an ancient man's treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly exaggerating its importance and claiming that no other treatment was needed and that it cured with a single application.
He even claimed that a person might enter the bath carried in and leave cured. The treatment involves taking green chisturaj, if not available, use fresh dried chisturaj. Grind it with some fat and apply it to the hip where it hurts, or on the leg or thigh.
Bind it tightly and leave it for three hours or as long as the patient feels relief from the burning sensation. Then put them in a bath; if their body sweats, put them in the tub. The pain will go away and they will be cured, God willing. If not cured, repeat the poultice after ten days.
God willing, they will be cured. Know that no one should cauterize with these medications except after the body has been purged. Dioscorides mentioned that burning sciatica with goat hair is beneficial.
Cauterization should be done as follows: Take wool soaked in old oil and place it on the deep spot between the thumb and index finger, closer to the index finger. Then take a dry goat's hair.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another one and continue doing so until the sensation reaches the middle of the arm to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called hair cauterization.
Chapter Forty-Two
On Pain in the Back.
Pain in the back may arise from many causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It is caused by the effusion of cold, moist matter, and cupping alone falls into this category.
When pain is caused by the effusion of cold matter, after the patient has vomited with a hot drink or similar remedy, he should be cupped on his back where the pain is located in three rows across the width of the table itself. After marking the location with ink, five or more cups should be applied in each row, depending on the patient's constitution and strength as you judge it.
The cupping should be done with a point-iron, or if you prefer, three or four cups can be applied with a medium-sized nail-iron in this manner.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On Cupping for the Beginning of a Swelling.
This ailment often occurs in young children, and its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving. You will find a protuberance at the end of his vertebrae which protrudes above all other protuberances.
If you see this and want to stop it, cup him with an iron that is round in shape like this. Let each cupping be taken on all sides of the vertebra equally. If you prefer, cup around the vertebra with a point-iron in two or three rows, the points being close together; then treat the area as we have described until it is healed.
Remember to use cupping only for swellings that are due to nerve tension.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On Cupping for Gout and Joint Pains.
If joint pains are caused by the effusion of cold, moist matter into any part of the body, and if the pain occurs in the legs, physicians usually call it gout.
If cold gout is treated with the types of treatments mentioned in the division and the pain does not go away, then cauterization will remove it. This is done by cauterizing after vomiting around the joints of the legs with many cauterizations, using an olive-wood iron medium in size as shown in this figure.
And if you need to cauterize on the face of the man, do so with a puncturing iron. If the pains rise to the knees or to all the joints, which often occurs, then cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-wood iron.
If you need more than this cauterization, then do so and do not deepen your hand with the cautery, but make it about the thickness of the skin only. If the pains rise to the hips or the back, use what we mentioned about cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, then cauterize around the wrists as if drawing two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, then cauterize each knuckle with a point and on the back of the hand. If the pains rise after a few days to the elbows or to the shoulders
cauterize them on each side, and do not neglect the patient from good treatment and taking medicine. For if he improves his diet and expels phlegm, he will be healed with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On Cauterizing the Gouty.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestines and fat descend to the testicle, and this is recent and near the beginning, then you should order the patient to abstain from food for a day and use laxatives that will expel stool from his bowels.
Then have him lie down on his back between your hands and order him to hold himself until the fat or intestines protrude, then push them back in with your finger. Then draw a mark under the hernia on the pubic bone, shaped like a half-circle with its ends pointing upward towards the body.
Then heat this iron in the shape.
Until it comes white, throwing sparks, then return the intestine or the rupture to its place, then a servant places his hand on the spot so that the intestine does not protrude, and after the patient's thighs have been separated and a cushion has been placed under him, another servant binds his legs.
And another on his chest holding his hands, then the iron is lowered onto the same mark and your hand with the iron stands straight and holds it until it reaches the bone or returns it again if it did not reach the bone the first time.
And preserve your effort from the protrusion of the intestine while you cauterize it so as not to burn it, for this would cause the patient either death or a great affliction. Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. The cautery iron for boys should be gentle according to their ages and for adults according to theirs.
Then treat the cautery site with butter after three days until the dryness of the fire is gone, then treat it with all kinds of ointments until it heals and let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound closes.
And his food during his treatment should be what softens his stomach so that the intestine does not protrude when he strains and defecates, then if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and hold it for another forty days, and reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud shouting.
For if he uses this treatment in this way, he will be completely cured, God willing. I will mention the treatment of hernia by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is in its early stages, if you want it not to increase, cauterize a circular cautery on it according to its size. Let what is burned of the skin be like a third of it, then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients have mentioned that a hernia should be cauterized with a triangular iron in this manner.
After you have learned about the hernia with a pen and make the line that is across in the upper side of the hernia, and the other line to the bottom, then lower your hand in the middle with one cauterizing, pincer-like burn, the first burn is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
In cauterizing the scrotum.
If it happens in some of the limbs from a fall or a blow, pain and that pain lasts for a long time, and medicine does not work in it, then it should be cauterized with a pointed iron, and the burn of each limb should be according to its size, weakness, and strength.
And it eliminates the pain and scrotum from it. If he recovers from the first burn, otherwise repeat the burn on him, because it is customary for these pains to move from the limb to what is near it, so you should follow them with a burn until the patient recovers.
Chapter forty-seven.
In cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you want to cauterize him, look and see if the leprosy is in its early stages and treat it with what we have mentioned in the division, and it does not subside nor stop, and you fear for the patient that corruption will take over all of his temperament, then cauterize him on the head five times. One in the middle of the head as usual, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two on the temples, and one from behind on the nape of the neck, and apply your hand to the burn a little until it affects the bone slightly.
And scales are removed from it to facilitate the escape of thick vapors, and a cautery is also applied to the spleen itself as previously mentioned. But if leprosy has persisted in the patient and appeared clearly, then these cauteries that we mentioned should be applied to the head. A cautery on the tip of the nose, and two on the cheeks, and two on the hollows of the neck, and six on the hollows of the back, and one large one on the coccyx at the base of the tailbone and another above it in the same area, and two on the thighs, one on each thigh. And two on the knees, one on each knee, and two on the elbows, and two on the shoulders, and a cautery on each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet, and one on each heel of his feet, and on the wrists of his hands.
And preserve the nerve at the back of the heels lest you burn them, and one may cauterize a cautery on the pubic bone, and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver, and know that the more cauteries you apply the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as a healthy person would, because his body is numb, and the cauteries should be of varying sizes according to the limbs and joints as previously mentioned in the characteristics of the irons.
Then treat the cauteries with carob bean powder mixed with honey and all other treatment until he recovers, if God wills.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
Chapter Forty-Eight: On Cautery for Numbness
Then treat it with ointment until it heals, and it may be cauterized for some numbness that occurs in the hand and foot in the back of the body at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, so the numbness goes away.
Let no one undertake this except one who is knowledgeable in the anatomy of organs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
The Forty-Ninth Chapter.
On Cauterization for Leprosy.
If leprosy has become chronic and no remedy of medicine has been effective, cauterize it slightly according to the thickness of the skin so that the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil, plantain leaf, pigeon blood, or the blood of snipesâ€”one part of eachâ€”mix them all together, apply to a cloth, and bind the area until it heals.
The Fiftieth Chapter.
On Cauterization for Cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular iron around it as if drawing a circle. Some sages have mentioned cauterizing severely in its center, but I do not advise this because I expect it to ulcerate; I have witnessed this many times.
Therefore, the correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle as previously mentioned or with numerous cauterizations.
The Fifty-First Chapter.
On Cauterization for Fistulas.
If someone develops a fistula and it is slow to mature, either due to the effectiveness of the cause, or because the patient is old and has little blood, or simply due to time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the fistula, cauterize around it with many small, punctuating cauterizations.
Then leave it alone; it will hasten maturation. If you wish to cauterize the fistula itself, hold a hot iron in this shape.
And lower it into the middle of the abscess until it penetrates the skin, and let the cautery be from what is below so that the pus can flow easily, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
The Fifty-Second Chapter.
On cauterization of ulcers.
An ulcer is a corruption that progresses in the member and eats it away as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, then prepare numerous small and large iron nails as appropriate for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from all sides until the corruption is completely eradicated, leaving nothing at all. Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas so that all the scabs and corruption are removed.
Then treat it with flesh-producing ointments. If, after three days, you see that the flesh is growing healthily without corruption, then well and good. Otherwise, repeat the cautery on any remaining corrupted areas. The ulcer may also be treated with strong medications.
For it acts in place of fire more quickly and effectively. I have already mentioned its treatment with strong medications in the appropriate section, so take it from there when you need it.
The Fifty-Third Chapter.
On Cauterization with Reversed and Unreversed Nails.
This ailment often occurs on the soles of the feet, and it is something rough that causes pain to a man. Cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with fire or with strong water.
As for cauterization with fire, heat a hollow iron tool resembling an eagle's feather, made to encircle the nail on all sides and have a thin edge. Then lower it onto the nail while hot and turn your hand with the tool around the nail until the tool reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days to ripen the pus, then dress it with wild barley boiled in salt and leave the dressing on it for a night, for it will detach from its roots. Then treat the wound site with flesh-producing ointment until it heals, if the pins are not reversed.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, so you should take a tube of copper or iron or an eagle's feather and lower it over the pin or wart, then pour a small amount of sharp water into the tube.
Hold your hand while turning it slightly with a pinch to make the edge of the tube affect the base of the pin, so that the sharp water finds its way down to the base of the pin and the patient endures the sting of the sharp water for an hour.
Then you leave it, for the pin will detach from its roots. You do this one by one until you have removed all of them from the body, then treat the sites after they are detached with flesh-producing ointments.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On Cautery.
If someone suffers a cautery burn from cold in a nerve or from fever quartus or other ailment, you should apply four or five cauteries on the vertebrae, placing one cautery between each vertebra and one on his chest. And apply a cautery to his stomach with an olive-wood iron, for cautery soothes and accelerates the maturation of the cold illness.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
On Cautery for a Wound in the Body.
Ugly boils may erupt in the body, caused by thick, spoiled cold matter. Upon their first appearance, a gentle cautery should be applied to the head of each boil using a slender as wood stick with one end ignited, or with long asparagus root, or with a lentil-shaped iron.
And the tumors may be cauterized in their first outbreak in this manner, so that it does not increase and the effective agent is dissipated, and the patient recovers from it, but this should only be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On Stopping Bleeding That Occurs When an Artery Is Cut.
Very often, bleeding of blood occurs from an artery that has been severed when a wound is exposed externally or when a tumor is incised or a member is cauterized, and so on, making it difficult to cut.
If this happens to someone, hurry your hand to the mouth of the artery and place your index finger upon it, pressing firmly so that the blood is confined under your finger and nothing comes out; then put several small and large olive-wood pegs in the fire and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large according to the wound and the place where the artery has burst open, and apply the peg to the same vein after quickly removing your finger; hold the peg until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding rushes out when you lift your finger from the mouth of the artery and the peg is extinguished, quickly take another peg from those prepared in the fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn any nerves that may be there, for this would cause the patient further suffering.  Know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be cut, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been amputated. For if it is amputated, both ends will contract and the bleeding will stop; or it may be tied tightly with thread, or medications that staunch bleeding may be applied and bound firmly with bandages.
As for someone who attempts to cut it with a ligature or by pulling with cloth or applying burning objects and the like, it will not be severed at all except in rare cases. If this happens to someone and neither a physician nor medicine is available, let him hasten to place the index finger on the mouth of the wound itself as we have described.
Press it very hard so that the blood is confined and flows from above the wound and onto the artery and finger, which should not be removed with very cold water at all times until the blood freezes and thickens and stops. During this time, look to see what it needs in terms of cautery or medicine.
Chapter Two.
On Incisions, Cauterization, Phlebotomy, and Surgeries and the Like.
As we mentioned in the first chapter, every disease that can be treated with cauterization by fire and burning medication, its reasons and causes, instruments and images of cautery tools have been made chapters from beginning to end.
And I will follow the same path in this chapter to make it easy for the student to attain what he desires...
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more intricacies than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, caution should be even greater in this chapter because the work here often involves vomiting blood, which sustains life, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor or cauterizing an abscess or treating a surgical wound or removing an arrow or cutting for a stone and so forth. All of these carry danger and fear, and most often result in death.
I advise you against venturing into anything doubtful, for in this profession you will encounter various types of people with different ailments. Some may be weary of their illness and find death appealing.
Due to the severity of their suffering and the length of their affliction, they may show signs indicative of death. Others may offer you their wealth, providing for your needs in hopes of recovery, but their illness is a fighter. You should not help those who come to you with this disposition at all.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not undertake anything of this until you have certain knowledge that what results will be a happy outcome. And employ in all the treatment of your illnesses the advance of knowledge and warning against what leads to ruin.
For in that you have help in gaining praise, glory, remembrance, and gratitude. May Allah inspire you, O sons, with right guidance and not deprive you of truth and success, for that is in His hand. There is no god but He.
And I have arranged this chapter into sections, building upon what was presented in the chapter on cautery from antiquity to the present, so that it may lighten for you your pursuit of whatever you desire, God willing.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of water that gathers in the heads of infants.
This ailment often occurs in infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also arise from a hidden cause that is unknown.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone but infants, and everyone I have seen with it quickly succumbed to death, so I decided to refrain from treating it. I once saw an infant whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the infant could no longer sit upright because of the size of its head, and the moisture increased until it died.
This moisture either gathers between the skin and bone, or it gathers under the bone on the peritoneum. The procedure for this depends on whether the moisture is between the skin and bone, and if the swelling is small; then one should make a single incision across the middle of the head, about two inches long so that the moisture can drain. This is a depiction of the scalpel.
But if there is more moisture and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions like this:
If the moisture is under the bone and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side and the water descends if you press it with your hand into the inside and this is not hidden from you, then you should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
And after the incision, all the moisture will come out, then tighten the incisions with rags and bandages, then dress it from above with syrup and oil until the fifth day, then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with twisting and ointments, and do not leave the head tight, but feed the patient with all dry food that is low in moisture.
Until the organ becomes strong and heals, another description of the incision is to look where the bone of the swelling appears and the gathering of water, because it may be more in the back of the head or in the front or on the right or on the left.
So aim the incision where the swelling and filling of water appeared for you, and cut it as much as you can, while being careful not to cut an artery so that bleeding occurs and the patient dies from that bleeding along with vomiting moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the arteries behind the ears, known as the temporals.
When someone suffers from a sudden cold in the eyes or chest and this persists, and medication does not work for it, then the treatment for that is to cut these two arteries.
So if you want to cut them, shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the place with a coarse rag so that the artery appears, then tighten the patient's neck with their own garment, then look where the pulse beats and their locations are the two lower places behind the ears; they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, and cut them with a dissecting knife down to the bone; this should be done across the head. If you wish, you can insert the knife under the artery and pull it upwards while cutting; the length of the incision should be about two joined fingers.
If the vein is severed, blood will spurt forth in a pulsating manner, advancing and receding continuously. If the artery does not appear to touch, one should measure three finger-breadths from the earlobe and then mark with ink, cutting down to the bone.
