Send six cups of blood from the median vein, perhaps more or less, all depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound; if any peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it away.
This is done to prevent a hot tumor from forming, then bind the wound with a linen wick and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Cutting the Arteries in the Fingers.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute illnesses brought on by humid heat and muscular soreness in the fingers, or severe persistent headache, and is treated with various medical remedies without success, we have found that cutting or cauterizing the arteries in the fingers, as we have described, can be effective.
The procedure for cutting the arteries involves shaving the hair from the fingers and locating the prominent artery in the fingertip; its pulse will be apparent, and it is rarely hidden except in a few individuals.
If the artery is concealed due to extreme cold, have the patient warm their neck by pulling up their collar, then rub the area with a cloth or apply a hot compress until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel depicted in this image. Carefully incise the skin with the scalpel until you reach the artery. Then insert a snare and draw it upward to expose the artery from its sheath, freeing it from all surrounding connective tissue. If the artery is thin, twist it with the tip of the snare. Then a portion is cut from it, according to the distance between its ends, so that it constricts and does not bleed. For if it is not amputated or severed, the blood will not rise at all; then six to three ounces of blood are drawn.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread, the thread being either silk or oud strings so that gangrene does not hasten to it before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then what is between the ligatures is cut, doing this at that hour or later. If you wish, you may cauterize it close to the bone with a cautery iron until its ends are severed, which will perform this action just as well or better, as we have said.
Unless the patient has a fever or is melancholic, because cauterization helps destroy moisture and so is more beneficial. After ligating the arteries, the area should be stuffed with soft cotton and tight bandages applied. After healing, it should be treated with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and bandaged until it recovers, God willing.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your work, immediately cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the area with pitch and pressing your hand until the blood stops. If you have none of these, place your finger there until the blood coagulates.
Wash the area with very cold water until the pain subsides and bandage it properly. Something lighter and easier than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this two-bladed iron after marking the locations with ink.
Lower it fiercely until it reaches the bone, so that the vein is severed in two places for its ends to separate; it will not join together at all with this cauterization. This is an image of the iron.
The knives should be sharp like small tweezers, but much less sharp than a knife, for if they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would quickly reach them and the flesh would not cut easily. If there is some thickness in them, it will hold the heat of the fire.
And it cuts the flesh quickly, and this action is better than all actions, lighter and easier, and let the distance between the knives be the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of continuous hot tears flowing to the eyes.
If the tears are continuous and flow from the veins on the head outside, and he does not benefit from any medical treatment with medicine, and you see that the face of the patient has reddened. And he feels a tingling in his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are shaken and wet, their lids have become swollen from the severity of the tears, so those who came before us in treating it resorted to this procedure, which is called treatment with the sword, which is that you order the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions on the forehead along its length, each incision being about two fingers long. The first incision is parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a short distance from the movement of the muscle that is in the temple, and the third on the other side.
Be careful not to cut the arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws. The distance between each incision should be about three closed fingers. Have prepared pieces of sponge or a lot of absorbent cloth ready to dry the blood.
Then insert the sharp, double-ended probe, pictured here. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and scrape off all the skin between them with the periosteum on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this probe. And one also enters into the first incision a tool called a skin knife, sharp on one side and smooth and not sharp on the other, and this is its image:
Its sharp side is turned upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin and its smooth side towards the bone, and it is pushed until it reaches the middle incision, cutting through all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the outer surface of the skin.
Then you do the same in the middle incision to the other, and after a moderate amount of blood has flowed, press the places where the solidified blood has formed. Then place a wick of cotton wool soaked in a mixture of wine vinegar and oil or vinegar and oil in each incision.
This is done so that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, the ligature is removed and much irrigation with lukewarm water is used. Then it is treated with basilicum ointment mixed with rose oil and all other remedies for wounds until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from within the head.
When one suffers from frequent, sharp, continuous discharges and sees the eyes as small and shaking, vision has weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated with eyelashes falling out. There is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges come from many deep vessels, and the best treatment for this is as follows: Order the patient to shave his forehead and then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
There is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges come from many deep vessels, and the best treatment for this is as follows: Order the patient to shave his forehead and then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly across and begin the incision from the left temple to the right, reaching down to the bone, keeping your hand away from the temporal muscles that move during chewing. Continue until the bone is exposed and all the blood has been absorbed with a sponge.
Separate the edges of the incision with lint or a flax twist, then tie over it with ligatures and soak the ligatures in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not occur. When you release them and see that the hot abscess has broken, you should rub the bone until flesh begins to grow there.
Then treat it with a drying remedy that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of tragacanth, and making an ointment from them to be used in the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the Treatment of What Falls into the Ear.
Anything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone or something like stone, such as iron and glass; or a vegetable seed, like chickpeas, kernels, and the like; or something liquid, like water, vinegar, and the like.
Or an animal. When a pebble or something like a pebble falls into the ear, and it is not too large for the ear, hold the ear up to the sun; if you see the pebble, put a drop of violet oil or ceruse in it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a quill. Plug the nostrils when you sneeze, after putting a collar of cloth or wool around the ear and pulling the ear upwards; it often comes out with this treatment. If it doesn't, then try to remove it with a fine probe, which has this shape.
If it comes out with the probe, or else try to remove it with a fine, flexible blind snare. If it doesn't come out that way, make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal carefully.
Seal around the tube with softened pitch so that the wind has no other path but the tube, then draw it up with a strong suction; it often comes out this way. If it does not come out as we have described, take a little mastic from gum arabic or hunter's gum used to catch birds and place it on the end of a probe.
After it is firmly wrapped in cotton, it is gently introduced into the ear canal after the ear has been dried of moisture. If it does not all come out as we have described, proceed to incision before hot swelling or rigidity occurs.
The incision is described as follows: you first make a cut on the patient's scalp and let out blood according to his strength. Then you hold the patient between your hands, turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its cartilage in the lowest part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until it reaches the stone, which is then removed as best you can with instruments. Then the incision is quickly sewn up and treated until it heals.
If the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the grains that swell and become inflamed, try to remove it as we have described. If this does not work, take a fine, delicate spoon like this.
Try to cut this type of grain that has fallen into the ear with it, but only do so if you are sure that the grain has become moistened by the ear's vapor until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a syringe, a delicate hook, or suction.
As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove. As for the water that has entered the ear, the patient should first sneeze with a candlewick inserted into his nostril after filling his ear with some loose cotton while lying on that ear containing the water; if it comes out this way...
Otherwise, take many smooth, flat pebbles the length of your fingers and warm them slightly over fire. The patient inserts one of them into the ear canal and rests on one leg from that side, striking the pebble in his ear with another stone.
He continues to do this pebble by pebble until all the water comes out. The water may also come out if one takes a reed or feather and inserts one end into the ear, while burning the other end over fire until most of it is charred; then repeat with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as previously mentioned in the case of gravel. As for removing the animal inside, see if its body is small like a flea or similar. If so, treat it as mentioned in the division.
If its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try to remove it with forceps and probes; this is easier than anything that grows in the ear. As for removing worms generated in the ear, if you have treated them as mentioned in the division in the article on catarrhs and your treatment has not been successful.
You should look into the ear towards the sun, if something of the worm appears to you, remove it with forceps or fine probes. If nothing appears to you from them, take a tube shaped like this.
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, insert the slender end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly. Do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not come out for you, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you in the case of gravel.
If they do not come out as mentioned, use the catarrhs that I have mentioned and which those who came before have tried to kill worms. You will find this in the article on catarrhs. Pouring your ointments and medicines into the ear with this instrument is shown in this diagram:
As you see, it is made of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you wish, the cannon in the inside of the tube may be of solid brass. If you wish, you can take a syringe and wind cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the ointment or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the syringe with cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the ointment is driven into the auditory canal and he feels it.
Let the medicine be poured into the ear, and let it be warmed a little in the fire. Be careful that what is poured into it is not too cold or too hot, for the ear cannot bear it.
Chapter Seven.
In the Treatment of Deafness.
Some children may be born from their mothers with their ear canals not pierced.
It may also happen that some people have a blockage in the ear canal from a wound or growing flesh. This blockage may be deep in the ear canal, invisible to the eye.
It may also protrude slightly from the opening and be visible. The blockage that is deep in the ear and invisible is usually difficult to cure, while that which is visible should be treated by placing the patient's ear in the sun. Look into it and if you see the blockage, open it with a delicate speculum shaped like this: .
Its tip should be slightly widened, while the rest of the speculum should be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a fine snare and cut it carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep in the ear, take a fine, smooth probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage and the patient experiences hearing difficulty, gently press it.
Be careful not to injure the nerve of the ear, and then place a wick in the ear according to the width of the opening, soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure that the wound has healed or take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered glass on it.
Use it. If you see that the ear has become swollen and hot, remove the wick and replace it with another wick soaked in a liniment made with rose oil to soothe the swelling. Then treat it until it is healed.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use all the treatment that cuts off the mentioned bleeding as stated in the article on catarrhs.
Chapter Eight.
In the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry, so they should be held with forceps or a snare and cut at their base with a scalpel, then powdered alum should be applied to the area.
If blood bleeds from it, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-sized iron after cutting, for it is best to cauterize after cutting, and it often returns if any of its roots remain. And if it is cauterized with fire, those roots will be burned and will not return.
Chapter Nine.
In the treatment of ophthalmia that occurs on the eyelids.
It often appears on the eyelids something resembling a cold in its severity and stiffness.
And for this reason, it is called ophthalmia, which is the gathering of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid, and the procedure for it is to look: if the ophthalmia is protruding on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, then its matter is easy, so make a transverse incision on it.
Then gently peel it off from all sides until you get rid of it, then hang it with a snare and cut it. If you are not able to cut it except after penetrating the eyelid by cutting, that will not harm the patient at all.  If the incision is large, bring it together with stitches and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm in that, for the ointment will hold it together and cauterize it. If the ophthalmia is slanted inward toward the inside surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid over and hang the ophthalmia with a snare without needing to make an incision, and cut it from all sides.
If the eyelid is cut, it will not harm anything; then wash the eye after cutting the bandage with salt water and treat the area with what heals so that the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of inflammation which occurs in the eyelid.
Inflammation is a redness that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid and it most commonly affects boys; it makes their eyes heavy and they are prone to colds at night and they cannot look at sunlight. So that tears flow quickly to them.
That is why they are always leaning on their faces and sleep on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are wet, with swelling above them, and the inflammation is clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the inflammation between them. The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare from it a wick, and make a circle from that wick to the extent that it surrounds the inflammation on all sides. Then place it on top of it and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the inflammation in the middle of the circle.
Then make an incision in the middle of that moisture with a scalpel, making a horizontal cut; the cut should not be larger than the one made during bloodletting. As for depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the inflammation.
In most cases, the inflammation will protrude from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat; grasp it with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers and pull it outward while turning your hand right and left until it is gone. Be careful not to increase the incision lest the scalpel reach the eye and injure it.
If the inflammation does not appear in the first incision, you should slightly enlarge the incision carefully until it protrudes, then pull it out as we said; then soak a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area, and secure it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
And place it in the incision so that the remaining moisture dissolves, then treat it until it is healed. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with cooling poultices until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On Types of Eye Stitching.
If hairs grow excessively on the eyelid, beneath the natural eyelashes, they harm the eye and cause various illnesses such as continuous tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, opacity, and thickening until it results in blindness.
Eye stitching is done in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or with a caustic drug as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or by cutting and stitching, or with reeds as I have already described.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn back the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns back easily, leave it as such; otherwise, insert a needle with thread from underneath the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread through from above. This should be done near the hair itself, and the thread should be drawn upwards along the eyelid.
Turn it back with a spatula and then make an incision inside the eyelid, away from the excess hairs, with a scalpel - starting from the larger forceps to the smaller ones. Then pass the thread under the eyelid and place a small dressing of cotton or cloth beneath it. Finally, apply ointment to the eyelid.
The shape should be like an ace of spades leaf, but it should be sized according to how much you want to lift the eyelid because people differ in this regard; some may need a portion of the eyelid cut away proportionate to how much it has relaxed.
Some may need less cutting, all depending on the degree of eyelid relaxation. Then make incisions with a scalpel along the lines you have marked, starting from the larger forceps to the smaller ones. Each incision should be close to the natural eyelashes and about the width of a spatula.
Then the thread is inserted into one corner of the skin and it is peeled off completely. Then the two lips are joined with a needle and fine wool thread, the blood is wiped away and the remaining threads are glued to the eyebrows with some sticky substances.
If you wish to do so, or if not, it does not matter. Then the stitches and threads remain for about three or four days, then it is treated. If you wish, you may leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and tightens. For when the wound closes and heals, the eyelid rises. Stitching is preferable.
This method of cauterization was mentioned by those who came before, but it has drawbacks for the patient. It is a good practice and not dangerous. Another method of cauterization also exists, which is to learn on the eyelid a shape like that of an as leaf as we have described. Then the eyelid is raised with three forceps, either separated or grouped in this pattern:
Then what remains of the eyelid is cut with a small scissors in this pattern, cutting moderately:
If you cannot hold the forceps and they do not stay in place for you, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger opening and a third thread near the smaller opening. Gather the threads between your fingers moderately, then lift them up moderately with your hand.
Lift the entire skin marked as we described, then bring the two lips of the wound together with stitches and treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling appears when you cut or make an incision, soothe that swelling with cerates and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also turn inwards, and the same cutting, stitching, and cauterization with fire or caustic drugs should be used. Cauterization with fire or a caustic drug is more effective and less painful for the patient than cutting and stitching. Cutting and stitching are preferable in both cases.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this manner: Turn the eyelid over and make an incision on the inner side as described above. Then fashion two reeds or thin pieces of wood, their length equal to that of the eyelid and their width less than that of a lancet.
Cut the ends of both sides where the threads will grip, then carefully bring them together over the remaining portion of the eyelid and tighten the reeds on both sides securely. Leave them for several days; the stretched skin will die, turn black, and rot.
It will fall off on its own; if it is delayed, cut it with pincers and then treat it until it heals. When the wound has healed, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes of the eye will not droop. This is the image of the reeds, they are made in this shape and size in length and width exactly:
Chapter Twelve.
On removing ingrown eyelashes with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is as follows: Take a fine needle and thread through it a fine, smooth silk thread; then gather both ends and tie them in a very delicate knot, making the length of the thread about a span long.
Then attach another, thinner thread to the needle holder, shorter in length, and tie its ends if you wish. Have the patient place their head in your lap, preferably near sunlight so that the work can be clearly seen; for this is delicate work. Insert the needle with the thread into the base of the ingrown hair.
Pass it quickly through the eyelid until it emerges above the normal hair, then pull the thread up toward the needle holder and insert into the needle holder that ingrown hair; if there is only one or two or at most three.
Then draw your hand with it until the hair comes out with the needle holder in the eyelid along with the normal hair. If you pull the needle holder and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle holder downwards with the thread that you attached to it until the needle holder comes out of the upper hole.
Then return the hair to it and draw them, do that until it rises in the upper hole and you see it with your eye. Then loosen the forceps and thread and pull them out, tighten the eyelid and leave it tightened for two or three days until the hair is fixed in its place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows after a while, then lift it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the chalazion that occurs in the upper eyelid.
The chalazion that the eyelid is exposed to is called an arnebia, and this chalazion can be either natural or accidental. An accidental chalazion results from the inflammation of a wound, scratch, or burn, and so on.
The procedure for treating it involves incising the inflammation, separating its edges, and placing a wick of linen between them, which is then tied until it heals.  Things that dry and constrict should not be used in its treatment.
For if you do that, the chalazion will return worse than before. Instead, use things that soften it, such as fenugreek and rinsing with water in which licorice root and flaxseed have been boiled, and a diachylon plaster dissolved with some ointment applied to the lesion and treated with it.
The aim of its treatment is to try every means to prevent it from healing in the shape it originally had. This procedure we mentioned is only for partially correcting the chalazion, not for returning the area to its original shape.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of a chalazion that occurs in the lower eyelid.
This chalazion that occurs in the lower eyelid is actually called a stye and can be either natural or accidental. An accidental stye results from a wound, scratch, burn, or something similar.
The way to work with it is to take a needle with a doubled thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it through from the left armpit to the right armpit until the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then, stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide spatula.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is appropriate, otherwise, take a retractor and place it on the incision site, turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, making the ends of the incisions from the corners of the cut that was made so that they meet and form an angle. Until when they come together, its shape will resemble this shape:
It is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is below what follows the eyelid, then bring together the scattered parts by two sutures sewn with wool thread, then treat it with the softening and dissolving drugs we mentioned until it heals.
And if the tear appeared from a cut, suture, or cautery, it should be slit simply without the hair of the eyelashes as previously mentioned, then separate between the lips with a twist as we mentioned.
In conclusion, in treating a tear whether it is from above or below, the work should be carried out according to what seems suitable to you from the shape of the tear, as it may vary greatly in form, and the skilled craftsman manages the trick in any way he can.
So that the shape returns to its natural form or approaches it, and the same disease guides you to what is suitable for it from work and tools in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On Adhesion of the Eyelid to the Conjunctiva or Cornea.
This adhesion may be presented to many people, where the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from moving easily and hindering it from its natural functions. So you should insert the end of a retractor under the eyelid.
Raise it up or stretch it with a string, then cut the adhesion with a fine knife, not as sharp as other knives, but rather blunt so as not to alarm the patient while working, lest the knife slip and injure the eye with its sharpness. Cut it as if you were peeling away that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour salt water into the eye to wash it, or drip a solution of zangar ginger in water into it. Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
Place a bandage soaked in egg white over the eye, and after the third day use softened wicks until it is healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Cutting the Veil and Removing Excess Flesh of the Eyelids.
The veil comes in two types: either it is nervous, resembling a thin, hard membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a solid white moisture which breaks apart when touched with iron or pulled by a string.
No snare can be attached to it, and both types begin at the larger eyelid and gradually extend until they cover the viewer and block light and eye movement. The procedure for cutting them is to have the patient rest their head on your lap.
Then, open their eyes and lift the eyelid with your hand. Catch the veil with a slightly curved snare and stretch it upwards. Then take a needle, thread a horsehair or cowhair into it along with a strong thread, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and insert it into the middle of the veil.
Pass the needle through and tie the veil with the thread, then stretch it upwards. Peel away the side of the veil next to the pupil using the hair as if you were spreading it to its end, then cut off the remainder at the base of the eyelid with a fine knife or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the eyelid.
So that there may not occur from the piece a continuous flow of tears, and the eyelid may be stretched by the needle alone or by thread as we have said; then the end is pared with a smooth scalpel, such as this:
Care should be taken that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane, lest it make a hole and the pupil is injured; then after the cutting, some powdered salt or zinzabar powder is put into the eye and left until the next day, and then it is treated as appropriate until it heals.
But if the eyelid is not nervous and it is impossible to put a needle into it or fix a ligature in it, this kind of eyelid is often treated with medicines until it heals as described in the division; but if you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate smooth scalpel weak at the edge like this: And pare the eyelid with it from above, gently; if you see that it loosens and crumbles and the paring has only affected it, put a little zinzabar powder or red powder or powdered salt into the eye from your hour-glass and bind the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the work until it is all gone, except if you encounter a hot swelling in the eye, leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals, then repeat the paring work on it until it is healed; as for the fleshy growth...
If the growth causes severe pain to the eye, suspend that growth with a ligature and cut off some of it; do not overdo the cutting lest there be a continuous flow of tears; then put red powder or zinzabar powder into the eyelids until it heals.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On Cutting the Warty Growths and Excess Flesh in the Eye.
Sometimes a red fleshy growth arises in the eyes of some people, piling up until it covers the seer or comes close to or overflows onto the eyelids and perhaps the eyelids turn outward and resemble a carnation.
The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap, then open his eyes and grasp all of that red flesh with suitable forceps.
Or hold it with a forceps or tweezers, and then cut the flesh one by one until it is all gone, being careful not to injure the eye while working. Your cuts should be made either with a scalpel as described in cutting for circumcision or with small scissors. Like those used to remove splinters, as its image will be shown in the chapter following this one.
Once you have finished cutting and all the fleshy growth is gone, fill the eye with pounded salt or drop into it red lead or a similar caustic medication, and apply cotton soaked in egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent hot swelling.
If some of the fleshy growth remains, and bleeding overcomes you and you fear hot swelling, leave the eye alone and treat it with what soothes swelling, then return to work on it until it is healed. Do the same for excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye, either by nature or by appearance, with iron instruments and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that the organ can tolerate it.
Chapter Eighteen.
On Removing Splinters from the Eye.
Splinters are red veins that weave across the eye, preventing vision and weakening it over time.
Then comes the ligature, which is to order the patient to put his head in your lap and then to hang those veins with one or two ligatures according to your skill. The ligatures should be fine and flexible in this manner:
Or they may be double ligatures in one body in this manner:
Then, with fine scissors, carefully cut those veins and wipe away the blood from time to time until you see that the eye is freed from those veins and the blood has clotted. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors and work in the morning facing the sun.
Be very steady in your work so as not to cut anything other than those veins; when you are finished, cauterize the eye with green or red vitriol so that its sharpness consumes what remains of the vessels. If you cannot ligate it all at that time, bandage the eye with something that soothes its pain.
Leave it for several days until its pain subsides and the hot swelling is gone; then resume work on it in the same manner until this image of scissors disappears from it.
Leave it [with the patient's head in the lap of a student, another drying his tears and observing the vision at both the front and back of the eye, and you] the principal thing in this work is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openers which a student holds.
Look intently, with the patient's head in a student's lap and another drying his tears and observing the vision at both the front and back of the eye; you have the option of starting the ligature from the top or bottom of the eye. The first method involves tying two ligatures on the upper side: one at the end of the crescent and the other at its beginning.
Hold them between the middle finger and index finger, then hang ligatures underneath opposite them; hold these with your ring finger and little finger and lightly remove your hand until you see that all the vessels have risen, cut them with the smallest scissors if you wish or with a fine probe.
And he inserts the needle holder and elevates all the layers until it rises above the muscle, without cutting the nerves, for tears and blood will flow freely upon you.
Then cut with scissors what is in excess of the crown above and below so that there remains nothing protruding except in the cornea; if you are able to lift it from the cornea, remove it with the needle holder, for this is better, and it may be possible in some people.
And if you cut it, it will become scarred for a few days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling is avoided, then resume working on it.
Chapter Nineteenth.
On Returning the Feather to the Nose.
Physicians call the feather a NÃ¢sÃ»ra if they treat it with cautery or a caustic medicine as previously described, and it does not heal; then there is no recourse but to incise the swelling when ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed, and if you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
It is called a rough-headed instrument and is made of Indian iron, with its head rounded like a button, finely engraved with a file or graver; place it on the corrupted part of the bone and turn it between your fingers while slightly compressing your hand until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Repeat this several times, then pack the area with drying astringent medications. If the area closes and flesh grows there, and the flow ceases for forty days and does not recur, nor does any harm or incident occur.
Then know that it has healed; otherwise, there is no recourse but to return the NÃ¢sÃ»ra to the nostril's perforation in the following manner: expose the bone again with iron or a caustic medicine. When the bone is exposed, take a borer of this form:
The point of the iron should be triangular, and its shaft conical, drawn down to a point as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the angle and a little distance from the eye with your hand. Then turn your hand with the handle until the bone is pierced and the patient feels it.
If he finds the wind coming out of it when he puts his hand over his nose, then forcibly compress the area with drying astringents as I have taught you. When the flesh grows and the place hardens, the material that was pressing outward will return to the nose, making it easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Returning a Protruding Eye.
If the eye protrudes entirely and no harm or defect has occurred to the sight, you should ease the patient, then loosen him in the cupping glass, and then apply a leech to the nape of the neck without restriction and suck gently.
Then place a poultice made of acacia, patience herb, frankincense, and mastic on the eye, and bind the eye tightly over the dressing with many bandages. The recipe for the bandages is to take many soft rags and fold each one four times, making many.
Then place them one on top of the other as much as the eye needs, and bind tightly over it with a strong rag, then repeat the leech sucking without restriction, then remove the leech and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, well and good; otherwise, repeat the poultice, bandages, tightening, and leech until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Cutting a Cataract.
If a rupture occurs in the lens layer, then it protrudes and comes out beyond the eyelids like a grape, spoiling the appearance of man; and you wish to cut it, proceed as I describe.
This is done by inserting a needle into the base of the bunion from below upwards, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread into it from the side of the big toe, passing it through and leaving the first needle in place.
Then the doubled thread is cut and tied to some of the bunion around the needle, and it is tightened well. Then the needle is removed, and wool soaked in egg white is placed over the eye and left until the threads fall off with the bunion, then the eye is treated with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound is healed, and the bunion may be tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth so that the eye becomes more prominent, then it is tamped with a fine spatula that reaches the bottom of the eye, causing the egg-white fluid to flow and the eye to descend and become flaccid in place. Then it is tightened until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the Treatment of Cataracts.
This condition, called a cataract, is a turbidity that collects in the eye resembling descending water but is not actually water. The procedure for this involves having the patient sit upright on a chair, then taking their head with your hands on both sides.
You move it until you see the turbidity descend downwards with your eye, then hold it still so that it does not return, and light will be released, and the patient will see things as they used to see them. If the turbidity does not descend, we know it is water. If the turbidity does not descend as described,
then have the patient sit between your hands, take a fine spatula and make a slight incision above the corneal membrane near the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal membrane in the limbus until the material comes out.
Then, if a cataract is extracted from the eye, pour hot water into which honey has been mixed, or water that has been boiled with fenugreek and honey, then treat it with all the usual treatments until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in our division and which are suitable for washing, explained in detail. You should take it from there according to its suitability. Then, you should have the patient sit facing you in a square position, near the sunlight.
You should then tie his sound eye and bind it tightly. Next, raise the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if the water is in the left eye, or with your right hand if the water is in the right eye.
Then place the tip of the hollow instrument near the pupil with the thickness of a millet seed, in the same white part of the eye as the smaller conjunctiva. Push the instrument forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel it has reached something empty.
The depth to which the instrument goes should be the same distance as from the pupil to the last part of the black, which is the pupil of the eye. You will see this in the copper because it reflects like a mirror in the one looking. Then turn the instrument upward to the place where the water is.
Then press it downward repeatedly. If the water descends from its place, the patient will see what has been opened before him with his sight and the instrument in his eye. Then rest a bit. If the water rises, lower it again without taking the instrument out of the eye.
When the water settles and does not rise, carefully remove the instrument while gently twisting it with your hand. Then dissolve a little bit of refined rock salt in some water and wash the eye from the inside. Finally, place a moistened poultice or cloth over the entire eye.
Bind both eyes with a mixture of rose oil and egg white. In our time, we use crushed cumin with egg white. If the instrument does not enter due to the stiffness of the eye, for some people have very stiff eyes... It should be taken the bistoury which is called the briad, which has this form:
Then pierce it with the briad alone, and do not deepen the puncture, but rather lightly touch the spot where the cautery will be applied, then insert the cautery as we have described. When you have finished your work, prepare the patient lying down firmly on his back in a dark room.
He must abstain from all movement and coughing, and his food should be what softens his nature; he should not move his head to either side at all. The bandage should remain as it is until the third day, then you should remove it in that dark room and test his sight.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage until the seventh day. This should not be done at the time of treatment or after cauterization for a while, but rather it should be avoided before water ascends quickly with intense looking.
If hot swelling occurs, the eye should be unwrapped before the seventh day and treated with something that will reduce the swelling until it subsides. Only then should his sight be released and a veil placed over his face, training his vision from underneath for several days while he remains in that dark room.
He should then gradually leave the house and behave as usual. Know that a learner cannot do without observation many times before undertaking this procedure. I have heard from some Iraqis that one of them mentioned making in Iraq a hollow cautery that absorbs water.
I have not seen anyone in our country make such a cautery, nor read about it in any of the books of the ancients. It may be something new. This is an image of different types of cauteries so that he who does not know them can learn:
It is made of copper, specifically, and its tip has this particular shape, triangular with a sharp point.
Chapter Twenty-Four
In the Treatment of Flesh Growing in the Nose.
Various kinds of excess flesh may grow in the nose, some resembling scorpions with many legs, some cancerous and hard like copper, and some soft and not like copper.
If the flesh is soft and not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, open their nostrils, and then a probe should be inserted into the flesh. It should then be pulled out and cut off from one side with a fine, sharp scalpel.
Continue this until you are sure that all the flesh is gone. If any remains that you cannot cut, scrape it off carefully with a fine instrument so that nothing is left. If bleeding occurs or a hot swelling appears,
treat it accordingly. If the tumor is malignant, remove it quickly until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Afterwards, pour broth or vinegar into the nose. If the nose opens and the moisture flows down the throat
you will know that it is healed. If the moisture does not flow properly, know that there is still flesh growing at the top of the loose bones to which the instrument did not reach.  In this case, you should take a thread of fairly thick cotton,
tie many knots in it, spacing each knot the width of a finger or less. The patient should then insert one end of the thread into their nose with a rod or whatever they can use after shaping it like a needle and pull on it until it reaches the cartilage.
The other end will come out of the throat. This is often done by boys in school, and it is an easy thing for anyone who wants to do it. Then gather the end that comes out of the mouth and the end that remains in the nose.
Use the knots in the thread to tear away the flesh, continuing until you are sure all the flesh has been removed. Then remove the thread and after cleaning the blood, insert a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose. Keep it there for three days or more.
Until the ointment eats up all that remains of the flesh, then the last thing in the nose will be a lead tube for several days until it heals; if it needs drying treatment, that is used.
This is an image of the syringe with which ointments and medicines are dripped into the nose.
He makes a small lamp-like object out of silver or copper, open at the filling point and its passage as well.
And if you wish, you make the tube closed like a reed pipe and the syringe's filler flat.
It has a handle at its end as you see, which is held onto when the fat or whatever juices or liquid things are heated inside it.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
On Plantar Warts At The Tip Of The Nose.
Often a wart grows at the tip of the nose, it enlarges and increases with days until its appearance is unsightly.
Therefore it should be cut off at its first appearance, and all of it rooted out, then either cautery or a burning medicine that acts in place of cautery is applied to the spot. If cutting it fails until it enlarges, then look and see if it is hardened, stony-like in color, and with little sensation.
Do not expose it to the iron, for it is a cancerous tumor and I have often seen this tumor cut out, and great misfortune returned upon its owner. If the tumor is soft to the touch and not stony-like in color, and you see that cutting can be done throughout all of it... ...then drain the wart, cut it without hesitation or fear, and treat the spot with something that dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Sixth.
On Sewing the Nose, Lip, and Ear. Know that if the connection is torn from a wound or something similar, it rarely succeeds unless in some people.
If one of these things should occur, examine whether the wound is fresh with blood; then bring the lips of the wound together by suture and treat it until it heals. If the separation has occurred and the two lips have become distinct, each portion should be pared down to the thickness of its apparent skin until it bleeds.
Then bring the lips together by suture, draw them tight, and scatter powdered myrrh and frankincense over them. Place a plaster of palm balm or other similar ointment on top and leave it drawn tight for two or three days, then remove it.
Change the dressing and leave it until the sutures come out on their own, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to bring together the separation, either with needles as described in the suturing of the abdomen or with thread as explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Removal of Nodes Appearing on the Lips.
Many people may present with small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling caraway seeds and others smaller or larger. You should invert the lip and make an incision over each nodule, then suspend it with a ligature.
Cut it from all sides, then pack the site after cutting with powdered borax to stop the bleeding. Gargle with vinegar and salt, and treat the sites with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Removal of Excess Flesh from the Gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, called by ancient practitioners an "apolus". You should suspend it with a ligature or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base. Allow the bleeding to continue for a time, then apply powdered borax to the site.
Or if it is one of the tenacious dried sinews, if that flesh returns after treatment, it often returns, so cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after cautery.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On Extraction of Teeth with Iron.
It may happen that on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums, rough, unsightly scales may collect. These scales may darken and yellow or even green, to such an extent that decay reaches the gums and the teeth become ugly.
The patient should be seated in front of you with his head on your lap. Remove the molar and any tooth where scales or sand-like matter appear, so that nothing remains. Do the same for the blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other such discoloration until it is purified.
If all the discoloration is removed at first, well and good; otherwise, repeat the removal on another day, a second and third time, until you achieve your goal. Know that molars require various differently shaped instruments, depending on what is needed for the task at hand.
This is because the instrument used to remove molars from the inside differs from the one used to remove them from the outside, and that used between molars has yet another shape. These are several types of instruments which you should have prepared:
The Thirtieth Chapter.
On Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat the molar for its pain by every means and delay its extraction as long as possible, for there is no substitute if it is extracted, since it is a noble essence. Even if its extraction becomes necessary...
If the patient insists on having it extracted, you should make sure that the molar's pain has subsided. Often the patient is deceived by pain and believes it to be in the correct tooth, so he has it extracted, but then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is also extracted. We have seen barbers do this many times.
If the toothache is in the tooth itself, then you should cut around the tooth with a strong forceps until the gum loosens from all sides, and then move it with your fingers or fine forceps, little by little, until it is loosened.
Then you can grasp it firmly with the large forceps, and place the head of the patient between your knees so that it does not move. Then pull the tooth straight up so as not to break it. If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it underneath from all sides gently, and wiggle it as you did before.
And if the tooth is carious or decayed, then you should fill that hole with a rag and plug it tightly with the end of a fine probe so that it does not crumble when you pull on it with forceps, and you should carefully cut around the gum from all sides.
And be careful not to break it, for if some of it remains, the patient will suffer more than he did initially. Beware of doing what ignorant barbers do in their shops, rushing to extract it without using what we have described.
They often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or some of its roots remain. Sometimes they even remove part of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times. After extracting it, gargle with a decoction or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, which often happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not work. The forceps with which you first move the tooth should have long tips and a short handle, and be thick so as not to bend when you grasp the tooth. This is an image of large forceps:
It should be as you see it, thick-handled so that when grasped it does not give way nor bend, short-armed, and made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel, with the ends squared off. At one end are teeth which interlock so that it grasps firmly and securely.
The ends may also be made like a file, so they are strong for grasping.

Chapter Thirty-Two
On Spreading the Erupted Molars onto Others
If the molars erupt in an unnatural way, they spoil the appearance, especially if this happens to women and delicate people. It should be looked at whether the molar has erupted behind another molar.
And it is impossible to spread it or push it back, so extract it. If it is stuck to another molar, cut it with this instrument:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of very sharp Indian steel, and cut it for many days due to the hardness of the molar, so as not to loosen any other molars. As for if it is erupting and capable of being cooled down, then cool it down with a cooler made of this:
It should be made entirely of Indian steel, with a very finely engraved blade, like the file used to make needles. Cool the molar a little at a time for many days gently so that it does not loosen and fall out, then smooth it last and pare it down a bit with some scrapers.
And if a molar has broken off and it hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled so that the roughness of the break goes away and becomes even and smooth so that it does not hurt the tongue or spoil speech.
Chapter Thirty-Third.
In the interlacing of movable molars with silver threads or with gold threads.
If the front molars become loosened and move about from a blow or a fall, and the patient is unable to chew anything edible lest they fall out, and they have been treated with astringent medications without success.
The remedy is to tighten a gold or silver thread, with gold being preferable because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium fineness and thickness, according to the space between the molars.
The method of interlacing is to take the thread and insert its bend between the sound molars, then weave the ends of the thread between the movable molars, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound molar on the other side.
Then weave back to the side where you started and tighten your hand gently and skillfully so that the molars do not move at all. The thread should be tightened at the roots of the molars so that it does not come loose. Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them with a forceps, and hide them between the sound molar and the movable molar.
So that it does not injure the tongue, and leave it tightened as long as necessary. If it becomes loose or breaks, tighten it with another thread, and thus enjoy it for life. This is an image of the molars and the manner of interlacing in two sound molars and two movable molars as you see:
It may happen that one or two molars return to their place after falling out and are interlaced as described and remain in place. This should only be done by a skilled craftsman, who may also carve bone from a cow's bone to make it into the shape of a molar and place it in the space where the molar has gone. And it is tightened as we said, and it remains and he enjoys it.
Chapter the Thirty-Fourth.
In cutting the frenum that appears under the tongue, it prevents speech.
This frenum that appears under the tongue may be natural, with which a person is born, or it may be incidental from a wound that has healed.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, place his head in your lap, and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous frenum transversely so that the tongue is released from its hold; if there is some stiffness and complexity and this is due to wound healing, insert a probe into it and split it transversely until the frenum is severed and the knots are untied.
Beware that the incision is not deep into the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and cause bleeding; then gargle after the cut with rose water or vinegar and cold water, and then place a linen wick under the tongue which the patient holds in every night.
So that it does not adhere again, if bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the spot, and if blood overwhelms you, cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped iron suitable for that purpose. Then treat it with all other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter the Thirty-Fifth.
On removing the frog that is generated under the tongue.
It may happen that a tumor resembling a small frog appears under the tongue, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function and perhaps growing until it fills the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth opposite the sun and look at the tumor.
If you see it dark red or black, hard and the patient does not feel it, do not interfere with it because it is cancerous; but if it leans towards white and has moisture in it, insert a probe into it and split it with a fine spatula and free it from all sides.
If blood overwhelms you while you are working, place powdered borax upon it until the bleeding stops; then return to your work until you extract it completely; and then gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all suitable remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the Treatment of Tonsillitis.
And whatever grows in the throat of all tumors may present within the throat glands resembling those presented externally, called tonsils; if you treat them with what I mentioned in the division and they do not heal.
Then examine whether the tumor is hard, dark in color and slightly sensitive; do not expose it to the iron. And if it is red in color and its origin is thick, do not expose it to the iron either for fear of bleeding; but leave it until it matures, then either cauterize it or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white, round and its origin is thin, this is what should be cut; the work is to look before working whether its hot swelling has subsided completely or decreased somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun with his head in your lap; open his mouth and take a servant between your hands to press his tongue down with this instrument:
Made of silver or brass, it should be thin like a knife; when you press the tongue with it and the tumor is revealed to your sight, take a snare and insert it into the tonsil, drawing it out as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape: resembling scissors, except that its two ends are curved; the edge of each facing the other is very sharp. It is made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If you do not have this instrument, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
A sharp instrument on one side and blunt on the other; after cutting one tonsil, cut the other with the same type of cut. Then, after the cut, the patient should gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, he should gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds or bay leaves or something similar from astringents have been steeped until the bleeding stops; then treat him until he is healed. Growths other than the tonsils may develop in the throat and they should be cut as I described for cutting the tonsils alike.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, extending towards the voice box with little sensation; it was almost blocking the throat and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage and prevented her from eating or drinking water.
She was close to death if she had remained for a day or two, and the tumor had grown two branches that protruded from her nostrils. I acted quickly and inserted a probe into one of them and pulled it; thus, a suitable piece was extracted.
I then cut it where I could reach it from the nostril and did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then, I opened her mouth, pressed down on her tongue and inserted a probe into the tumor itself and cut some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat immediately opened and she began to drink water; then she was able to eat. We continued to cut from the tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor kept replacing what was cut until it became tiresome for both of us.
I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside her throat, so it stopped growing. Then I left that region and did not know what God had done with her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven
On the Excision of a Goiter.
That which is called a swelling if it descends to the eyelids and swells, and is white and rectangular, it is called a column; and if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a swelling. If it is treated with what we have mentioned in the division and the treatment is unsuccessful.
And I saw the hot tumor subside from it and it was thin, so it should be cut; and what was gathered together from it, round and without length, and was bloody or dark-colored or black, or without feeling, should be avoided from being cut.
There is danger for the patient in this, so if you see it on the characteristic I mentioned of its whiteness and length, the patient should sit facing the sun and press his tongue with the instrument whose description has been given before; then the snare should be inserted into the swelling.
And it should be drawn down and cut with one of the two instruments that I mentioned for cutting the tonsils; and you should not cut from it more than what exceeds the natural state, because if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the cut, you should use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals; if the patient refuses to have it cut, then you should use a trick to cauterize it without fear or caution, and the method of cautery in this case is with a caustic drug.
Then you press his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned, then you take some of the caustic water that we mentioned in the chapter on cautery and knead it into a paste that is neither too thick nor too thin, and fill the cavity of this instrument. This is its shape:
The end of it where you put the medicine should have a cavity like that of a pomegranate spoon; and you place the instrument with the medicine on the eyelid itself, the patient lying on his side so that saliva flows from inside his throat, so that nothing descends from it into his throat and harms him.
Then grasp your hand on the medicine while you press it onto the wound for half an hour until you see it has blackened and the sting of the medicine subsides. If you wish, you may take some cotton wool and wrap it around the tip of a rod and soak the cotton wool in the medicine.
Insert the rod with the cotton into a tube from above so that the cotton adheres to the wound. Repeat this several times until you reach the desired degree of cauterization, then leave it. It will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, prepare it again.
After cauterization, wipe around the cautery with cotton soaked in butter and dry the medicine around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The wound may also be treated with something milder than cauterization and incision, treating it with fumigation in this manner: Take fennel, rue, savory, sage, wormwood, chamomile, and quinces or similar herbs, gather them all or some of them in a pot and submerge them in vinegar.
Boil the pot, which is coated with clay of wisdom, and make a hole in the middle of the lid.  Place the hollow instrument on top as follows: it is made of silver or brass.
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the wound through the tube until the wound becomes softened, then repeat this several times until it dries up. Beware of performing this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling, but it should only be done when the hot swelling has subsided. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell at one end so as not to burn the patient's mouth.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, this is a good treatment and it is safe.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On extracting thorns and other things that become lodged in the throat.
Often a bone, fishbone, or something similar becomes lodged in the throat, so what appears and is seen should be removed after pressing down the tongue with an instrument at the sunshine.
So that you can see what is in the throat, and what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before their food is digested in their stomach. Perhaps the lodged object will come out with vomiting or the patient may swallow a piece of lettuce or a radish root.
Or they may swallow a piece of stale bread, or you can take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it with string, and then the patient swallows it. When it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly, doing this several times.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out as described above, otherwise use an instrument made of lead in this form:
It should be slightly thicker than a pestle and at its end, there is a hook that the patient inserts into their throat gently while raising their head upwards. They must avoid touching their Adam's apple so as not to cause coughing which could push the bone or thorn further in, or the doctor may insert it with their hand.
Having the patient insert it is best as they know the location of the lodged object and push it down or pull their hand up with the instrument, all according to what is possible until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On extracting leeches that become lodged in the throat.
If you have treated the leech as mentioned in the section on drug treatment and it has not been successful, then look into the patient's throat at sun after pressing down their tongue with the instrument I have described for you.
If your sight falls upon the foreign body, draw it out with a small snare or a fine, tight forceps; if you are unable to do so, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the throat of the patient as far as the foreign body, then insert into the inside of the tube an iron instrument protected by fire.
Do this several times, and let the patient abstain from water all day; then he should take a mouthful of cold water, open his mouth into it, and gargle without swallowing a drop, moving the water from time to time with his hand.
For if the foreign body feels the water, it will fall into its place; but if it does not come out as we have described, cauterize the throat with urine and with a solution of saltpeter with the instrument which was described in the chapter on fumigation for laryngitis. Do this several times, for it will then fall out.
The method of working with the fumigation is to take a vessel containing live coals heated by fire; the vessel should be covered with a lid in which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument should be fitted into this hole. Then fumigate, and let the patient put his mouth on the end of the tube and close it so that the smoke does not escape until he knows that the smoke has reached his throat.
For the foreign body will then fall into its place; but if it does not, repeat the fumigation several times, and let the patient abstain from drink and eat salt and garlic, without drinking water; for it is certain to come out with this treatment. This is a drawing of an instrument with which to draw out a foreign body from the throat when it has been seen.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this extension which enters the throat; and its ends resemble the beak of a bird, with a roughness like a file so that when it grips anything it does not let go.
There is another instrument with which to press down the tongue; it is a broad iron ring of a size to fit the mouth above the tongue. It is introduced into the mouth standing on its side so that the mouth remains open against the will of the patient and he cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
