Chapter forty.
It discusses speech about tumors and their incision.
Tumors are of many varieties, delicately distinguished as will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their incision and treatment depending on two factors: the type of tumor itself and its contents.
The second factor is the location in the body where they occur, for a tumor in the head is not treated like a tumor in the buttocks, nor is a tumor in a fleshy area treated like one in a joint; each has its own rules of treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus within them has matured and become complete, while others should be incised even when they are still immature, such as those located near joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and is allowed to fester, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, leading to lameness in the limb; or the tumor may be located near a major organ.
Therefore, if you delay incising it until it matures, you may harm that major organ; or it may be located near the buttocks and should be incised while still immature.  A tumor near the buttocks must be incised while still immature so that it does not fester and spread into the buttock, becoming a danger.
And in some cases, it may not heal at all; you should know when to incise tumors that have matured completely â€“ this is when the tumor pain subsides, fever disappears, redness and throbbing decrease, and the top of the tumor becomes indented along with other signs.
The incision should be made at the lowest point of the tumor whenever possible so that drainage flows downward, or in the thinnest and most prominent area; the incision should run lengthwise in the body if the tumors are located on the arms and legs.
And the places of muscles and tendons and nerves and arteries, in general in all flat places that have no bend. As for those that bend, let it go with a bandage according to that place. And if the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it alone.
Until its maturity is fully established, as we have said, if you bandage it before that, the pus will flow from it for a long time and it will be full of harm and filth, and perhaps its lips will harden and become rough. Some tumors may be bandaged across the body when necessary or according to what the limb requires.
And it is best to use one bandage for small tumors and a wide bandage or many cuts according to the size of the tumor for large tumors, and some tumors may need the skin to be cauterized and cut if it has become like rags.
And it has become to some extent dead, like what is shown in many abscesses and ulcers, some of which are cut with a three-cornered incision, and some are cut from it in the shape of an ace of spades or an Arabic plum.
Some of them use a round incision and a crescent incision, and so on from these incisions, and if it does not have a head like smooth, flat tumors, it is best to just bandage it with a simple bandage. And if the tumor is large and has collected for a long time and you bandage it, do not hasten so that all the pus comes out at that time.
Rather, draw some of it out, then tighten the tumor to another day, then draw out some pus again. Do this repeatedly gradually until all of it comes out.
Especially if the patient is weak or a pregnant woman or a young child or an old man, for the animal spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus, and the patient may die and you will not feel it. So beware of this matter with extreme caution.
After you have incised these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look; if the wound of the tumor is small or if there is only one simple incision, use a bandage of linen or soft cotton. But if the tumor is large and the incisions of the cutting are many,
you should insert a wick into each incision so that they reach one another. But if the tumor has been cut away from some of its skin or has become wrinkled, you should stuff it with soft cotton or hemp from linen without moisture and tighten it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs while you are working, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them and applying it to the bleeding spot several times.
If the bleeding continues, you should use the measures, treatments, and necessities that we have described in many parts of this book and in its division. If you are in the winter season and the tumor site has many nerves, you should moisten the bandages with a syrup and hot oil.
And place it on the area. If you are in the summer and the areas are hot, you should apply bandages soaked with water and oil or syrup and oil, all of which is cold, until the third day as we said, then you should dissolve the tumor and wipe it.
And use for its treatment what is appropriate until it heals. This is all you need to know about treating tumors in general. As for detailed instructions, I have mentioned how to treat each tumor, summarized and categorized.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision for tumors that appear on the skin of the head.
Small tumors appear on the skin of the head, and they are of a fleshy type contained in sacs; they resemble the gizzard of a chicken. There are many types, some fatty and others containing moisture like mud.
And some of them contain moisture like broth or soup, and the like, and some are petrified and solid; all of them pose no danger in cutting and removing them unless you encounter a vein when making the incision.
The procedure for cutting it is to first pierce it with the instrument whose form comes after this, which is called a dilator, until you know what it contains. If it contains moisture, cut it lengthwise in a simple incision as follows:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~j~, and once the moisture has drained, peel away the sac that contained it and cut it all out, leaving nothing behind at all. For it often recurs if anything remains.
Then soak cotton in soft ointment if available, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it, leaving it until the next day. For it will consume what remains of the sac; then apply the cotton with myrrh again a second and third time as needed.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains, then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains fatty matter, cut it as follows:
Make a firm incision, pulling the edges of the wound together with forceps, and peel it away from all sides. Exert every effort to remove the fascia that surrounds it. If you encounter a vein, do as I have previously described. Do the same for a petrified tumor when making an incision.
The treatment is the same as previously mentioned, and the incision on a petrified tumor is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these tumors found in the heads of certain individuals have no moisture at all.
The head of an old woman, and I found the tumor like a hard, rough stone, white in color, which could not be broken even if someone threw it, and none of the other tumors that occur on the head were like this except for the tumors that occur on the heads of boys.
And at the bases of the ears, split them all with a simple incision and make its bottom flat so that the material flows easily downward, then treat it with what is appropriate for its treatment.
Chapter Forty-Two.
In the incision on pigs that occur in the neck, these tumors often occur in the neck and under the armpits and in the arms, and they may occur throughout the body. The one that occurs in the neck is usually single, but there can be many of them, some arising from others.
Each pig is within its own separate sac, as in hernias and tumors of the head as we described. There are many types of these pigs: some are petrified, some contain moisture, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
I have not seen any of them in a good condition on palpation, and their appearance was close to the color of the skin, they moved in every direction, and were not attached to the neck nerve, nor to a vein or artery, nor were they depressed. It should be split with a simple incision from top to bottom of the body in this shape, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
Remove it from all sides and spread the lips of the skin with a retractor or two retractors or many retractors if needed, as we said in head tumors. Remove it little by little so that it is on the neck without cutting an artery or nerve, and let the scalpel not be too sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting or agitate the patient, so that you cut what you do not need to cut. If you cut a vein or artery and are prevented from working, place powdered alum or some astringents in the wound to stop the bleeding.
Tighten the wound and leave it until the sharpness of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and tends to putrefy, for then the blood will stop. Then return to your work until you are finished, and then search with your index finger if any remains, and then cut off other small piglets and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the piglet, it should not be cut from its origin, but it should be tied with a strong doubled thread and left until it falls off on its own without harm. Then the wound should be stuffed with loose cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment and then treated.
And if the whole piglet is cut off, the lips of the wound should be brought together and sewn immediately after you know that there is absolutely no remainder; but if you see that a remnant of the piglet's bone remains.
It should be intended to cut it from its origin and base, and use the stitching as we have mentioned, and whatever piglets contain moisture should be bandaged with a simple bandage where you see the place of their maturation, and make the bandage what is below as we said.
Then use after the bandage, wrapping with Egyptian ointment or the like to consume what remains of corruption; and when you see that the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Third
On the incision of the larynx for a tumor occurring within the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the larynx, and I have not seen anyone in our country perform it; this is consistent with their statements. As for those suffering from angina, they should avoid making an incision in the larynx, as they will not benefit from it.
This is because all the veins and lungs will be diseased, while those with a hot tumor in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no cause in the trachea, should use an incision in the larynx to escape the calamity that comes from choking.
If we wish to do so, we should cut the throat under three or four small circles of the trachea, in width between two circles as much as the cut is in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage. This place corresponds to the cut.
Because it is devoid of flesh and blood vessels are distant from it, if the healer is timid he should stretch the skin of the throat with a string then cut the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the peritoneum as we have described, and deduce the cutting of the trachea from the phlegm that comes out of it along with the bubbling and the cessation of voice, and leave the wound open for a time. If the time when choking was feared has passed, bring the lips of the wound together with skin.
And sew it together without cartilage, then use medicines that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book says the interpretation of this statement which we have recounted is only if they see the patient has had one of these tumors blocking his throat.
And if the patient is near death and about to expire, they should go to cutting the throat so that the patient can breathe on the wound for a while and be saved from death, but they are commanded to leave the wound open until the illness runs its course, which will take three days or so.
Then they are commanded to sew the wound and treat it until it heals. What I myself have witnessed is that a maidservant took a knife and sent it across her throat, cutting part of the trachea.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was slight, so I was sure that neither an artery nor a vein had been cut, and wind was escaping from the wound. So I proceeded to stitch the wound and treat it until it healed, and nothing happened to the servant except for a little pain in his voice.
And after a few days she returned to her best state, so from this we say that splitting the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision for the tumor that appears in the pharynx from the outside and is called elephantiasis of the throat.
This tumor, which is called elephantiasis of the throat, is a very large tumor the color of the body, and it is common in women, and it is of two types.
It is either natural or accidental, as for the natural kind there is no remedy, while the accidental kind comes in two types: one type is similar to fatty growths and the other type is similar to a tumor that results from the complication of an artery and it is dangerous to incise it. Therefore, it should not be exposed to iron at all, except for a small part of it, if you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty growth and is not attached to any veins, then incise it as you would incise fatty growths. Remove it with its sac if it is in a sac, otherwise, excise all of it and then treat the area as appropriate.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On incision for different types of fatty growths.
Fatty growths are of many types, and I have mentioned all their types in the division, but we should point out here the difference between a fatty growth and an abscess since it is a common confusion: Then the gangrene is completed, at which time the fever and pain subside, and the ulcer is not accompanied by heat, fever or pain; it is enclosed in a peritoneal sac which is its special envelope and it is the color of the body. Its beginning is like a boil and it becomes like a pumpkin, larger or smaller.
It is of two types, either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are numerous as mentioned in the division. If you proceed to treat the ulcer, you should first probe and examine it with an instrument called a speculum, whose image will be shown in the following chapter.
Determine the size of the tumor, then remove the speculum and look at what comes out in its wake. If liquid moisture comes out, regardless of its color, make a simple incision as mentioned for other tumors. If no moisture emerges after using the speculum, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have instructed you, and secure it with forceps. Carefully remove the skin from all sides and preserve the sac if possible so that you can extract it intact with the ulcer.
If the sac ruptures during the procedure and you are unable to remove it intact, which often happens, remove it piece by piece so that nothing remains. For if any part of it remains, whether small or large, the ulcer will most likely recur.
If, despite your best efforts, a small amount remains, cauterize the wound thoroughly with some sharp caustic agents and draw the wound tight. Place something soothing on top of it to ease the hot swelling and treat it with all appropriate remedies until it heals. If the ulcer is large, sew its edges together and treat it with substances that promote healing.
If you encounter a sharp or dull throbbing pain and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered litharge and leave it constricted for two or three days until the wound festers and the blood flow subsides. Then return to excise what remains of the ulcer.

Chapter the forty-seventh.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breast of some people swells up to resemble a woman's breast during puberty, remaining warm and unsightly. So whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast like this:
From line ~b~ to line ~j~. Then remove all the fat, and fill the wound with salty medicine, then bring together the edges of the wound by stitching and treat it until it heals. If the breast sags downward and relaxes due to its size as it happens to women.
It should be incised in its upper sides with two incisions resembling a crescent shape, each connecting to the other at their ends so that the larger line encircles the smaller one as in this image: From line ~b~ to line ~j~, then the skin between the two incisions is peeled back, the fat is removed, and the stitching and dressings we have described are used, along with whatever treatment it requires until it heals.
If the amount you need to cut cannot be removed due to the patient's anxiety or excessive bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in an absorbent ointment and leave it until it absorbs any remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of a hog-like nature, hard and enclosed in a sac; some contain fluids. Those containing fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut like this.
From line ~b~ to line ~j~, and once all the contents of the tumor have been evacuated, pack it with soft cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
Treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of a hog-like knot, incise it as we described for hogs, whether or not pus develops. If pus does develop, use cauterization as previously mentioned.
Chapter forty-nine.
On incising a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If an artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often develops as a result. Similarly, a vein may also swell and form a tumor.
The signs by which one can tell whether a tumor or swelling is from an artery or a vein are as follows: if the tumor is from an artery, it will be rectangular and deep within the body. If you press on the tumor with your finger, you will feel a pulsating sensation.
When it is before a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body, and incision into these tumors is dangerous, especially if they are in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many places on the body where they were very large, so treatment with iron should be avoided.
And if any of them were also in the extremities or head, they should be avoided.  As for those that are swollen at the mouth of the artery, a long incision should be made on the skin and then the incision is opened with forceps, then the artery is stripped and freed from its sheaths.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle under it and pass it to the other side, and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places as you have been taught for the tying of arteries in the fingers, then scoop the place between the ligaments with a curette until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor will dissolve and you should use a treatment that produces pus so that the ligaments fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
But if the tumor is before a cut vein, you should hold as much of the tumor with the skin as possible with your hand, then insert a needle under the place that you are holding with your hand and pass it through with a double thread so that it comes out on the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly as I have explained to you about tying a grape from both sides at the ends of the thread, and if you fear that the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the whole tumor where the first needle intersects.
Tighten your threads in four places, then cut the tumor in its middle so that what is inside it comes out, then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied from it, then put a bandage soaked in syrup and oil on it. Then use treatment consisting of bandages and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that arises from nerve torsion.
Just as a tumor appears in the artery and vein, so it also appears in the nerve if there is a blow to it or excessive exertion or something similar. This occurs most frequently in the wrists and ankles.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a hard tumor whose color resembles that of the rest of the body and is most often painless. If pressed hard, the patient feels a kind of numbness. This tumor is not deep within the body.
But it is under the skin and moves in all directions, neither going forward nor backward. What arises in the joints should not be treated with iron, for it may cause suppuration. Nor should what arises on the head or forehead be treated.
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, hang it with thread and dissect it out; then remove it and bring the wound together with stitches. Treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On cutting off warts that appear on the abdomen.
Warts, called fungous warts because they resemble mushrooms, often appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people. They have a thin base and a thick head, sometimes with split lips. Some are small, while others grow very large.
I once saw a man with two warts on his abdomen that resembled mushrooms in every way. They had a thin, white base, split lips, and were constantly weeping moisture. I cut them off, and one weighed about eighteen ounces, while the other weighed about six ounces.
The operation for cutting them consists of looking to see if the patient is moist and the color of the wart is white, moist, thin in origin. Cut it with a wide scalpel and have your forceps in the fire, as blood often flows profusely when it is cut.
If blood overcomes you, cauterize it. If you see the patient cowardly and afraid of cutting with iron, take a tight lead thread and tighten it around the wart described above and leave it for two days, then increase the tightening of the lead, continuing to tighten it.
Continue tightening until the wart breaks off on its own without pain. If the wart is thick at its base, there is some fear and risk in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut it in half or partially.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and treat it until the wound heals. Be careful not to cut a wart that is dark in color, numb, and looks malignant, for it is a cancerous tumor, and we will discuss cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the Treatment of Cancerous Growths.
Cancerous growths arise from many causes, either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen from which the omentum or intestines protrude as is seen in all hernias; or from blood that flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned; or from wind that collects within it.
If it arises from a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the omentum, the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft, and painless, and its appearance will vary. If it arises from the protrusion of the intestines, its position will be even more different than what we have described. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns. There may be gurgling associated with it, and it increases greatly when entering a bath and during strenuous activity. If it arises from moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when grasped with the hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it was previously blood, then with these signs, the swelling will turn black. If it was previously newly formed flesh, the swelling will be hard and fixed at a constant size.
If it was previously wind, then touching it will be soft. The procedure for this is to look and see if the swelling arose from arterial or venous blood or wind. You should abstain from treating it, as there is fear and deception in doing so, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned the tumors that occur from arteries and veins.
But if the swelling arose from the intestines or phlegm, you should order the patient to hold himself and stand straight and then mark with ink all around the swelling. Then order him to lie on his back between your hands and cut around the swelling with a wide scalpel on the spot you marked with ink. Then extend the center of the swelling upward with a large trocar and tie the incision site with a strong thread or silk suture tightly.
The ligature should be tight, then open the center of the tense swelling above the ligature and insert your index finger.  Seek the intestine. If you find that it has been caught by the ligature, loosen the ligature and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find phlegm, extend it with a trocar.
Cut off its excess. If you encounter an artery or vein, ligate it well and return to your work. Take two needles and thread strong threads through them. Insert the needles into the incision you made around the swelling, crossing them, and then tighten the swelling in four places on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the tightened flesh rots and falls off on its own, or cut it if it softens and rots. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the swelling arose from newly formed flesh within it...
Or if it is from moisture, the tumor should be cauterized as we have said, and the blood or moisture found in it should be drawn out, then it should be treated with something that will heal the area.
Chapter Thirty-Five.

Chapter Fifty-Seven.

Then quickly raise the skin upwards and extract the head of the penis; then let the blood flow a little, for this is best and least likely to cause swelling of the penis; then dry it with a damp cloth and sprinkle over it dried, roasted pumpkin ashes which are the best I have found, or also powdered tragacanth. Chapter Fifty-Eight.

It is made of silver and is thin, smooth, and hollow like a bird's feather quill in fineness and length, about a palm and a half long; it has a delicate funnel at its head. The way to draw urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie a wick or cotton firmly at its end.

This is continued until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Sixty.
On Removing Stones.

It is a stone that is generated in the bladder, especially one that becomes lodged in the urethra, and I will describe it with a clear explanation and brevity.

You make the incision between the scrotum and the testicles, not in the middle but to the side of the left testicle; the incision should be upon the stone itself, with your finger in the urethra pressing it outward so that the incision from outside will be wide and from inside narrow, as much as is possible for the stone to come out.

And some are smooth and resemble an acorn, and they roll easily so that their removal is easy; but if it has angles and projections, you should enlarge the incision a little; if it does not come out this way, then you must try to manipulate it, either by grasping it with forceps whose tip is like a file.
Let him position himself over the stone so that it does not escape. Then, either introduce a fine instrument with a blunt end underneath it. If this is unsuccessful, widen the incision slightly. If blood flow becomes excessive, cauterize it with alum.

Either he will die, or he will suffer constant urine leakage because the site will not heal at all. Instead, try to push them out or maneuver to break them with forceps so that they come out in pieces.

Its tip is triangular and sharp, fixed into a handle. Then take a thread and tie it to the rod below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.  Then gently insert the steel instrument into the urethra until the instrument reaches the stone itself.

Chapter Sixty-One.
On Removing Stones from Women.
Stones rarely develop in women. If one of them does have a stone, its treatment is difficult and often impossible for several reasons: one is that the woman may be a virgin, and the other is that you will rarely find a woman who will allow herself to be examined by a doctor.

There is danger in that, for if necessity calls for it, a female physician who is skilled should be taken, which is rarely found; if she is not available, seek a chaste and kind physician, or bring a midwife who is skilled in the affairs of women, or a woman who has some indication in this craft.

Chapter sixty-two.
On incision for watery tumors.
Watery tumors are a collection of moisture in the white peritoneum that is under the skin of the testicles surrounding the testicle, and it is called the scrotum; it may be in a special membrane prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
It may even be mistaken for another testicle, and it occurs between the skin of the testicles and the white peritoneum we mentioned; this is only rare. These tumors arise from weakness that afflicts the testicles and builds up this material, or they may develop after a blow to the testicles.
This moisture comes in many colors: it may be yellow, or bloody red, or dark purple, or watery white, which is the most common. The signs by which it is recognized areâ€¦
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the tumor will be somewhat elongated like an egg and the testicle will not be visible because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in a special membrane, the tumor will be round on one side of the testicle.
Because of this, a person may mistake it for another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white peritoneum, then it will be palpable. As for knowing the color of the moisture, probe the tumor with the square probe whose image we have shown; judge by what comes out from under the probe.
If we turn to treatment with iron, it is necessary to order the patient to be bled if he can, and you see that his body is full; then he should lie on his back on something a little high and you should put many rags under him; then you should sit on his left.
You order a servant to sit on his right and extend the patient's penis to one side of the scrotum skin and towards the direction of the abdomen; then you take a wide bistoury and cut lengthwise through the middle of the scrotum skin, nearly to the perineum.
You make the incision straight and parallel to the line that divides the scrotum skin in half, until it reaches the white peritoneum which you then dissect out and be careful not to cut it; the dissection should be done more from the side that adheres to the testicle and you should extend the dissection as much as possible.
Then you place a wide tampon over the peritoneum filled with fluid and extract all the water; then you separate the lips of the incision with speculums and elevate the peritoneum upwards, not touching the containing scrotal skin; you cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either entirely or in pieces.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not thoroughly cut it, you are not safe from the water returning; and if the testicle protrudes outside its skin while you are working, when you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it and then bring together the lips of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it like all other wounds until it is healed; but if you find that the testicle has become diseased from some other cause, then it is necessary to tie the vessels in the spermatic cord to prevent hemorrhage and then cut off the testicle from the spermatic cord, remove the testicle and treat it as we have described.
If the collected fluid is on both sides, know that it is hydrocele; cut the other side as you have done in the first, and if it seems to you that the operation can be one, do so; then wipe away the blood.
Introduce wadding that has been soaked in oil or rose ointment into the incisions and apply a second layer of cotton wool soaked in wine and oil over the testicles. Cover the abdomen with a sheet and place a folded rag, the "rufayda", on top. Tie it from above with a bandage having six ends in this manner:
Then take two pieces of cloth, sew them together and stuff them with fluffed cotton as shown. Make the ends to be tied with cotton or damp wool and gather them tightly into a trouser belt. One of the ends should extend from the seat to the back.
The second end extends towards the groin, with the sides running down the thighs and the remaining ends on the upper arms. Gather all the tightness into a trouser belt. This bandage can also be made by cauterization instead of cutting with an iron.
This involves taking a fine, skinny iron and cutting the scrotum skin while protecting it as previously described until the white peritoneum containing fluid is exposed. Then take another iron in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye and then cauterize the peritoneum while protecting it until all the moisture comes out. Then stretch the peritoneum with forceps and peel off the layer containing it, cutting it according to what is possible to remove it entirely. Be careful not to touch the egg with fire.
Know that this method is safer and further from bloodletting and better than working with incisions. If you encounter a hot swelling or bleeding or something else during the incision or cauterization, you should treat it according to what has been previously mentioned.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot bear this procedure because of its frightening nature, then use a bandage as I describe it. This is valid if it aligns with the evidence we have presented.
The abscess is watery, so the patient should sit on a high chair and then a wide speculum should be inserted under the swelling, and the incision should be made along the body with a wide opening so that all the water flows out, then place cotton on the area and tie it.
Leave it to drain the rest of the water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out while you are cutting it, this is because the white membrane obstructs the mouth of the wound and prevents the water from coming out.
Then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument with which water is extracted from lovers, whose picture has been presented, or widen the opening of the wound a little.
Know that the water may return and collect after six months or so, and if it gathers, cut it in the same manner and the patient should rest for a few days.
Chapter sixty-three.
On incision of fleshy abscesses and their treatment.
Know that incision of these abscesses is a dangerous undertaking leading to death in most cases, so we prefer leaving them and staying safe from them. Nevertheless, I will mention the procedure and types of abscesses.
I say that many tumors can occur in the body, which are composed of anthrax, and this happens for many reasons, either from excess humor flowing into the anthrax or from a blow. The color of the tumor is the same as the body and there is no water in it.
The tumor is hard, and it may be petrified, its color dark and without sensation. It may also be due to the complexity of the arteries and their swelling, as mentioned earlier, or from the swelling of the veins. Swelling of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the tumor.
Do not press it with your fingers at all, but the one caused by swelling of the veins does not disperse anything when pressed with the fingers, and this type can be incised along with all other flesh.
And it is to cut the skin of the testicles, then to extend the testicle upwards and to remove it from the white peritoneum, and to free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and to tie the vessels and to cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from all sides of the testicle, if the testicle has adhered to those nascent flesh.
Then the testicle should be removed and cut off, and if the adhesion is between some of the peritoneums or among the vessels, then all that adhesion should be freed and cut in a circular manner.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which occurs behind it, then it should all be cut and the testicle removed as we have said, and when your work is done, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with all other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Fourth.
On the Treatment of Hernia with a Testicle.
The testicle is a twisted tumor, somewhat like a cluster with relaxed ligaments, making it difficult for the patient to move, exercise and walk. In treating this ailment, what follows is similar to what has been mentioned previously.
But it should be mentioned that we act in this case as our predecessors did. I say that the patient should sit on a high chair, then push the spermatic cord downwards, and grasp the skin of the testicle with your fingers along with the vessels which are near the penis.
And have a servant hold it and stretch it firmly, then make a wide incision with a sharp scalpel in the direction of the vessels until they are exposed, then peel them off from all sides as I mentioned to you when stripping the arteries in the fingers, then insert a needle with a doubled thread into them.
Tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared, and also tie it at the last place, then make a vertical incision in the middle along the length of the body and remove all the foul and corrupted humors that have accumulated there.
Then treat the ovary like all other wounds in which you desire a delay to occur so that the vessels feeding one of the ovaries may fall away; there is no harm in that. But if the ovary presents with all its vessels, then it is necessary for one of the ovaries to be removed along with its vessels so that the egg does not become deprived of nourishment. Chapter sixty-fifth.
In the treatment of intestinal hemorrhoids.
This hernia occurs from a rupture appearing in the extended peritoneum on the abdomen toward the groins, whereupon the intestines protrude from that hernia into one of the ovaries.
This hernia is either from a rupture in the peritoneum or from its extension, and these two types occur for many reasons, such as a blow, a leap, a shout, lifting something heavy, and so forth. The sign of it, if it is from the extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly and the swelling is level towards the depth because the peritoneum presses upon the intestines, and the sign of it if it is from a rupture in the peritoneum is that it occurs immediately with great sudden pain and the swelling appears distinctly under the skin nearby.
This is due to the protrusion of the intestines and their passage outside the peritoneum, and sometimes the omentum protrudes with the intestines; this hernia is then called an intestinal and omental hernia. It may also occur with flatulence, and pus may form in the intestines and become trapped there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when pressed, the treatment of these types of conditions with iron is dangerous, and you should be wary of using it. The prescription for action is to order the patient to return the intestines to their interior with their hand if they happen to return.
Then he lies on his back between your hands and raises his legs, then you extend the skin next to the groin upwards and cut all the scrotal skin lengthwise. Then you insert pins into the edges of the incision as much as needed to open it with them.
The incision should be as long as the egg can be extracted, then you peel away the peritoneum under the scrotal skin until the white, firm peritoneum is exposed on all sides. At that point, insert your index finger next to the egg.
Between the white peritoneum under the scrotal skin and the second peritoneum, loosen the adhesion behind the egg, then with your right hand bend it inwards into the scrotal skin. With this, extend the white peritoneum upwards with your left hand and raise the egg with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the attendant to pull the egg upwards and you completely release the adhesion behind it. Then search with your finger so that there is nothing of twisted intestine in the white, firm peritoneum; if you find any, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then you take a needle with thick thread twisted from ten threads and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin next to the incision. Then you cut off the ends of the thread bend so that there are four threads. Then you bind some of them together in a firm manner and tie the peritoneum under the scrotal skin with a tight ligature on both sides. Then you wind the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels feeding it can reach it. This is so that no hot tumor arises, and a second ligature is made outside the first one, further away by less than two fingers. After these two ligatures you leave a portion of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin as long as a finger bone.
This is so that no hot tumor arises, and a second ligature is made outside the first one, further away by less than two fingers. After these two ligatures you leave a portion of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin as long as a finger bone. And the rest is cut around in a circular fashion and the egg is removed with it, then the lower skin of the scrotum is incised in a line from which blood and serum flow as we have previously described, then wool soaked in oil is applied to the wound and a ligature is used as we have described.
And the white peritoneum which we said should be cut with caution to avoid bleeding, for this often occurs, is cauterized and the ligature is left until it falls off by itself; if it delays falling off, let it be moistened with hot water and a poultice applied that will cause sweating, until it falls off.
Then the wound is treated like all other wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Sixth.
In windy hernias, I have not seen anyone dare to treat them with iron, as those who came before us described it should be done as we described in hernias which are accompanied by a testis; that is, the vessels are tied gently below after they are incised.
Then it is incised in the middle and the tumor is treated with what will open it up so that the vessels fall off, and the wound is treated as we have mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Sixth.
In a hernia that occurs in the groin.
As we said, a hernia may occur in the groin, and it prolapses from its location but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum, and even if some does descend it is slight and always returns.
But if it lasts for a long time, the opening in the peritoneum increases so that intestine and omentum descend into the scrotum, which occurs because of the extension of the peritoneum in the groin as we have said. And that is because the peritoneum stretches and then relaxes, the place bulges and protrudes, and it is treated by cautery as we have previously described.
It may also be treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then the site of the warm hernia is incised transversely to a width of three fingers. Then the peritoneum under the skin is sutured.
Until the white peritoneum beneath it is exposed, you take a ligature and place it upon the site resulting from the peritoneum, pressing it down to the depth of the abdomen. Then you sew the two resulting sites on either side of the ligature to the peritoneum and bind them together with sutures.
Then you draw the ligature tight and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else as I have instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernias; then treat it like other surgical wounds.
When the sutures have been removed, you clean them and dress the wound until it heals; the peritoneum then contracts and the hernia does not protrude further.
Cautery is best in this situation as it is closest to safety.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
On Relaxation of the Scrotum.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly. Therefore, he who wishes to treat it should put the patient to sleep on his back.
He cuts away all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, then brings the two lips together. If you wish to suture first, the excess relaxed skin with three or four sutures and ensure they are secure, then cut between the sutures.
Then treat it like all other surgical wounds until it heals and the sutures fall out.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book of mine; however, I have done so for two reasons: one that it may be within the knowledge of the physician if he is asked about it and so that he may know how to treat someone who has undergone it; and the second that we often need to castrate some animals for our own benefit.
As for rams, weasels, cats and the like of animals, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by pressure or by incision and cutting.  That which is done by pressure, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its genitals relax, soften and dangle.
Then you press it with your hand until it dissolves and is not apparent to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, it should be that you hold the animal and compress the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the spermatic cords and make one cut on each testicle.
Until the two testicles protrude, cut them off after you have skinned them and do not leave on them any of the peritoneum except the thin peritoneum that is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is done by pressure.
Because pressure may leave something of the testicles so that the animal desires copulation, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy
On the Treatment of Hermaphrodites.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two types, one of which appears below the space or in the scrotal skin between the testicles in a shape like a woman's vulva with hair and urine may flow from what is in the scrotal skin.
As for women, there is one type which occurs above the vulva on the mons pubis like small male genitals issuing outward, one of them as if it were a man's penis and the two like testicles. The treatment of the three types is as follows.
The two types in men and the one type in women, it is necessary to cut off that excess flesh until its trace is gone, then treat it with the treatment of other surgeries until it heals. As for the second type of men from whom urine flows from what is in the scrotal skin, there is no action on it nor benefit whatsoever.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the cutting of the vulva and the flesh that comes from the pudenda of women.
The vulva may have grown excessively beyond the natural state, so that it becomes prominent and unsightly, and in some women it may become enlarged to such an extent that it resembles the male genitals and is suitable for copulation.
Therefore, one should grasp the excess part of the vulva with one's hand or with forceps and cut it off, not cutting too deeply, especially at the base so as not to cause hemorrhage.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals, and as for the nascent flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
It may sometimes protrude outward like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients called it the "tail disease," and one should cut it off just as one cuts the vulva, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when the vagina of a woman is not perforated or has a small perforation, which may be congenital or acquired. Acquired prolapse results from an underlying cause and is usually due to excess fleshy growth.
It may also be due to a thin or thick peritoneum, located either deep in the womb, on its sides, above it, or below it; it prevents copulation, conception, and childbirth, and may even prevent menstruation.  Knowledge of this condition is gained through examination by a midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and nearby, if not, examine it with her fingers or a speculum. If the obstruction is due to thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, proceed by puncturing it. This is done by placing something resembling stirrups on the labia, then place both thumbs of your hands together.
The woman is laid on her back with her legs spread, then the labia are forcefully stretched until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the barrier opens. Then she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the spot, and has intercourse with the woman every day lest the place unite again.
But if the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a wide spatula resembling an aspen leaf. If the barrier is of newly grown flesh, suspend it with forceps and cut it. Have remedies to stanch bleeding without cauterizing, such as quince, mastic, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not heal quickly, hold it for days and use a dry flax wick. Then treat it with all the remedies for healing until it is cured.  It may happen that another growth of flesh appears in the womb which should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, for a cancerous tumor in the womb should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter seventy-three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts and red pimples that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are the swelling of the openings of veins until a great deal of blood flows constantly from them. If hemorrhoids persist, they become warts, as mentioned in the division of types and symptoms. We shall mention them here.
We shall speak of what is treatable and what is not treatable among them, and say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep in the uterus and do not appear to the sense, are not treatable with iron. Only those that are in the mouth of the uterus and felt by touch are treatable.
The woman should enter a cold room, then the warts should be stretched with a speculum or a rough cloth and cut from their roots. Then, when blood flows, one of the styptic drugs which do not cauterize should be applied to them.
Like the cresses and parsley and frankincense and the like, then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, sit her in a decoction of marshmallow root, pomegranate peel and rue and the like, then take barley flour and knead it with honey and vinegar.
Apply it to her back, if the bleeding stops, otherwise adhere to the solid bandages on the breasts and armpits without restriction. Then take a wick and dip it in the juice of purslane or birthwort or the juice of elder branches with a rue drink and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for red lichen, it resembles the heads of rushes, rough in appearance, you should cut off what appears from it as I mentioned in warts and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Fourth.
On the incision of an abscess that appears in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may appear in the uterus, such as cancer, petrified tumors, ulcers, fistulas, ulcers, warts, hemorrhoids, fissures, corns and hot tumors. I have mentioned all these diseases, their types and symptoms in the division.
We should mention in this article the hot tumor that appears in the uterus if it is one of the tumors that collects for a period, how its incision with iron would be. You should look to see if the pain of the tumor at its beginning is sharp with throbbing, inflammation and fever.
And if you see it red when touched, do not rush to incise it and treat it with what helps ripening until these symptoms subside. Then she should sit on a chair with two seats and lean on her back.
And bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and place her arms under her knees and tie them with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the picture of which will come later. If you wish to use this instrument, you should estimate the depth of the woman's uterus so that what enters from the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
It hurts the patient if the instrument is longer than the depth, so pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the whole instrument from entering the depth of the uterus, and the spiral that runs in the instrument should be from the upper side.
You hold the instrument and the servant turns the spiral until the cervix opens, then if the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, it should be cut at its head with a wide scalpel, and if all the time has elapsed.
A soft wick soaked in rose oil or green oil with some astringents should be placed in the wound, and the wick should come out of the incision in the cervix, and pure wool soaked in water that has boiled with baker's yeast should be placed on the outside of the uterus and the vulva.
Then it is treated after two days with the ointment suitable for that until it heals.
The uterus and the wound may be washed by injecting it with water and honey, then with water that has been boiled with the root of licorice or long asparagus, then return to the ointment. If the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Then treatment with iron should be avoided and treated as mentioned in the division.
Chapter Seventy-Fifth.
On teaching midwives how to treat live fetuses that come out in an unnatural way.
The midwife should first know the shape of natural childbirth, and its signs are that if you see the woman leaning down and yearning to smell the air, it will ease what she is in labor with.
And hasten the exit of the fetus, know that this birth will be in a natural manner and its exit will be on its head with the placenta, or hanging from its cord, so if you see these signs, it is necessary to press her abdomen so that the fetus comes out quickly.
For if it descends on its head, the placenta will descend with it and be cleansed from those superfluities in a good manner. And except for this birth, it is unnatural and blameworthy, because it may happen that the fetus comes out on its legs and its hands come out before its head and legs.
Or one hand or one leg comes out, or its head and a hand come out, or it comes out folded, and perhaps it turns on its back, or something like these blameworthy forms. So the midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things and beware of slipping and error.
And I will explain each form and how to deal with it so that you can recognize and understand them. If the fetus comes out on its head, this is a natural delivery, and if it becomes difficult for the woman and labor pains are hard for her and you see that her strength has weakened, seat her on a chair and order some women to hold her steady.
And apply a poultice of boiled fenugreek and wet ointments to her womb, then let the midwife insert a small speculum between her fingers and cut the placenta with it or cut it with her fingernail until what is inside flows out, and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, the woman should be injected with fenugreek saliva mixed with henna oil; then order her to sneeze and tickle her with a feather, while holding her mouth and nose, for the fetus will come out in its hour. If the fetus's hand comes out first, it should gently be pushed back a little at a time.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a platform and raise her legs upward; then shake the platform on the ground while the woman holds on so as not to fall when shaken. If the hands still do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or bind his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, for he will come out, emerging on his feet. If the fetus emerges on its feet, you should push them upwards, then turn the fetus little by little until it is straightened. Then take one of its legs and extend it little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, order her to move and push with a speculum, for he will come out. If he does not come out as we have described, or otherwise, return the fetus little by little until it becomes in its natural shape. Then it will come out smoothly. If he refuses everything that has been described.
Take saliva of the hyssop, saliva of fennel, fat from the marrow and dissolved mastic, and beat all of them in a mortar very well. Then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen. Then seat her in lukewarm water until the effects are felt.
If you see that it has relaxed below, make for her a suppository of rue and let her hold it in; if she holds the suppository for an hour, seat her on a chair, then push with a speculum, close her mouth and nose, and squeeze her lower abdomen lightly. The fetus will come out at its appointed time.
If he comes out in this manner, proceed to insert his hands a little at a time; then let the woman lie on her back and dangle her legs downwards while lying on a bed, clasping her hands together; then squeeze above her abdomen lightly. Then remove what is under her legs from the pillows so that she becomes upright below; if the fetus does not come out, take hold of both her legs and move them vigorously; then squeeze above the pudendal cleft lightly until the fetus ascends upwards; then let the midwife insert her hand and straighten the fetus a little at a time.
Order the woman to push until the fetus comes out, for the fetus is coming out obstructed, delayed by one of its hands.  If you can put back its hand, then do so. If you cannot put it back, make the woman stand and have her walk.  If she cannot walk, lay her down on her back.
Her bed and shake her legs vigorously; then use the forceps that I have described from the specula. If the pains seize her, seat her on a chair, then proceed to put back the hand and arrange the fetus into its natural shape.  Have the woman sneeze and order her to push until it comes out.
If the fetus comes out on its head with its arms extended and its face towards its mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's hand and then turn it little by little, straightening it gently. Once it is straight, apply to the mother's vulva the ointment and specula that we have described.
Then have her move a little at a time; and if she does, seat her on a chair and order her to lean to the right side. Then have her push and sneeze her with a speculum. The fetus will then be born.
If it descends into the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, split the placenta with your fingernail until you are rid of the moisture; if it is attached to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the cervix so that it will not be strangled and die, then push the fetus inside until it is positioned correctly.
Order the woman to shift several times, for it comes out easily; as for the birth of twins or many fetuses, know that twins are often born and three or four may be born and live, but that is rare. As for five, it is something outside of nature and they do not live at all.
And they are surrounded by one placenta, and they are separated by a partition membrane tied at the cervix of each one of them; they may come out as a single fetus comes out easily and naturally, or they may come out in a difficult manner as we have described.
If some of them are in distress during birth and take on the unnatural forms we have described, then treat them as we have described. The midwife should be gentle and do everything with care, patience, and devise every means possible to achieve safety.
Know that fetuses conceived in the womb may be miscarried; one, two, three, four, five, six, seven or more than ten may be conceived. I have heard it confirmed that a woman miscarried seven and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the grace and knowledge of God Almighty, all this should be known by midwives, for they may encounter such situations one day and handle them accordingly.
Chapter Seventy-Sixth.
On the Expulsion of a Stillborn Fetus.
If you have treated the stillborn fetus as described in the section on treatment and it does not emerge, and you are forced to proceed manually, then you should observe if the woman is healthy and strong and does not exhibit any symptoms that would put her at risk of harm. In this case, she should lie on her back on a bed.
Its head should be inclined downwards and its legs raised, with an attendant holding it steady from both sides, or tied to the bed so that its body is not drawn during delivery of the fetus.
Then the mouth of her womb should be moistened with soft unguents, mixed with the saliva of the mastich tree and fenugreek seeds, then the midwife should anoint her hand with these unguents and saliva, and gently insert it into the womb. She should then search for a suitable place to embed the forceps, and the corresponding locations.
See if the fetus is coming out headfirst, then embed the forceps in its eyes, or in its temple, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its clavicle, or in places near the ribs, or under the scapulae.
But if it is coming out feet first, then in the vulva and the middle ribs; the forceps should be held in the right hand, its curve between the fingers of the left hand. The hand should be gently inserted and a forceps embedded in some of those places we have mentioned.
Until it reaches something empty, then embed another or a third forceps opposite it if needed, so the pull is even and does not lean to one side. Then extend steadily, but the extension should not be straight only; move it to every direction.
This will make it easier for it to come out, just as is done when extracting a tooth. Between extensions, the midwife should loosen her grip; if any part gets stuck, she should anoint some fingers with unguent and insert them from that side to turn what is stuck. If any part of the fetus comes out, she should move the forceps to other places.
She should lift it slightly while doing this until the whole fetus comes out. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be pushed back because it is compressed, she should wrap a cloth around it and pull until the whole hand comes out, then cut it off.
Whether in the wrist joint or elbow, and so it should be done in the other hand and in both legs. If the head of the fetus is large and subject to pressure during delivery, or if there is water collected in its head, a pointed bistoury should be inserted between the fingers.
And it should be split with it so that the water comes out, or it can be inserted with an instrument called a forceps, the image of which will come with the images of all the other instruments in the chapter after this.
The same should be done if the fetus is large-headed by nature, for it is necessary to split the skull or crush it with forceps as we said, then remove the bones with pincers. If the head emerges and gets compressed at the nape of the neck, it should be split until the moisture in the chest drains.
For the chest will then unite. If it does not unite and it is impossible for it to come out, cut what you can. If the lower abdomen is warm or contains pus, it should be poulticed so that the moisture drains from it.
If the fetus is on its legs, pulling it out is easy and straightening it to the mouth of the womb is simple. If it gets compressed at the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand and the abdomen or chest should be split until what is inside flows out.
If all the other limbs are removed and the head returns and gets stuck, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix and use it to seek the head and pull it towards the mouth of the womb with her fingers. Then she should insert a spoon or two spoons and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has closed due to a hot swollen tumor, it should not be forced. Rather, at that time wet fatty substances should be poured on it and poultices should be used, and she should sit in waters which soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, if it can be straightened, use what we mentioned for the living fetus. If not, cut in pieces and remove it, and nothing of the placenta should be left inside at all. When you are finished with your treatment, use what we mentioned for the treatment of hot tumors that occur in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, let her descend into grasping waters and use all other treatment; I have seen a woman who had been pregnant, the fetus died in her womb, then she became pregnant again.
The other fetus also died, and after a long time, a tumor appeared in her womb and swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, so I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I applied some strong drawing ointments to it.
A bone came out of the place, then after a few days, another bone came out. I was amazed by this because there are no bones in the abdomen. I concluded that they were bones from the dead fetus, so I searched the wound and removed many bones, especially those from the head of the fetus.
I continued to do this until I removed many bones from her, and the woman was in excellent health. She lived for a long time afterward, with only a little pus coming from the place. I have presented this rare case here.
Because it contains knowledge and support for what the physician, the creator, tries to achieve with his hand in treatment.
