Chapter Seventy-Eight.
On the delivery of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should order the patient to hold herself and then sneeze with a speculum while you pull on her mouth and nostrils; if it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, take a quantity.
And pierce a hole in the lid and put in it herbs which are open to the womb, such as pennyroyal, rue, fennel, chamomile, hyssop, wormwood, and angelica; all these drugs or some of them; and immerse it in water and place it on the fire. Then you put a silver tube on the hole in the lid and the other end into the mouth of the womb.
Hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the womb, then sneeze as we said; the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in sheep fat or camel saliva.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta; if she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little until it comes out. If it is stuck deep in the womb, insert the hand as we have described until, when you find the placenta, pull it out gently, a little at a time.
So that the uterus does not fall out when pulled hard, but it should be moved gently to the right and left sides, then increase the amount of pulling; it will respond at that time and get rid of adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, beware of violence in pulling on it again; but you should tie what has come out from the mouth of the womb to the woman's thigh and then inject her with four-part ointment; for it will soften it after a few days.
And it dissolves and comes out, but if it becomes putrid, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and this harms the patient; therefore, one should use appropriate incense.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried incense made from aloe wood and dried figs. Here is an image of the instrument with which women are fumigated when they have a retention of menses or placenta, etc.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper. The thin end is placed in the vagina and the wide end on the fire. Incense is placed on the embers; it is held until that incense is gone, and then it is replaced.
Chapter Ninety-Seventh.
In the Treatment of an Unruptured Fistula.
Many boys are born with unruptured fistulas, often closed by a thin layer of peritoneum. The midwife should puncture this peritoneum with her finger; otherwise, she should incise it with a sharp curette, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she should place a wick soaked in wine and oil on it and treat it with ointments until it heals. If there is fear that it will close again, insert a lead tube into the opening for many days. Remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
An unruptured fistula may also result from the contraction of a wound or a tumor.  It should be incised, and then treated as described above.
Chapter Eighty.
On the Treatment of Fistulas That Occur in the Lower Parts.
Fistulas that occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening that happens near the anus, either externally or within the space from one side, and there may be one or more fistulous tracts.
When this complication softens and opens, a watery, whitish fluid or thin pus may flow from it. Some of these fistulas may extend to the rectum and intestines; others are not connected. Those that communicate can be recognized by the passage of feces and flatus through them when the patient defecates.
Sometimes worms may come out of it, and there may be fistulas from it if it is in the space that penetrates to the bladder and into the urethra, or penetrates to the hip joint and to the tailbone. And what is known as a fistula penetrating to the seat is also known without penetration.
Insert your middle finger into the anus and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula if there is no curvature in the fistula, but if there is a curvature, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger, if you do not feel it at all and nothing of feces, gas, or worms protrudes from the hole as we have said, know that it is not penetrating, so hasten to treatment.
And if the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urethra, its guide is the exit of urine from it and its refusal to unite the place with medicines. As for if it penetrates to the hip joint or to the tailbone, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If there is no curvature to the bone and there is pain in the vicinity and pus constantly discharges from it, and treatment does not work in it and it does not unite with ointment, and these penetrating fistulas are all, there is no cure for them at all.
And treating them is a vain effort for whoever attempts it from ignorant doctors, but the one that is not penetrating and not chronic, healing is expected by iron as I will describe and what I have tried, which is to lay the patient down on his back between your hands.
Raise his legs upwards and tilt his thighs towards his stomach, then insert a copper or lead probe if there is a curvature in the fistula until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, then you should insert your middle finger into the anus.
If you feel that the probe has penetrated of its own accord, exposed and without feeling anything between your finger and it, either fascia or flesh, then know for sure that it has passed through. Do not tire yourself with it, for there is no healing from it as we have said.
They have said that it heals in some people rarely, and of the treatments from which benefit is hoped for is to heat a thin iron according to the width of the fistula as previously mentioned and insert it heated into the fistula until it reaches near the seat, then return it two or three times.
Until you know that all of that excess, tangled flesh which resembles a bird's quill tube has been burned, then treat it with a twisted cloth moistened in butter until that burned flesh comes out, then treat it with ointments made with salt. If it heals.
Otherwise, it will not heal with any other treatment ever. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger at the seat and there is a thick veil of flesh or fascia between it and the probe, and you see the fistula following the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And while passing with the incision and the probe inside the fistula, continue until you reach where the end of the probe is, then the probe will come loose and fall out. Then cleanse those excess tangled flesh which resemble a bird's quill tube so that nothing remains of it.
Then treat it with ointments made with salt until it heals. If the blood overwhelms you and prevents you from working to cut those flesh, the best thing to do and the quickest benefit is to cauterize them with fire or a caustic drug because cautery with fire combines two good states: burning that excess flesh.
And it cuts the blood and dries the moisture, then treat it with a twisted cloth moistened in butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the area festers and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound at that time with ointments made with salt which promote the growth of hard flesh.
These are medicines that contain astringents and drying agents. If they congeal, the wound heals and there is no fear of recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the seat and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the seat and explore it.
If you feel the probe and there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh between you and it, and it is close to penetration, then the only course of action is one of hope and chance. You should use one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said.
Or make an incision until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the ligature and treat it from close proximity, but do not overdo the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the seat and cause the patient to involuntarily expel feces; then treat him as we have mentioned, for he may be cured as we said.
Or pierce that barrier after making an incision near the seat as we have said, either with a probe or another sharp instrument, then clean out all the adhering flesh in the incision as thoroughly as you can.
Then suture the entire wound along with the incision and the upper opening so that it closes, leaving the fistula open inside the seat; this will be easier for the patient, and the fistula may heal in this manner.
If you insert the probe into the fistula and it is on the side of the seat towards the surface of the body and the skin surrounding the seat, then take a probe with a pierced end like a shoemaker's awl in this manner:
And insert a twisted thread of five threads or so into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it is perforated in the edge of the seat from the inside near
then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the seat and pulling out the end of the thread, then gather both ends together and tighten them, leaving it for a day or two. Every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it firmly so that the flesh between the two ends of the thread is severed and falls away, then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the nostril is not perforated, perforate it as best you can, unless it is too deep, in which case do not do so on account of the muscle lest you should cut it; then proceed with it as we have described until it is healed.
The scalpel for splitting the nostrils has a very sharp point, and the other side is not sharp lest it should cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids which bleed and their removal, and on the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds: either they are inside the anus, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and some are small and some large, and blood always flows from them.
They may be single or multiple, and they may be outside the anus, at its edges; but those which are outside the anus are, in most cases, less moist, from them flows a yellowish fluid or a little blood that oozes constantly, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to order the patient to defecate and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids are visible to you. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or hold them with your fingernail and cut them off at their base; if the forceps do not hold them because of their moisture and relaxation...
...grab it with a coarse cloth or a piece of toasted bread, or pull it with your fingers and cut it off; then sprinkle some sharp grains on it after cutting so that they will act as cautery, or burn them as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with all the usual remedies until it heals. If the anus does not protrude for you, inject the patient with an enema containing a little spice to wash out what is in the anus and make it protrude quickly when the patient strains; as for the hemorrhoids that are outside the anus, their treatment is easy.
Take it with your fingernail or hang it on a thread and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it is healed. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use its stalk in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then draw the wart upwards and pierce it with the needle at its base from the other side, wind the ends of the thread under the needle while it is obstructing and tighten the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the warts and leave one without removing it.
So that excess blood flows from it, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or quince on the seat and put it on the seat and order the patient to rest, then leave him until the warts fall off. When the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and all other treatment until they are healed.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often appear from dryness and constipation, if it persists and medicine does not help, then you should scrape it with a scalpel blade or your fingernail until it becomes moist and removes the upper crust that prevents it from healing.
Then treat it until it heals properly, if it does not heal, repeat the scraping more intensely than the first time until it bleeds well and peels, then treat it, for it will be cured.
Chapter eighty-second.
On the treatment of inverted and everted corns, dry warts, and calluses.
A corn is a round knot the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, found throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes. Pain arises from it when walking.
Therefore, it should be cut around the corn and gripped with forceps or a thread and pulled out from its root, then treated; if you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, hard, round bump above the surface of the body.
It is often presented in the hands of boys and its treatment is to lengthen the warts and cut them or cauterize them with a silk thread or hair until they fall off, and if you wish, burn them as previously mentioned with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Inverted and non-inverted warts may also be removed with the instrument which I have mentioned in the treatment of the mole; as for the mole, it is also a small, thick, adherent protuberance on the surface of the body going very deep, and if cold affects it, one feels in it something like the sting of an ant.
It occurs throughout the body, and most often on the hands; its treatment is to take a tube from a goose feather or a strong eagle feather and place that tube around the mole so that the circle of the tube surrounds it from all sides, then turn your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the mole.
Make it deep into the flesh and the mole will be removed from its root, and if you wish, you can do that with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be constricted and folded so that it is easy for the fingers to grasp and turn it, and if you wish, you can burn the mole as previously mentioned in the burning of warts, in the section on burning.
Chapter three and eighty.
On the instruments used for injection in the ailments of hemorrhoids, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or chinaware, or brass, hammered or cast; and small ones and large ones may be made of this instrument according to those who use it. Those used for treating young boys are small, and those whose hemorrhoids are narrow or painful have very fine syringes. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, in which the cannula is attached, is wide, and it has a barrier where the cannula is attached as you see; its lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, is constricted and very smooth, with two holes at one side near the top, and one hole on the other side.
The size of the opening should be the thickness of the rod or slightly thicker, and the tube into which medicines are put should be made of animal bladder or sheepskin, fashioned like a small tray, with a diameter of one span.
It is pierced with many holes as it rotates, and the distance between each hole should be the thickness of a finger. Then, a tight thread spun from ten or so threads is inserted into those holes, gathering the tube like a tray. When medicine is placed in it, the top of the injector is tightly squeezed between two stones and then injected. This is an image of the tube and the hole:
To inject it, fill the sheepskin or bladder with warmed medicine as much as the patient can tolerate. Be careful that it is not too hot or too cold. Then, have the patient lie on their back and raise their legs upward.
Place a pillow or sheet underneath them, then grease the tip of the injector with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or something similar. Then gently insert it into the rectum. A servant should hold the tube with both hands and squeeze it firmly until all the contents of the injector are emptied into the intestines.
Then remove the injector and have the patient press their legs against the wall. If the medicine urges them to expel it, let them hold on as long as they can. If they sleep with it all night, it will be most effective for softening the stool. If injecting for diarrhea, and they want it to come out quickly, repeat the injection until they are cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, use a very delicate and smooth injector, smoother than those used for children. This is an image of a delicate injector with its skin pulled tight:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the section on the treatment of what resembles burns, and I will mention here its treatment by hand and medicine completely. I say that surgical procedures differ according to what is being operated on and according to the location of the wound.
The things by which surgical procedures are performed are numerous, such as a stone bruise, a cut by a sword or knife, a spear thrust, or a stick or arrow wound and the like of many things.
As for surgical procedures according to the locations on the body, they are like a wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and the like of organs.
I will describe the treatment of some surgical procedures to make them a measure and a law for all surgical procedures, and I will begin with simple head wounds, especially since complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no bone fracture, and if it is from a stone bruise or the like and the skin has only been scratched and the wound is large and I fear the occurrence of a hot swelling for the patient, then hasten and apply a bandage over the wound immediately. Do not delay this and draw out blood for him according to his strength, unless something prevents cupping, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little; let the cupping be on the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against overeating, drinking, and consuming coarse foods. If something prevents cupping, let him reduce his food intake and avoid meat and drinks.  Apply a cotton pad soaked in rose oil alone or with a decoction that has astringent properties to the wound if a hot swelling occurs.
And if you are confident that there will be no hot swelling, then apply a dressing to the wound if it is soft with its blood and has not been changed by the air that I will describe to you shortly. As for if it has been changed by the air, apply some ointments to it.
Until it discharges pus, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and was caused by a sword or similar weapon, and its edges do not meet with sutures, bring them together with stitches as I will describe in stitching abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs loose, and its adherence is only slight, cut it away then treat the wound with medications that promote firm flesh in place of the skin. Instruct the patient to eat strengthening foods such as heads and hooves, etc.
If the skin develops gangrene and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all the gangrenous tissue, cauterize it, then treat the wound. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding and the bleeding does not stop with medication, search for the artery.
If you find it, do not amputate it, but cut it with a scalpel and tie it off. If necessary, and what was mentioned does not work, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy; it suffices to treat them by leaving them covered in their own blood.
Before the air changes it, the formula for the poultice is to take one part frankincense, two parts mastic, and three parts slaked lime or unslaked lime; crush all of them, sift them, and fill the wound with it, binding it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood, then tie it securely on top with sutures and leave it undisturbed as long as there is no swelling, for the medication will not separate from it until it heals. If frankincense and mastic are unavailable, slaked lime alone may be used.
This same procedure may also be done with a linen cloth in many large wounds if it is carefully applied and tightly bound, provided the wounds are soft with blood and have not been changed by air. However, if the air has already somewhat changed them, especially during summertime,
There is no point in placing this poultice, as we have said, since holding some stimulating ointments on it or holding a gruel made from barley flour with water and honey on it will only serve to promote pus, after which it should be treated with all other remedies until it heals.
If a slight fracture occurs in the bone along with the wound, draw it together with plaster. The treatment of head fractures has been mentioned previously, under surgery of the neck. If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with anything that constricts severely, such as lead plaster and alum, for they will injure the nerve and tighten it.
Nothing cold at all should be placed on the wound, for the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs,  requires that any treatment be with soft medicines such as marshmallow washed in fresh water several times and moistened with oil or rose ointment.
If it is also washed with fresh water and all mineral medicaments in this manner, and soft ointments are applied; but if the wound is large, use sutures or bring its edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and an abscess has formed at its bottom where pus has collected, lance it in the lowest part of it. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, amputate it, ligate it, or cauterize it if necessity demands.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx while the vocal cords are intact, bring together the edges of the skin with sutures over the trachea and do not touch the larynx itself but rather put it back into its natural shape, then bind it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it. If there are bone fragments in it, draw them all out or as many as possible. Leave what you cannot remove until the wound becomes healed and dry, making its removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the sawdust we have described. If bone becomes putrid and necrotic in the wound, treat it as follows, which will be mentioned in its chapter, in Surgery of the Chest and Between the Shoulders.
If a wound is from a spear or knife and you see a passage in it, observe whether wind comes out when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a fighting wound. If there is no passage and the wound is fresh with blood, do not immediately put sawdust into it nor tighten it lest the blood becomes trapped within its passage.
For if it flows back to the heart, it will kill the patient. Instead, apply an absorbent ointment to it and dissolve it twice during the day. If you do not have ointment available, place an old piece of cotton in the wound to absorb any moisture that comes out and let the patient sleep on the wound.
Let it absorb what gathers in it. If the wound has been present for three days or more and the patient has not experienced tetanus, a poor heart rhythm, or difficulty breathing, and you see that all his other conditions are good, know that the wound is clean. Treat it with bandages and other remedies until it heals.
But if pus constantly forms and flows, know that it has become putrid. Treat it as follows in its chapter. Let the patient sleep on the wound so that what gathers in it may drain. If the chest or back wound has been present for some time, treat it with the stitching previously mentioned.
If it is large, treat it with sutures; if it is small, treat it with sawdust. If the wound has affected the bone and fragments have broken off from it, explore the wound and immediately remove those fragments if they are clean. If they are not clean, leave them until
If the wound is clean, it facilitates its removal; and as for all other wounds occurring in all other parts of the body, their treatment ruling is the same as we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Fifth.
On abdominal wounds and protrusion of intestines and their suturing.
A rupture that appears on the abdomen may be large, or small, or medium-sized; if it is large, intestine(s) may protrude from it, and introducing them and suturing them will be more difficult and harder.
And the small rupture may also be difficult in another way; it is necessary for its return to be more difficult, and if the intestines are not promptly returned to their place, they will swell and thicken, making it harder to introduce them. Therefore, a medium-sized rupture has become the best because it is not as difficult to return the intestines with it as it is with these two types.
Know that suturing the abdomen is done in four ways; two types of sutures are suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body, and the other two types are specialized and apply to all wounds, but they are more specific for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation, saying that if the wound is small and some intestine protrudes from it, and it is difficult to return it, this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the small rupture as we said, or because the intestine has been swollen before by cold air.
If this is the case, it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or which may have been cooked in it saffron and anise; and let the intestine be moistened with it until the swelling subsides.
A decoction containing astringents may also do this, and it is better than water alone in dissolving swelling; if you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which marshmallow or anise have been cooked; it will thus enter more easily.
If it is impossible for him to return after this treatment, make a small incision in the rupture with the instrument used for making incisions, and the first treatment if successful is better than incision. But one is compelled to make an incision only after necessity and failure of other methods, and this is the image of the instrument:
One of its sides is curved and limited, the other side is not limited, and the thin end is not as thin as a lancet but is somewhat blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small spatula, as you see. And if the wound widens and the intestines enter, they should be returned to their natural shape.
Its proper place, if you are able to do so, is best. But if the rupture is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head; and if it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
And if the rupture is on one side of the abdomen, always make your aim and purpose to raise the side where the surgery is higher than the other side. This should be used in major surgeries and in medium-sized surgeries.
But in small surgeries, place it as you are able. Then have a careful attendant ready who will hold the entire rupture with his hands and bring its lips together; then he will expose a part of it at a time to the one stitching.
This is the description of a common stitch: take one needle or several needles according to the width of the wound, then leave a distance from the edge of the rupture equal to the thickness of a fingernail, and insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. Passing through both edges of the peritoneum under the skin, until it comes out on the other side. This has joined the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four thicknesses. Then tie a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly brought together.
Then leave a thickness the width of a finger and insert another needle, then interlock it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as you need until the entire wound is sutured. Let the needles be of medium thickness.
Because very fine needles quickly cut the flesh and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, it is necessary for them to be of medium thickness, and it should not be inserted into the edge of the skin near the wound.
Lest the flesh quickly cut and the wound open before it heals, and do not space the stitches too far apart lest the wound fail to heal together. Then cut off the ends of the needles so as not to hurt the patient when he sleeps, and make him pads of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed together. This type of suturing with needles is thus most suitable for small surgeries because it may be sufficient to suture them with one or two needles, or so forth.
As for the description of suturing for the second type, it consists in gathering all four margins together by one needle threaded with a moderately fine and strong thread, namely the margins of skin and peritoneum at once, and then when the needle has passed through these four margins, return it from the side where you started.
Let the thread fall locked from above the wound so that the suture is like the stitching of a sack which is drawn tight, and place between each stitch a space the width of a small finger. This type of suturing is known to all people.
With this suture, a surgical incision was closed on a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a knife. The rupture of the surgery was more than a span long, and it protruded from his intestines for about two spans from the middle intestine.
I stitched it up after his intestines were outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he was cured. He lived many years afterward, conducting all his affairs as usual. The physicians had pronounced him incurable.
It is astonishing that I did not treat him with an ointment, for I was in a place where there were no medications at all. I would place clean cotton on the wound twice daily and commit to washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the special types of suturing, I mention it according to the words of Galen, which is to begin the suture from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. When the needle has passed through the skin and into the muscle going straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle, and passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other edge of the omentum. When I had done this, I passed it again into the same edge from the omentum from outside to inside.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on this side and inserted the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through the peritoneum into the edge of the omentum in that direction so that it passes entirely through.
Then I began again from this same side and sutured it along with the edge from the peritoneum on the other side, bringing the needle out through the skin nearby and then inserting it back into that skin, suturing the edge of the peritoneum on the other side along with this edge of the omentum.
I brought it out through the skin on that side and did this again and again until the entire operation was sutured in that manner. The overall description of this suture is that it resembles the furriers' double stitch.
To sew the peritoneum once from one side with a skin edge and to leave it once from the other side so that it drains, and as for the second type of particular stitching which he also mentioned, Galen's words are:
It is to sew it according to the example of how some healers sew it, by bringing together each part to its corresponding opposite, naturally joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the omentum to its other edge, and this is as I have described to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the omentum closest to you from the outside and passed into it alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum, then the needle is returned and passed from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then it is returned again.
And it is passed from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the omentum on the opposite side, and this type of stitching is better than the general easy stitching which was previously mentioned, these are Galen's words textually, and some have said that suturing the abdomen can be done with two needles. This is done by threading one wire into both needles and beginning by inserting one needle from your side and passing it to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side and passing it back to your side according to the stitching of a saddle girth, whether.
Know that if a rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, sewing it is more difficult than in all other parts of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its ruling in treatment is like all other surgeries. That is, if you find the wound soft with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestines and stitch them tightly, then apply a poultice soaked in brine to it.
If the wound has been changed by the air, apply some ointments that you prepare to it so that it suppurates and the threads fall out, then the peritoneum and omentum will join together, and then treat it like all other surgeries until it heals.
If you do not have any medicines available, apply clean cotton wool to the wound as soon as it begins to produce pus and change it twice a day as I have instructed you until it heals. You will not need any further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound may involve the major organs,
You should soak soft cotton in lukewarm oil or rose ointment and place it around the areas between the armpit and groin. If he feels pain or a foul odor in his dressing, this often occurs, so inject him with a warm black astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound into his abdomen, and know that what was from the thick intestines is easier to heal and what was from the thin intestines is more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from surgery at all due to the abundance of large and strong veins, the thinness of its body, and its proximity to the nature of nerves. If what protruded from the wound is pus and you find it soft, return it according to how you returned the intestine.
If a period has passed and it has turned green or black, you should tighten it with a thread above the place from which it has blackened so that bleeding does not occur, for there are veins and arteries in the pus, then cut what is below that ligature.
Make the ends of the thread hanging from below the surgery, outside of it, to make it easy for you to pull and remove them when the pus falls out and the wound drains. When mentioning the wound that appears in the intestine, if a rupture occurs in the intestine and is smallâ€¦
It may be possible to close it in some people, for I once saw a man who had been stabbed in the abdomen with a spear and the wound was to the right of the stomach. I sutured the wound and it became healed, from which feces and gas were expelled.  I treated him as if I did not expect his recovery. I continued to treat him gently until he was healed and the wound closed. When I saw that the wound had closed, I feared that the patient might suffer a mishap within himself, but nothing bad happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. It is true that he eats and drinks, has intercourse, enters the bath, and attends to his service. Some experienced individuals have mentioned that if a small wound appears in the intestine, it should be sutured in this manner.
This is done by taking large ants with heads, then bringing the lips of the wound together and placing one ant on them with its mouth open. When it bites down and tightens its jaws, its head will be severed, and it will adhere firmly and not come undone. Then another ant is placed near the first.
This process is repeated with several ants according to the size of the wound, and then it is replaced and the wound is sutured. Those heads will remain attached in the intestine until it heals and recovers without any harm to the patient. It is also possible to suture the intestine with fine thread.
The method for drawing the Egyptian colocynth, which is the adhesive substance extracted from it, after inserting it into a needle, is as follows: A portion of this thread is taken from the colocynth and drawn out, then a fine, twisted linen thread is tied to its end. This linen thread is then inserted into the needle. The colocynth thread is used to suture the intestine and then returned to the abdominal cavity. This type of suturing with ants and colocynth is a matter of trial and hope.
If the rupture is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy and it will not heal at all.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the treatment of colds and influenza.
Know that any wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic, does not heal and continues to discharge pus incessantly, is generally called a "naasoor" (running sore), and we call it "zaqam" (cold).
It is truly a solid white tumor without pain and has a cavity like that of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some people called it a feather, and sometimes it is moist, constantly discharging pus. Sometimes the moisture may stop, and this moisture can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
And when I discussed surgical procedures, I mentioned that any ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine reasons, one of which is a lack of blood in the body or its poor quality, or because there is solid flesh inside it and on its edges that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or it is full of wounds and dirt, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material it discharges is of poor quality, or the medicine is not suitable for its treatment, or a pestilence has occurred in the town, or there is something specific to the town.
Or it is full of wounds and dirt, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material it discharges is of poor quality, or the medicine is not suitable for its treatment, or a pestilence has occurred in the town, or there is something specific to the town. Just as it appeared in the city of Saragossa, where diseases mature slowly and tumors heal tardily by nature, or because there is one bone or several bones in it; I have clarified all these reasons, their signs, and their treatments with medicine in the section on surgery.
Here I should inform you about the treatment of fistulas and colds that occur through manual work. Know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body, some leading to large veins or arteries, or to nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a complex joint, such as the hand and foot; some even lead near a major organ, and so forth.
Fistulas of this nature are among the difficult diseases that are intractable and do not accept treatment, working on them is a hardship and ignorance. If a fistula does not reach these locations I mentioned, there is hope and expectation of treating it.
And if you desire to ascertain this, take a speculum of copper or iron, if the passage runs straight, and probe it with that. If there is a bend in the passage, probe it with a speculum of thin lead, because lead softens its body.
If it turns aside upon entering towards the bend, and if the passage has many mouths you cannot ascertain them with a speculum, inject into one of its mouths; for the moisture which you inject into it will run towards the other mouths and flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in whatever way you are able, to know whether there is bone or nerve there, or whether the passage is deep or shallow, or whether it is one passage with many mouths; and be insistent on all of this to the extent of your ability, through careful questioning and by feeling for pain when you press your hand upon the spot.
And such are the signs and causes of the tumor; and when you have established all of this truly, then proceed to treatment with confidence, which is that you look to see if...
the passage is outwardly apparent, near or in a sound location, far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein, or any of the locations which I have mentioned to you, then split open the passage as has been previously described and remove from it whatever there is of concretion and corrupt flesh and superfluous flesh.
And remove from its mouth whatever there is of flesh or a wen, and the like; and treat it until it heals. If the passage is deep-seated and runs straight, then you should split it open to the greatest depth possible and cleanse it thoroughly of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use a twisted pledget soaked in caustic medications and insert it to the bottom of the passage which you have reached with the iron; do this repeatedly until that caustic drug consumes all that remains at the bottom of the passage of corruption.
Then force it with ointments that grow flesh soundly until it heals. If it does not heal by that, excise it as described previously. If the cause of the sinus is bone and that has been confirmed, then explore and cleave it as I have told you unless something prevents you from doing so.
If a vein or nerve or principal member is revealed, as we said, then if bone is exposed to you and it has some corruption and blackness, scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will cause it to grow flesh until it heals. If it does not heal and pus persists as it was doing.
Know that you have not reached all of the excision of corruption, so uncover everything on it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort, then force it. If it heals, otherwise know that the corruption is in a distant depth, so uncover everything on it again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is a small bone and you are able to extract it, then extract it with fine forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave anything behind. If any ligaments obstruct you without danger in cutting them, cut those ligaments and the flesh if there is some and free the bones.
If you are unable to extract the bones at that time, apply something that will make it putrefy and leave it for days until what is around those bones of flesh and ligaments decays. Preserve the wound so that the incision you made does not close during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. If those ligaments decay and the bones soften, they will be easy to extract and draw out.
If it is one large bone like the shinbone or thighbone and what has decayed from it is only its surface, scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption is gone, then compress the wound. If a large part of it has decayed and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be excised.
Cut it all the way to where the corruption ends, then dress it so that it heals and joins together. I will tell you about a cold sore that appeared on a man's leg as an example and aid for your treatment. This man was of adolescent age.
About thirty years ago, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause that moved upon him from within the body until the humors reached the leg and there was great swelling and inflammation, and there was no external cause. Then time passed with the error of physicians until the swelling burst open.
Much matter flowed from it and his treatment was mishandled until the leg became ulcerated and there were many openings in it, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them were skilled in surgery.
Until they sent for me, and I saw his leg with the matter flowing from those openings in a great stream and the man had wasted away and his complexion had turned yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of those openings and it led to the bone. Then I explored all the openings.
I found that some of them led to others from all sides of the leg, so I proceeded and made an incision along one of those openings until I exposed some bone. I found it corrupted, eaten away, blackened, putrid, and perforated until the hole reached the marrow. Then I spread out what was revealed to me.
And I was able to remove the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I cut out and spread, and that I had eradicated it. Then I closed the wound with astringent medications for a longer period, but it did not heal together. So I returned and exposed the bone again above the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I spread out what appeared of that corruption as well. Then I tried to close it by force, but it did not close or heal together. So I exposed it again, and I continued to cut away parts of the bone, aiming to close it, but it would not close until I had cut away about a span's length from the bone.
And I extracted it with its brain, then treated it with medications so that it healed quickly and was cured. Indeed, this repetition in his work and incision was necessary due to the patient's weakness, his low tolerance, and my fear for him of death because he would experience, at all times, a bad fainting spell from excessive vomiting, but he was completely cured.
And flesh grew solidly in the place of the bone, and his condition improved in his body, and his strength returned, and he behaved normally, and he was not harmed at all while walking. If a bone that has grown in a part of the body has been broken.
It should be spread in this manner: take a bandage and tighten it at the end of the broken bone, and order someone to pull it upwards; then make another bandage of thicker wool than the first one.
Then tie it to the flesh under the bone and extend its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, while you expose the flesh from the place you want to spread so that the saw does not injure the flesh; place a plank or board firmly under the bone from below.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone; and the sawing should be done slightly above the place of decay so that there is no decay in the cavity of the bone, and it does not appear outwardly to the sense, forcing you to spread it again.
But if the bone is decayed and not protruding, but connected to itself, and the decay is in its middle or part of it; then expose the flesh from all sides, then place a plank underneath and spread it from the first side where the decay is.
And when you have finished spreading it, spread it from the other side, the sawing should be a short distance from the decay as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and spread the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in connection with two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the corruption is in the web of the hand or foot, the matter is very difficult to treat; however, you should pare down the corruption as it appears to you and excise and purify it in any way that you can.
And by any means it straightens to you as long as a vein or nerve does not impede you, and know that the saws and knives for cutting these bones are many according to the position of the bones, their setting, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, their solidity and fragility. Therefore, you should prepare for each type of work a tool suitable for that work.
And know that the operations themselves may guide you to the type of instrument you need if you have long practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for he who is skilled in the craft and has witnessed various types of diseases. May deduce for himself what instruments are suitable for each disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.

Chapter seventy-eighth.
On the amputation of limbs and the spreading of bones.
Limbs may become gangrenous from either an external or internal cause. If you treat that gangrene with medication and the treatment is unsuccessful, and you see the gangrene progressing in the limb and nothing stops it.
Then you should amputate that limb where the gangrene has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or great suffering greater than the loss of the limb. A sign that this has happened is when the limb turns black as if it had been burned by fire.
Or it may become gangrenous after blackening, so that the gangrene spreads to the limb and takes hold of the entire body; hasten to amputate it. Similarly, if the cause of the gangrene is from the sting of certain creatures such as the sea scorpion, viper, centipede, or the like.
If the gangrene or sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not delay until the gangrene spreads to the hand. Similarly, if it has affected the hand, amputate it from the wrist and do not delay until the gangrene spreads up to the forearm.
Similarly, if it has affected the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the gangrene is allowed to progress and you see it spreading toward the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for that would mean death for the patient. Use other treatments as much as possible.
And do the same for a man if gangrene sets in his finger, cut off the finger at one of the joints. And if it sets in the toe, cut off the entire foot; and if it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the gangrene has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and resign the patient to death. The method for cutting off or removing a limb is to tie a ligature below the place where you wish to cut and another ligature above the place.
One attendant extends the lower ligature downward and another attendant extends the upper ligature upward, and you pare away the flesh between the ligatures with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or remove it. Rags of linen should be placed on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct spot and cause the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling, if bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the place quickly or apply some astringents to it, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable ligature and treat it until it heals. I will tell you an example of a man who had this same ailment in his leg, namely that blackness appeared on his leg along with a burning sensation like fire.
This gangrene first appeared in his toe, until it spread to the whole foot. The man amputated his own foot when he saw the gangrene spreading through the limb and felt intense pain and burning. He was cured afterward.
After a long time, this same gangrene appeared in his index finger. He came to me and I tried to repel that evil with the remedies I applied to his hand after cleansing his body, but the gangrene did not relent and continued to spread through the other finger.
Until the gangrene spread to his hand, he urged me to amputate it, but I refused, hoping to repel that evil and fearing for his life if I amputated his hand because the man's strength was failing. When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
I was informed that he was completely cured after cutting off his entire hand. I have only related this story so that it may be of assistance in cases similar to this illness and serve as a guide on which to act.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Abscesses and the Method of Injecting Them with Medicines.
If a tumor occurs in some fleshy organ and persists for a long time, until it collects matter and then bursts or is incised, discharging all the contents, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin upon it, like a rag, has become thin and not excessively corrupted so that it has affected neither bone nor nerve nor ligament; therefore, it deserves to be called an abscess and not a fistula, unless its duration is prolonged until putrefaction affects any of these organs.
Then it is called a fistula or a sinus. Its treatment consists in cutting away all that skin, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its putrefaction.
But if you expect the skin to adhere and it has not reached that degree of putrefaction, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. That is, look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus which issues from it has a foul smell, then inject it with Egyptian medicine.
This is vinegar, oil, honey and ginger in equal parts, mixed together in a vessel and cooked over a fire until the medicine thickens and becomes like honey. Then you take what you need from it and thin it with water and honey and inject it into the abscess. Tie its mouth tightly and leave the medicine in it for two hours.
Then squeeze it out. Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor disappears. If you do not have this medicine, you may inject with ash water; that is, take the ashes of vine wood or oak wood and pour water over them.
Then wash it and inject until you are sure that the abscess has been washed out. If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or lye water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered ginger has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and drink mixed together, since honey has the property of purifying and washing, and drink sticks to the abscess, especially if the drink contains some astringency and dryness. If you do this several times and are sure that the corruption of the abscess has gone, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent syrup, or injecting it with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have established in the treatise on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and cannot accommodate the syringe tube.
Then widen it slightly with iron, or insert a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or cerate ointment into it until it expands. Likewise, if the mouth is also wide, gather its lips with stitches and leave an opening through which the syringe can enter without further ado.
And if the mouth from which pus flows is raised upwards, cut it in the lowest part so that the pus flows downwards, because if the pus accumulates in the depths of the abscess, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the abscess downwards as you wish, then position the limb so that the pus flows easily according to what is available to you and make sure that nothing of the pus remains trapped at all.
As for the medicated substances that are placed on the abscess from the outside and how to apply them, it is to take some of the appropriate medicated ointment and spread it on a linen cloth, making sure the cloth is large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then cut a hole opposite the mouth of the abscess with scissors, slightly wider than the mouth of the abscess. Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth according to the size of the hole and place it over it, spaced away from the mouth of the abscess so that pus can easily flow out. Do not remove the large cloth until later.
For several days, and the one on the mouth of the abscess is the one that you remove at all times so that what has collected in it may be discharged and so that you may know whether what flows from it is much or little, ripe or unripe.
And likewise, examine the place of the abscess itself to see whether its possessor feels pain in it or not and whether there is swelling in it or not. Then if you continue your treatment thus and see that the material which comes out on the mouth of the abscess is slight.
Know that the abscess has adhered or is about to adhere, then solve it on the morrow or in the third day. And when you have solved the wound replace the linen with other and renew the ointment. If after a long time there issues from the abscess thin pus do not despair of its adhering.
For its healing is near because some men are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds and others are contrary to that. If after many days there issues from the abscess unripe pus know that the abscess has not adhered. If the adhering of the abscess is delayed
and its affair is prolonged increase the drying medicaments and let them be medicaments which are moist in their consistence and dry in their power, such as palm oil ointment if to it has been added an excess of an excess of an excess from alum. And the most effective treatment with which it is treated is to take palm oil ointment
and solve it with rose oil and sprinkle upon it old wine that is moderate in its consistence, then knead it with it and use it. You may do a similar act to this if palm oil ointment does not come to your hand. Honey if you cook it until it thickens and use it, or take aloes and myrrh and asafoetida and pound all of them and throw them upon the honey while it is on the fire then spread it upon a linen cloth and bind it upon the abscess or paint with honey which you have cooked until it thickens throw upon it the medicaments and place upon it the linen cloth and bind it.
For such a case, long zarwand may be used, as well as the root of the asphodel, the powder of caraway, and centaury singly or mixed together. They should be crushed, sifted, and sprinkled on the honey which has been described and applied. If the hidden malady persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones or any of the other solid nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment for elephantiasis as previously described.
Chapter Ninety-Eight.
On the Treatment of the Daches and Sick Nails, Removal of Superfluous Fingers, and Splitting Together of Fingers.
The daches is much flesh that grows under the toenail or fingernail, and it may grow in all the fingers. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells and becomes a hot tumor.
Or it may become corrupted and rotten until it eats away at the root of the nail, and perhaps it spoils it all. Corruption may even reach the bone, so that it has a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes broad and its color dark. If you treat it with what we have mentioned in the division and your treatment does not succeed...
...then you should cut away with an iron all the excess that grows from the nail, and then cauterize the wound afterward. Cautery is very beneficial in this case. But if the bone is sound and the nail is also sound...
...and the angle containing the nail had drawn the flesh inward, causing it to be pinched and swollen, then you should place a thin pledget under the angle of the nail that is pinching the flesh and lift it up, and cut away that flesh carefully.
Then place on the remaining flesh some burning, caustic medicines so that it all disappears, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if corruption has affected the bone, you should cut away that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is a corrupted bone at all.
If you see suppuration and corruption advancing in the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or contusion and there is severe pain therein, it is necessary to lance the affected area first.
Then split the nail with a sharp, slanting knife from above to below and be careful not to reach the flesh under the nail with the incision, for you will thereby cause the affected area severe pain and lead to the growth of excess flesh in that place. Then treat the area with something that relieves pain.
As for the extra finger that occurs in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in some parts of it, or it may even have a nail. Its growth may occur at the base of the joint of one of the fingers.
Or it may grow in one of the phalanges of the finger. Those that grow in some phalanges do not move, and those that grow at a finger joint may move. Any fleshy part should be easily cut off; cut it at its base with a wide knife.
As for those that grow at the base of a joint, their treatment is difficult; refrain from cutting them off. As for those that grow on a finger in one of the phalanges, its flesh should first be cut in a circular fashion down to the bone, then the bone should be spread with one of the saws suitable for that purpose.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between the fingers, it is common and may be congenital or result from a wound or burn. It should be split.
So that the fingers return to their natural state, then place a wick or a thin, shredded rag soaked in rose oil between them so that they do not adhere quickly and separate them, or put a thin lead plate between them until the area heals properly.
And if some of the fingers are joined to the palm, cut that union according to what seems fitting and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On Cutting Arteries.
Arteries are twisted, thick veins filled with black phlegm that occur in most of the body's organs, and they are most common in the legs, especially in the thighs and calves of carriers and laborers. First, one should use strong repeated shaking of the body to dislodge the black humor.
Then, let the patient be bled from his basilic vein. The treatment with iron is of two kinds: one is to cut and extract the black blood, and the other is to cauterize the artery and remove it entirely. As for cutting it, this is done as follows: first, soak the leg in hot water.
Until the thick, murky blood dissolves, then bind the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage. Then cut the artery in one place or two or three wide incisions.
Then, draw out the black blood with your hand from below the leg to above and from above to below until you have extracted the amount of blood that you deem sufficient or what the patient can tolerate, then bandage it and order him to avoid foods that produce black bile. Repeat the vomiting and bleeding.
When the veins fill up and this harms the patient, but as for cauterizing it, do this: shave the patient's leg if it has a lot of hair, then immerse him in a bath and soak his leg in hot water until it reddens and the artery swells or let him engage in strenuous exercise.
If a bath is unavailable to heat the limb, then cut the skin opposite the artery lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or below it near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and remove the artery from all sides until it is exposed to touch.
When it appears, you will see it reddish-purple. And when freed from the skin, it will also appear like a tendon. Then insert a ligature underneath it until it rises and comes out of the skin. Hang it with a blind, smooth snare, then make another incision near that incision about three fingerbreadths long.
Then flay the skin off the vein until it appears, then raise it with a ligature as you did before and hang it with another snare as you did at first. Then make another incision or many incisions if needed.
Then draw it and cut it at the end of the last incision at the heel, then pull it and draw it until it comes out of the second incision. Then pull it up to the incision above and do that until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all, until it all comes out, then cut it off.
If it does not yield to pulling and drawing, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread and tie it, pull it, insert a ligature underneath it and twist it with your hand in every direction until it comes out. Be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult to draw it out.
And a complication will befall the patient from it. And if you draw it all out, place wool soaked in syrup and rose oil or oil on the site of the operations and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is convoluted and has twists in directions...
And was not on a straight line as we mentioned, it should be incised at each side of its convolutions and twists and then hung with snares until it is all drawn out. Be careful when incising it not to cut or injure the vein, for it will be difficult to draw it out if you do so, and preserve your effort.
A diagram of the instrument used to incise the varicose vein.
Image of the Blind Syringe.
It does not have a guard like other syringes, nor is its tip sharp so as not to injure the vein. It is thick and curved, smooth because if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its sharpness.  Rather, it should have the advantage of thickness as we have said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
On cutting a vein in the arm.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries such as the Hejaz and Arab lands, and in hot, barren countries with little vegetation, and it may arise in other parts of the body besides the legs. It is produced by putrefaction that occurs under the skin.
It also occurs within living bodies, worms, gourd bugs, and worms that develop between the skin and flesh. A sign of the beginning of this vein is a severe inflammation in the leg, then the area swells, and then the vein begins to emerge from that swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, so when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, and tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything comes out of it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows very long, cut off some of it and wrap the rest, and do not cut it from its origin before it all comes out because if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a boil and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be manipulated and gently pulled until it comes out entirely, leaving nothing of it in the body. From this vein, it may emerge in some people to a length of five spans and ten.
It has been reported to me that it emerged from a man to the length of twenty cubits. If it breaks off while you are treating it, insert a bougie into its hole and press it with a long stick against the body so that all its contents are emptied out. Then try to clean the area with medications for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment of tumors, for this vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the knee joint or in the leg itself, causing it to have many mouths from which a branch comes out. Treat it as we mentioned in the division and what preceded.
The second ninety chapter.
In the incision for worms generated under the skin, and it is called cow disease.
This disease is called cow disease in some of our countries because it often occurs in cows, and it is a single small worm that generates between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body ascending and descending.
It becomes apparent to the senses by its crawling from one organ to another until it pierces wherever it pierced in the skin at a spot and comes out, forming it from some putrefaction of humors as it occurs with worms and animals and pumpkin seeds in the abdomen.
And what is expected from its harm is that if it crawls in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open up in it and come out, thus ruining the eye. This occurs frequently. If you wish to treat it and extract it, it must be done when it is crawling and appears to the senses.
You should tighten what is above it and below it with a strong ligature, then make an incision on it and extract it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you do not find it, apply heat to the spot with fire until you burn it. As we said, the greatest harm expected from it is the corruption of the eye.
If you see that it has become in the head near the eye, tighten under it on the forehead with a strong ligature, then make an incision on it and extract it. The patient should take care to purify his body with purgative medicines for foul humors and abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
The third ninety chapter.
On incision for the malady known as nafar.
This disease which is called in our country nafar is a pain that appears in some organs and then transfers from one organ to another, and I have seen it as I will describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some remote regions, and upon examining her arm, I observed a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After an hour, I saw that swelling creeping upwards along the vein like a worm, ascending rapidly as fast as mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain departs from that location and settles in the elbow. Then I waited an hour and it ran throughout the rest of her body until it reached her other arm. She then told me that it circles her entire body as she had observed.
I was amazed by the speed with which it transferred from one organ to another. I had never seen this disease before with my own eyes in this manner, although I had heard of people experiencing pain that moved from one organ to another.  But I had never witnessed it firsthand as I did in this woman.
I surmised that the reason for this was because the woman was from the desert, with a lean physique and visible veins, which allowed this moving sensation to be perceptible. It would not appear in this same way in those who are well-nourished and have moist, delicate physiques with concealed veins.
Therefore, if you wish to treat it and the sufferer feels pain, and if it appears to you as we have described, bind tightly above and below it and make an incision so that the trapped air is released and the area is cleaned.
However, if you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by rubbing the body and using remedies that expel wind and disperse it, such as loveage seeds, spikenard, and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-Fourth.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and according to the places in which they are located in the body. As for their difference according to their types, some are large and some are small; some are hollow glass arrows and some are solid glass arrows; and some have three corners and some have four corners.
Some have tongues and some have splinters. As for those that are located according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two types: either the organs are principal hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. So when an arrow falls into one of these organs and the signs of death which I will describe to you appear, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it for death will befall its owner in most cases. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not been concealed in the depths of the organ, extract it and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain if an arrow strikes it and pierces the bone and injures the dura mater which is on top of the brain, is that he will suffer from severe headache, tinnitus, and dizziness.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not been concealed in the depths of the organ, extract it and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain if an arrow strikes it and pierces the bone and injures the dura mater which is on top of the brain, is that he will suffer from severe headache, tinnitus, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, spasms, confusion of mind, vomiting sometimes and perhaps blood comes out of the nostrils or ears; perhaps speech ceases and the voice is gone; and a white moisture like porridge comes out from the wound's location and flows from it like meat broth.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not come out. As for the signs of an arrow that has struck the heart, which is close to the left breast, and he feels as though it has been embedded in something solid rather than something empty.
Perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound; followed by coldness of the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Know that death is inevitable. And a sign of an arrow that has injured the lung is the exit of foamy blood from the wound.
And the vessels following the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes with a high breath, and requests to inhale cold air. If the arrow has struck the diaphragm in the chest, it will be close to the small ribs and the breathing will be great with severe pain.
And he sighs and all the limbs of his shoulders move. If the arrow has struck the liver, severe pain will follow and blood resembling the liver in redness will come out from the wound. And if the arrow has struck the stomach, perhaps something undigested will come out from the wound along with food.
Its matter is apparent, and if the arrow has struck the abdomen and lodged there, and something of feces comes out from the wound or pus or a gut that has been pierced, then there is no hope for his treatment or for removing the arrow. If the arrow has struck the bladder and urine comes out.
And something has protruded from it outward, and the pain has intensified upon the patient, then know that he is lost. As for the rest of the organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, upper arm, vertebrae of the back, clavicle, thigh, leg, and anything similar from those organs, they have been spared in most cases.
Provided the arrow has not struck a vein or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned, I will tell you some of what I have seen regarding these arrows so that you may deduce treatment from it. This is because an arrow had struck a man in the cartilage of his eye at the base of the nose.
I removed it for him from the other side, under the cartilage of the ear, and he recovered and nothing bad happened to his eye. I also removed another arrow for a Jew that had struck him in the cartilage of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow was concealed and I had only reached its small tip that was stuck in the wood.
It was a large arrow, one of the hard composite arrows with a square iron head that was smooth and had no ears. The Jew recovered and nothing bad happened to his eye. I also removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian man and the arrow was Arabic.
He was the one who had two ears cut, and between his jugular veins, and he had plunged into his throat, I softened it until I took it out, so the Christian recovered and was healed, and I took out another arrow for a man who had placed it in his belly, and we thought he would die from it.
When thirty days or so had passed and nothing of his condition had changed, I cut open the arrow, worked around it and took it out; he recovered and no ill befell him. And I saw a man with an arrow embedded in his back, the wound having closed over it.
Seven years later, the arrow came out at the base of his thigh, and I saw a woman with an arrow in her belly, the wound having closed over it; the arrow remained and nothing changed in her condition, nor did she find it harmful in any of her natural actions.
And I saw another man with an arrow in his face, the wound having closed over it; he remained and did not find it very painful. There are many such cases, and I took out an arrow for one of the Sultan's leaders, which had been lodged in the middle of his nose, slightly inclined to the right.
The arrow was completely embedded, and I was called to treat him three days after the arrow had been lodged. I found the wound of the arrow very narrow, so I widened it with a fine probe; he did not feel it, but experienced a twinge and pain under his right ear from the incision on the right side.
I thought that twinge might be from the tip of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a bandage that had drawing power and ripeness, hoping that the area would swell and a mark of the arrow would appear. I then cut him open, but nothing happened in that area to indicate that the arrow had reached it.
I continued with the bandage for many days, but nothing happened. The wound closed during that time and the patient remained miserable from having it removed for a period until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me about it. I then put a sharp caustic medicine on the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, feeling the point of the fine arrow stuck in the wood, then I further widened the wound with that sharp medicine until the point of the arrow appeared clearly to us. And time passed with it for a period of about four months.
Then when the wound widened and I was able to enter the forceps into it, I drew and moved it, but it did not respond to coming out. So I continued to soften it and try various instruments on it until one day I seized it with firm forceps as will be shown in the figure at the end of the chapter.
And physicians used to rule that the cartilage of his nose could not be joined, but I joined it and the wound healed, and the patient recovered completely without any pain whatsoever, and I tell you how some arrows are removed so that this may serve as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
For the details of this craft cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book, but rather the skillful craftsman measures the little against the much and what is present against what is absent and deduces new work and new instruments when confronted with strange emergencies.
If we descend from this craft, I say that arrows are only removed from the organs in which they are lodged in two ways: either by drawing them from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction. And those that come out from where they entered.
Either the arrow is prominent in a place of flesh, so it is drawn and pulled out. If it does not respond to coming out at the time it occurred, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it rots, making it easier to pull and remove. The same applies if it is lodged in bone.
If it still does not respond to coming out, leave it for days as well and try drawing and moving it every day, for it will come out. If it still does not respond after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine awl until it widens for the arrow, then draw and pull it out.
If the arrow that has penetrated is in the bone of the head and has been buried deep into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms which I mentioned to you have appeared from the wound, then refrain from drawing out the arrow and leave it until his condition becomes clear after several days.
