Then proceed to draw out the arrow, and if it is deeply embedded and does not yield to your pull, then use the trephine around the arrow as I have described to you, and then treat the site until it heals. But if the arrow has become hidden in some part of the body and is out of reach, then search for it with a probe, and if you feel it, draw it out with some instrument suitable for the purpose. But if you are unable to do so because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow's penetration, and there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein in that location
then make an incision over it to widen the wound so that you can seize the arrow and draw it out. And if it has two flanges by which to grasp it, then free the flesh adhering to them from every side by any means possible. If you are unable to free the flesh in breaking the flanges and splitting them, continue until you succeed.
And if you attempt to extract the arrow in any location, use the twist of your hand with all its strength in every direction until you free it, and exercise the greatest gentleness lest the arrow should break and make it difficult for you to draw it out. If at the time you are unable to do so,
leave it for several days until the flesh around it decays, and then return to it; for it will then be easier to extract. If hemorrhage should interfere with you, use what we have mentioned in its chapter as treatment, and take every precaution against cutting a vein or nerve or tendon. Use stratagem.
In any case, you can remove the arrow, let it be gently and carefully and firmly as I have described to you, and you should use when drawing out the arrow to make the patient in the same position he was in when the arrow struck him, for this is best.
If you cannot do this, use any form that is possible, but the arrow which comes out from the opposite side may have something protruding outwards, or you may find the point of the arrow by touch near the skin's surface and see it emerging.
Then make an incision upon it, let the incision be of such size that the forceps can enter, then draw it out, for it will easily come out; if it is caught in a bone, twist your hand in rotation until the arrow strikes against the bone and widens itself, then draw it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and return to it until it comes out; if the arrow's shaft is still in, push it in with force, but if the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use pressure, insert a hollow instrument into it so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow and then push it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that fits into that cavity, for the arrow will easily come out this way; if the arrow is poisoned, you should cut out all the flesh where the poison has entered, if possible, and then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is in the chest or belly, bladder or side and it is near what can be felt with a probe, and if you are able to make an incision upon it, do so, and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then stitch the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals; image of forceps used to draw out arrows:
Its tips are shaped like a bird's beak, made as if they were a file, so that when it grasps the arrow or something else, it does not let go;  and they can be made in large, small and medium sizes all according to the size of the arrow, and the width or narrowness of the wound.
Image of the hollow bullet.
Image of the solid bullet.
This is a solid-pointed end like an awl to facilitate its entry into the hollow arrow and propel it.
Chapter Ninety-Fifth
On Phlebotomy.
The veins that are commonly bled in the body are thirty veins, of which sixteen are in the head: the two temporal veins behind the ears known as the hssees and the two arteries in the temples that are visible, and the two veins in the eye sockets known as the viewers.
And the vein that stands in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the end of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, these veins are known as the jaharks, and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are bled in the arm and hand, there are five veins: one of them is the cephalic vein, which is on the ulnar side, and it is commonly called the head vein, and the basilic vein, which is the middle vein, composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and it is commonly called the body vein.
And the basilic, which is located on the radial side, is also called the axillary vein, and it is commonly called the abdominal vein, and the brachial artery, which is located on the elbow, is the one that sticks to it and appears above the thumb clearly, and the asylem.
And it is the vein between the little toe and the next toe to it, has two branches, and in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is under the knee from the ulnar side, and the second is the saphenous, located at the heel from the radial side, and the vein of women, located at the heel from the ulnar side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them, but as for the two veins behind the ears, stopping them is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, ringworm, and foul chronic ulcers of the head, and I will describe how to stop them.
The method is to shave the patient's head and rub the back of it in the place of the veins with a rough cloth until it is well rubbed, then constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the veins appear behind the ears in the lower parts of the head. Then search for them with your finger and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then take a sharp scalpel, the one known as a 'nasl,' and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin in a severed piece. The length of the cut should be equal to two joined fingers.
Or about that, and let out the amount of blood you want, then tie them with bandages and leave them until they heal. I have already mentioned cutting and cauterizing them at the beginning of this book. As for the two arteries in the temples, stopping them is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent ophthalmia, and acute copious discharge directed to the eyes.
And the method of stopping them is as follows: constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the arteries become clearly visible and their pulse is evident beneath your finger. Mark them with ink at that point, then lift the skin from above the artery upwards with your index finger.
Insert the 'nasl' scalpel from below and lift the artery upwards, then sever it as you did with the other veins. Let out the amount of blood you need, then release the constriction on the patient's neck and place your finger on the artery for an hour.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it, and tighten it firmly from above, leaving it until it heals. Their mention, severing, and ligation have already been mentioned in the beginning of the book. As for bleeding the forehead vein, it is beneficial after bleeding the temporal vein for chronic facial conditions such as tinea, ulcers, and erysipelas.
How to bleed it, as I will describe to you:  You strangle the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears, then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:.
You place the pointed fork that is at the head of the axe on the same vein and strike it from above with a comb or something similar, and leave the blood to flow in the amount you desire, then you loosen the patient's strangulation and bind him until he heals.
It may also be opened with a wide spoon, but it should not have a sharp end like other spoons, but rather a slightly wide end. You open it on the bias because the bone is nearby and the spoon might break if it were thin.
As for the two veins in the temples, they are beneficial for eye conditions such as scabies, redness, ophthalmia, and facial diseases. As for how to bleed them: you have the patient tighten his neck with a turban, then bleed them while standing above his head; let the bleeding be on a slight diagonal with a small spoon.
Since the area is not fleshy, if the spoon has a thin end, it may break. Then you send the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then loosen them. As for bleeding the nose vein, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headaches, and facial diseases such as red scabies that appear on the nose, especially if they are chronic. To bleed it: you have the patient tighten his neck, then hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long spoon.
And insert it into the middle of the nose itself between the cartilage, for the vein does not appear there to the sense; then blood will issue from it. You should press your hand a little with the probe and draw from it the amount of blood you need, then tie it for a night, for it will harden quickly.
As for the veins, their benefit is in cutting them off for shortness of breath and the beginning of leprosy and black diseases that appear on the surface of the skin such as black vitiligo, ringworm, and foul sores. The way to cut them is to have the patient tie them under his neck with a bandage and the maker stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you cut the vein lengthwise, making a wide incision, then draw from it a moderate amount of blood or as much as you deem necessary. Then do the same to the other vein and untie the bandage.
Tie the veins moderately so that the patient does not choke, and leave them until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the jugular veins, their benefit is in cutting them after cupping the head, for it is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, gum disease, foul sores, chapped lips, and foul sores.
Those that are in the nose and around it. The way to cut them is to have the patient sit facing you, tie his neck with a turban, then turn back his lips and look for the two veins, one of which you see on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and identify them by their blackness.
You will also find thin, black veins around them, so cut them off completely. If it seems unclear to you and you do not know which ones they are, aim to cut the largest and clearest ones; similarly, do the same with the two veins that are on the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, their benefit is in cutting them after cupping the head for ulcers in the throat from diseases of the mouth and throat.  The way to cut them is to have the patient sit in front of you in the sunlight, raise his tongue, and look under it on one side for a vein and on the other side for a vein, both black in color.
Describe them in detail and memorize them, do not pay attention to cutting them for there are arteries beneath them; bleeding may occur from these arteries. As for the three veins that are incised at the elbow, they are the ones usually incised in people generally. Incision of these veins can be done in two ways.
Either by inserting a wide lancet or an olive-shaped one to the skin, or by making an incision with a scalpel, which is called "nashal" [and this is its picture], picture of the wide lancet.
It is wide as you see so it is suitable for opening the hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins that contain thick blood; and this is a picture of the olive-shaped lancet.
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for phlebotomizing the thin veins that contain clear, yellowish blood; and this is a picture of the nashal lancet.
This nashal, which is suitable for incision, comes in various widths and thinness according to the width and narrowness of the veins. It can also be distinguished from others and is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, phlebotomizing it is beneficial because it draws blood from ailments that are located below the throat and neck, extending to the chest and abdomen. The phlebotomist should be cautious when phlebotomizing it and make sure it is on the neck; for there is an artery beneath it. If he errs and inserts the lancet further, he may cut that artery.
This will cause bleeding, so it should not be phlebotomized with a lancet for insertion, but rather incised with the nashal. If the basilica vein does not appear clearly, it should be avoided and another one chosen instead, or assistance should be requested from someone else.
Or phlebotomize the brachial artery in its place, for it is visible and incised with the nashal lancet as we mentioned. If you wish to phlebotomize the basilica vein specifically, before stretching the arm, you should feel the spot until you recognize the pulse location, then mark it with ink.
Then bind the arm and make a slanting incision in the vein with a scalpel, as we have said, taking care that the blow falls at a distance from the artery. And when you see puffing in the place which you marked with ink upon tightening the ligature, know that this puffing is the swelling of the artery; avoid it.
If you see the blood spurt during venesection as a boy's urine spurts and if the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood; then hasten to put your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger: if the blood stops, which it often does, bind the arm tightly.
Leave it and warn the patient not to neglect it, let him keep his neck still and not move it for several days until he is healed. If the bleeding does not stop and overpowers you and there is no medicine available to you at that time, amputate the artery if it appears to you; its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, and take one half; tighten it over the vein with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise treat it as previously mentioned, by applying styptic dressings to stop the hemorrhage. Cutting off his blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the small size of the wound and the ligature's ability to control it in the arm, inform him of this. As for the ulnar vein, its usefulness lies in drawing blood from the upper head and lower body since it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic as we have said.
The person who performs the venesection should be careful not to cut below the vein, for there is a nerve underneath. If he inserts the scalpel too deeply and injures the nerve, numbness will occur making it difficult to heal, and it may never heal at all. This nerve often presents itself to sensation.
If a vein is hidden in some people and thin, not clearly visible, it should be cut with a scalpel, avoiding the nerves as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, cut the vein lengthwise; as for the temporal vein, there is benefit to cutting it because it draws blood from the head.
It is useful for eye diseases, and in this vein especially if you wish to dissect it; with an olive scalpel or a wide rhinological scalpel because it is the safest of all veins as there is neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when phlebotomizing, you should avoid the muscle head with the scalpel and aim for the soft spot. It does no harm if it is not hit on the first attempt to repeat the phlebotomy several times, unless it may swell in some people if it is not hit on the first attempt.
But that swelling does not harm it; as for the method of phlebotomy and its effects and what should precede its correction, the first thing you should know is that phlebotomy is used either to preserve health only.
Or it is used for diseases. Phlebotomy used to preserve health and maintain it, and to prevent diseases from occurring should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the temporal, ulnar, and basilic, and phlebotomy should be in the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy should be performed on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since dawn. As for phlebotomy used for diseases, it does not have a limited time but whenever necessity and need dictate, whether day or night.
And in every hour and every time. Children should not be phlebotomized until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years of age be phlebotomized. If someone decides to undergo phlebotomy for any reason.
His intestines should be cleansed before bloodletting by a gentle enema if there is much retained slime, lest it be drawn into the veins during bloodletting from the intestine causing an excess of putrefaction which would harm the principal organs. The intoxicated, the drunken and the one under the influence of narcotics should not be subjected to bloodletting until that state has passed.
Bloodletting should also be avoided after a bowel movement, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, exertion, exercise, staying up late and fasting, and everything that depletes physical or mental strength. Then, consider thinning the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
with foods, drinks and medicines if possible, then he should enter a bath if there is no contraindication or engage in some exercise to thin the blood.  Bloodletting should be done in the early afternoon as we have said, and one should aim to clear their chest that day of all bad mental states.
Such as worries, anger and fear, and from all physical conditions such as excessive fatigue, exertion, and sexual intercourse and the like. Prepare his surroundings with things that people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes, incense, and amusements, according to each person's ability.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the patient sits. Then he extends his arm and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with the tourniquet and twists it two or three times, the tightening should be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightening will prevent blood flow, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent blood flow. Then after tightening, the patient should rub their hands together briskly.
So that the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then places the index finger of his left hand on the same vein below the place where he wants to make the incision, so that the vein does not move away from the blow. For some veins are like a cord, they retreat when bled, and others are full of air, so when you place the lancet upon them they sink beneath it and deceive the phlebotomist, who does not open the vein; and if he opens it, the opening will be narrow.
Therefore the phlebotomist should be certain and proceed carefully in all these matters, then he should lower the lancet; if the vein opens at that time, well and good, otherwise he should try again a little below or above that spot quickly, unless the place becomes swollen.
If there is swelling or the patient becomes frightened, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it may bring on a hot boil, and he should not enter the bath; then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes. If the lancet was inserted and the opening was small and the blood flowed thinly,
and you feared that the quantity of blood you desired would not come out, reinsert the lancet into the same opening carefully, keeping it straight, and enlarge the opening a little; do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place swells up at a small opening.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat the procedure at all, for it will not help; put something of oil of roses on it, for it will facilitate the flow of blood and is better in this place than oil itself or any other unguents.
Use oil of roses in all your phlebotomies of the veins when the flow of blood is difficult; also the faruq electuary and the shukhsnayay can do this if one of them is placed upon the spot, for it will thin and dissolve the blood if it is thick.
If a large boil should occur in the place of phlebotomy, which often happens, especially to one who has not been bled before or whose vein was opened small, then hasten and put a sponge soaked in warm salt water upon the boil and bind it for an hour, for it will dissolve.
And it should be done after all the blood has come out from the vein itself or from another vein; if something black or green remains in the place after a few days, it does not matter. If you wish, apply to it something of aloes and myrrh dissolved.
Or something of fig juice or the like, a tumor and swelling often occur when cupping the basilic vein; so place your hand upon it, and if you find it softens when you press upon it, then that swelling is bad, so beware of putting anything of what we mentioned upon it.
For it may bleed from an artery, but compress it with what constricts so that the place hardens and then treat it with all treatment until it heals. You should draw out blood from each person according to their strength and what color predominates in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow out until it becomes red; and if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins; and if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness goes away.  Whoever is full and strong and needs to have blood drawn all at once...
...should widen the incision of the vein and let the lancet be wide; but for whoever is weak, it should be the opposite. And blood should be drawn in stages and the puncture should be narrow. The best thing to use for venesection is a sharp, well-tempered lancet that cuts, not punctures.
This type of venesection is safe from hemorrhage and severing a nerve, it is better and safer than venesection by crosswise and lengthwise incision. Whoever is accustomed to fainting during venesection should be given before the procedure some bread soaked in pomegranate juice or syrup.
If it is sharpened and blood has been drawn for him three or four times, if he has a cold temperament, let him take before venesection bread soaked in syrup of the quince, or in honeyed syrup sweetened with violets, or in fragrant pleasant syrup.
If syncope occurs during bloodletting and its cause is excessive blood loss, he should be given broth and thin wind-expelling drinks, and perfumed with musk, rubbed on his chest with it, and all that we mentioned in the division in the chapter on syncope should be used.
As for whoever wants to lighten his arm and loosen his blood again, it is necessary for whoever bled him to vomit a lot and whose strength is weak to let out the blood little by little according to his strength on consecutive days.
As for whoever wants to lighten his arm and loosen his blood again, and whose body is strong, let him do so after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting. As for whoever wants to attract blood from his body to the opposite side to which it leaned, it should be done for him on the second or third day.
As for whoever has a lot of blood in his body, which has heated up and become intense and caused fever, it is necessary that all the blood be drawn out at once, and a large amount be drawn out, and the incision be widened until syncope occurs, after you have ensured that all the conditions for bloodletting are met. And place your hand on his pulse while the blood is flowing so that death does not occur at the time of syncope, for this often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs; and it should not be done if you want to lighten the arm and loosen the blood again.
And if the mouth of the vein has closed and the blood is difficult to extract, do not pinch it forcefully or twist it hard, for that is very bad indeed. Rather, either leave it until you bleed him again, or remove with the lancet blade what has congealed in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply something of salt dissolved in water to it, or apply some of the sovereign remedy or the pulverized rue, and pinch it gently until the blood comes out. If the vein has become swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides, for if necessity requires the release of blood again, it must be done either by making an incision above that location or by making an incision in the other arm or vein.
As for bleeding the arm tendon, it is bled instead of the ulnar and basilic veins if they are not present or hidden because it is composed of them, and the way to bleed it is for the patient to put his hand in hot water until the arm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then tie a bandage above it with moderate tightness.
Then bleed the vein slightly sideways, neither wide nor long, and let the opening be wide, and make your incision above the wrist joint a little, and if the blood does not flow, put the hand back in the bowl of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches the desired amount, and if you are in summer days, you may dispense with putting the hand back in hot water, and it is most likely that the blood will flow into the hot water during winter time, and bleeding this vein is safer than all other veins. Because there is no underlying vein or nerve, and as for bleeding the basilic from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the way to bleed it is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no underlying vein or nerve, and as for bleeding the basilic from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the way to bleed it is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch, then incise it slightly sideways, and even if you cut it completely, it will not harm it, be careful not to insert your hand deeply with the scalpel, for underneath it is the nerve of the fingers, and the location is bare of flesh, then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow freely, for if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will coagulate in the vein opening and stop flowing. If you have extracted the desired amount of blood, apply ointment and salt to the vein so that it does not heal quickly.
And so it should be done with every narrow vein, and the benefit of stopping it from the left hand is that it is beneficial for splenic diseases, and you do the same in stopping it as you did in the second [vein], and as for stopping the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower part of the body such as uterine diseases.
And retention of menstruation, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and the like of these diseases, and the way to stop it is for him to put his foot in hot water and rub it until the veins flow, then tie it above the ankle joint with a bandage soaked in sweat where the vein is visible near the heel towards the thumb.
And many branches branch off from it on the front of the leg, so stop it in the widest branch or at the heel where they join, for this is better and safer, and if you stop it on the front of the leg, be careful of the nerves underneath it on the front of the leg.
And make your incision as if you were going to amputate it, and let the lancet be sharp. If the blood does not flow, put his foot back in hot water and let the blood run into it until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt.
Then repeat the incision a little higher, for that place is safe and there is no danger to be feared from it if you are careful of the nerve as we said, and so do you with the saphenous vein on the other leg. As for the vein of Venus, its location is, as we have said, at the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of stopping it for pain in the hip if it is due to hot blood, and how to stop it is for the patient to enter a bath and quickly tie his leg from the hip down to above the heel with four fingers using a thin long bandage. For it will not appear otherwise.
Then, if it appears, stop it in whichever position you find it, either by incision, which is better, or by cutting it off completely or making a slit. For its location is safe and it is very subtle in most people. If you cannot find it
And if it does not appear to the sense, then cut some of its fibers which are those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little toe and the one next to it, preserving the nerves and sending out the amount of blood you desire.
Then loosen the constriction and place cotton on the site of the phlebotomy and bind the area, for it will soon heal.
Chapter Sixty-Sixth.
On Cupping and How to Use It.
Cupping instruments may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass. Cupping is performed in two ways: one is cupping with a restriction and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without a restriction.
This type of cupping without restriction is also done in two ways.
There is cupping at the two hollows of the neck on both sides, cupping under the chin below the lower jaw, and cupping at the shoulders. There is also cupping at the coccyx, cupping at the elbows, which are in the middle of the arms, cupping at the legs, and cupping at the wrists.
Cupping draws blood from the fine, delicate veins scattered throughout the flesh. Therefore, it does not weaken the body as much as phlebotomy. Cupping with fire or without fire should not be used for any diseases characterized by fullness until the whole body has been emptied.
If cupping is necessary due to illness or habit, we use it at any time: at the beginning, middle, and end of the month, and at any other time as well.
And some people, when blood increases in them to the point where they need it to be removed by cupping, experience heaviness and headache in their head. And others experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And others experience itching in their face and sides, darkness, and blackness under their eyes. And some itch at the place of their cupping.
And some laugh excessively, and others taste blood in their mouth and their gums swell and they spit up blood, and some sleep excessively, and some see blood, redness, fighting, and wounds in their dreams, and things similar to that. So whenever we see something like that...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed from noon. As for the benefit of puncturing cupping, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water flowing into the eyes.
But it should be done after the whole body has been evacuated, and this cupping may be a substitute for phlebotomy from the head. It is warned that it should not be used by someone who has a cold brain or a cold, as it will greatly harm them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly, nor by those who have cold illnesses in their head, and whoever indulges in it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should be ordered to lower his hand with the cupping instrument a little downwards, fearing the development of forgetfulness. As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for phlebotomy from the ulnar artery and phlebotomy from the basilic vein.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, headache due to the respiratory system, coughing, and fullness. Cupping on the shoulder should be raised a little because if it is lowered too much, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from headaches, ophthalmia, migraines, choking, and pain in the roots of the teeth. It is a substitute for phlebotomy from the basilic vein. You should order the cupper not to deepen his hand with the instrument so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth and corruption of the gums and other diseases that are in the mouth, and it may replace cupping of the temples which are on the lips. As for cupping of the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the abdomen, it is beneficial for what benefits cupping of the three veins: basilic, cephalic, and saphenous because it draws blood from all those thin veins that are in the flesh and draws those thin veins from thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three thickest veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to overdo the suction because the place is bare of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping that is applied on the sacrum, it benefits from hemorrhoids in the anus and ulcers below.
The cupper should be instructed that the cupping jar should be large and made of copper because the place needs strong suction and a glass cupping jar may break, and it requires a lot of suction. As for cupping the legs, it reduces fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the whole body and benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder, and brings on menstruation, and benefits from pimples and boils and replaces cupping of the safenous veins and cephalic veins except that it weakens the body greatly and fainting occurs in most people, and cupping of the cephalic veins its benefit is close to the benefit of cupping the legs.
How to place the cupping jars: first, the cupping jar is placed empty and sucked moderately; the cupping is not left on for a long time but is placed quickly and removed quickly so that the humors are drawn into the area evenly. This is repeated continuously until the area becomes red and opens.
Redness of the blood then appeared, at which point it should be cauterized and sucking should be resumed gradually. Then look at the condition of the bodies; if a person has soft flesh with loose pores, only one cautery should be applied so that the place does not become ulcerated.
Order the barber to widen and deepen the cautery a little, and adjust the suction gently with light movement. If there is thickness in the blood, it should be cauterized twice; the first time to open a way for the thin blood and its water, and the second time to ensure the extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very dark, cauterization should be repeated a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a little blood, one cautery is sufficient; if we want to extract a lot of blood, we cauterize more; and if we think the blood is thick.
It should be cauterized deeply, the moderate limit of the cautery being the depth of the skin only; ointments should not be used when applying the cupping glasses, nor water, and what the phlebotomist warns against. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, yellowish, and with tight pores.
Their cupping glass sites should be greased with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments. If it is summertime, like the ointment of henna, violet ointment, sweet almond ointment, or pumpkin seed ointment.
If it is winter, it should be like narcissus ointment, lily ointment, chamomile ointment, hyacinth ointment and the like. If the excess is thick and cold, the ointment should be marjoram ointment, nettle ointment, milk thistle ointment, or burdock ointment and the like.
If the phlebotomist has wide pores and soft flesh, ointment should be avoided. These individuals should wash their cupping glasses after phlebotomy with rose water or cold water or fox grape water or pumpkin water or chickpea water and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist.
It is washed with vinegar or with water and sumaq, etc. As for what has thick pus, it is washed with old wine or with marjoram water or a decoction of chamomile or chamomile, etc. And it should be avoided to cupping in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath and no one should sleep after cupping, what the cupper and the cupped should manage before and after cupping.
First, you should look if the cupper or the cupped is bilious and his blood is mostly sharp and inflamed, then he should take refrigerants like pomegranate and dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, sekandjbeen and jalab, etc., and make his food be game and lamb meat, skewers, and sparrow, etc.
And whoever is cold in temperament should be given honey syrup or mihrab syrup or basil skandjbeen, and take fragrant wine which is between old and new wine, and he should be ordered to eat little food and make his food game, onions, asparagus and white doves, and the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting should be more than food.
And it may be necessary to give some people from the Farooq antidote or musk medicine or siltha before cupping and bloodletting or after them to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood, and it should not be given to pregnant women.
As for the cuppings that are without a condition, they are the cuppings that are placed on the liver and spleen and breasts and abdomen and perineum and place of kidneys and right thigh because these organs cannot bear a condition on them, but rather it is intended to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping on the breasts in case of runny nose or using it to dissolve a cold wind that may have settled in the organ, such as placing the cupping on the abdomen and perineum, for it loosens the organ and heats it and relieves pain to dissolve that wind.
And it may be placed upon the kidney if there is a stoppage or stone in it, for by its strong attraction it might perchance open the stoppage or remove the stone from its place; and similarly it acts when placed upon the liver and spleen in case of wind which disturbs them. These specula may also be used empty with suction only.
It may also be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of sciatica; that is to say, fill the speculum, let it be a large one, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for that purpose have been cooked.
Then it is placed full upon the affected part, held there, and removed, and repeated as many times as necessary. This is a picture of the speculum which is used with fire:
Its mouth is of the size of two fingers open as depicted, and its depth is half a palm's breadth; there is on its side, towards the middle, a small hole just large enough to admit a needle, made of Chinese copper or yellow copper with a thick rim.
Smooth, flat and rounded so as not to injure the member when placed upon it; in its middle is a transverse groove of copper or iron where the candle is placed for the fire. This speculum may be made larger or smaller than what we have described according to the diseases.
And as for the use of it, specula for boys and thin people are not the same as those for men with large bodies. As for the way to apply this speculum by fire to the member: take a wick firmly fixed in a candle flame or a small candle made of pitch and place it upon the middle groove of the hardened rod which is in the center of the speculum.
So that the flame rises upwards towards the lower part of the speculum so as not to burn the patient's body; then it is placed upon the member, and the finger is kept on the hole we mentioned until when we have held the speculum as long as needed, the finger is removed.
The steam will then escape through this hole and the ligature will dissolve in place. The wick is then advanced along the line and replaced if necessary. As for the ligature used in the disease of elephantiasis with water, it has neither a rigid rod nor a hole.
It is only used by filling it with water and placing it on the member. This is its image:
The larger this ligature is, the more water it can hold, and the better it is.
Chapter Seventy-Seventh.
On the Suspension of a Leech.
Leechs are used in most cases for organs where ligatures cannot be placed, either because they are small, such as the lip and gums, or because the organ is devoid of flesh, such as the finger and nose.
The way to use them is to select leeches from fresh, pure water from springs and then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their stomachs. The body is then evacuated first by phlebotomy or cupping, and then the affected limb is wiped.
Until it reddens, and then the leech is placed on it. When it is full, it will fall off. If the area can be sucked by a ligature, this is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, rub and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a discharge, soak a linen cloth in cold water.
And place it on top until the discharge stops. If there is a lot of blood, sprinkle powdered alum or saffron or something similar from astringents on it until the bleeding stops, or apply half a peeled pomegranate to the area.
Leave it until the pomegranate adheres to the area, as the blood will then stop. If you need to reapply a leech, do not use the same one if possible. If the leech refuses to attach itself, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until something of blood comes out, then apply it; if it feels some blood, it will stick to the location. If you wish for it to fall off, sprinkle something of alum or salt or ashes upon it and it will fall off the location.
Chapter III.
In setting bones.
This chapter is also about what is needed in the craft of medicine, namely setting broken bones and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by ignorant physicians and commoners, and those who have never browsed an ancient book or read a letter from it.
For this reason, this art of medicine has become nonexistent in our land, and I have never encountered a single competent practitioner in it. And whatever I have gained from it, I have gained through my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them until I extracted the knowledge of this art from them, then I adhered to experimentation and practice throughout my life.
And in this chapter, I have drawn for you everything that my knowledge encompasses. My experience has gone into it after I brought it closer to you, purified it from the branches of prolixity, and summarized it to the utmost, clarified it to the utmost, and presented you with many images of the instruments used in it.
This is because of further elaboration, as I have done in the preceding two chapters. There is no power except with God, the Most High, the Great.
The First Section.
In it are general and comprehensive matters concerning the fracture of bones which must be presented before we begin to mention broken and dislocated members one by one,  it is necessary that we mention in the introduction to this chapter some general statements and sections. You are first compelled to understand them and grasp their truth, you and anyone else who is eager to learn this honorable craft.
And I say that if anyone suffers a fracture or dislocation or sprain or fall, it is necessary to hasten first to reduce it or loosen it or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength or if what happened to him was a child or an old man, or the time was extremely hot or very cold indeed.
Then he should restrict his diet to cold legumes and poultry meat, and should avoid drinks and thick meats and overeating and all food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling is relieved and you do not expect any discharge of material to the area.
Then let the patient return to his original treatment that he is used to, and if the broken bone is in a cast, the patient should eat nourishing foods that are thick and strong, such as barley, rice, heads, acorns, and cow hooves. And eggs, tender fish, thick drinks and the like, for by this treatment, the fracture will heal faster and better. Know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot join and unite on their original nature at all.
Because their bones are dry and rigid, and what might connect and unite is only the softest part of the bones, like the bones of young boys. But nature grows on the broken bone from all sides something that resembles glue, which is thick and sticks to it and tightens it until some parts stick together.
It binds some parts to others until it comes in the utmost strength and firmness as it was originally, so that nothing hinders his actions.
Therefore, it is necessary for the patient to eat foods that contain strength, viscosity and thickness as we said. Know that fractures may differ in types according to the difference of organs because a fracture of the leg bone is different from a skull fracture and a rib fracture is different from a spinal fracture.
And so are all the other members contrary to each other, and I will mention every type of fracture in detail in its chapter. The type of bone fracture may also differ in itself.
For it may be a fracture by crushing without splintering, or it may be fractured along the length of the bone with splinters and detached fragments, both sterile and non-sterile, and the fracture may be with a wound and rupture in the skin, or it may be a slight crack.
Each type has a special method for setting it as will be mentioned in detail in its place, and what distinguishes a bone fracture is its crookedness, swelling, and tenderness to the touch, and crackling when you pinch it with your hand.
So when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor crackling nor disturbance when you touch the bone and the patient does not experience much pain, then there is no fracture; rather it could be a sprain or a slight crack or a minor fissure, so it should not be moved by stretching or pinching at all.
But apply to it the remedies that will be mentioned later as appropriate for the location, then bind it with a gentle compression. Know that if the bone breaks and splits into two without splintering, even though each part is separated from the other.
You should hasten to straighten and align it as soon as possible before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling occurs, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then straighten it in any way you can with gentleness and skill.
Know that setting and aligning it is easier than the bone in which splinters have occurred, then bind it as will be mentioned later. If there are splinters in the bone, it is necessary to stretch the broken limb from both sides, whether it is an arm or a leg.
Either with your hands if the member is small, or with two cords, or with a cord and your hand. The position of the member should be level on its natural shape so that when the broken bone extends, then cut away those excrescences in their places by every means you can manage with skill and gentleness.
Do your utmost to avoid causing the patient pain or anguish with your actions and strive to join one of the bones to the other in the best formation. At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hand; if you see anything amiss, correct it and even it out as much as you are able.
Beware of excessive tension and forceful wedging, as many ignorant people often do, and which frequently causes a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have witnessed from their actions many times.
Then, after the adjustment, refinement, and tension, keep that limb still and at peace. Warn the patient not to move it during his waking hours or sleep, and when he turns, is disturbed, or has a bowel movement, and in all his movements as much as possible, and to seek a position for the limb that will protect him from pain.
If he feels any pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, let him change to a different position where he does not feel pain, and still strive to make that position level and straight.
So that the limb does not become deformed if it is forced, and as for how to bind a broken limb, it is as I have described to you; know that broken limbs differ in size, shape, and nature. Those that are smaller, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the like...
...should have soft, fine bandages. Those that are larger, such as the thigh, back, and chest, should have wide, firm bandages, because a broad bandage is needed to bind a large limb and tighten it evenly on all sides without any looseness.
Then, after you have finished smoothing it, you will apply the appropriate plaster for it. Place a soft bandage over the fracture site, then begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, depending on what the limb requires, and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then take it up to the upper part of the fracture site.
Tighten it less than you tightened the broken area, then space the wrapping away from the fracture site a little and loosen the tightness gradually until you take something suitable from the correct place, then take another bandage and wrap it also over the broken area.
Wrap it then take the wrapping to the lower part of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrap be as we mentioned in the first upper wrap, then place soft padding or rags between the wrappings according to what straightens the fracture.
If there is any angulation, put something in it, otherwise do not put anything in it, then wrap another bandage over it and then apply firm splints over these wraps from your time unless there is swelling or a hot tumor in the limb.
But if there is swelling or a hot tumor, apply something to soothe the tumor and reduce the swelling and leave it for days, then tighten the splints on it at that time, and let the splints be from the wide halved reeds, carved and prepared wisely, or the splints may be from raven wood.
Which are made from pine, palm leaves, boxwood or hollyhock and the like of what is available from that, and let the width of each splint be in this image and shape exactly.
Unless the splint that is placed on the fracture itself should be thicker and slightly wider than the rest of the splints, as for the length of the splint it is made according to what suits the broken limb in terms of its size. Picture of the splint:
Then bind the splints with another bandage according to your first binding, and then tie it from above with tight threads as we have mentioned regarding the tension, which is that your tension should be greater on the fracture site. The less tension the farther you are from the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, and they should be made from damp linen, especially since thick threads, like those used by ignorant people who make their threads from twisted linen strips, are a grave mistake.
Because the tension with them will be excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you will not achieve the desired tension with them. There should not be less than the width of a finger between one splint and another.
If the patient suffers pain from the ends of the splints after binding in the correct places, place soft padding or cotton wool underneath so that he does not feel any discomfort. As for a fracture with a wound and a rupture in the skin, we will discuss it separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be bound with splints from the first day. If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five, seven, or more days have passed, depending on your confidence in preventing a hot swelling.
This is the description of the bandages which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains - a general, brief bandage that sets bones and is suitable for most temperaments, especially boys and women because it is neither too hot nor too cold.
It consists of taking bran from the mill, which is the flour dust that clings to the walls of the mill during grinding, and kneading it as is without sifting it. Add egg white and make the dough neither too thick nor too thin, then use it.
Description of another plaster which sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains; take ten dirhams of each of mastich, lentisk, acacia, rosan, frankincense, and mastic, five dirhams of myrrh and patience from each, twenty dirhams of marshmallow, and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Pound all together and mix with marshmallow water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then apply this plaster; it is good for uniting, quickly setting broken bones, and suits most people due to its moderation.
Description of another plaster which is also good for fractures and sprains; take ten dirhams each of frankincense, mastich, and white henna, five dirhams each of myrrh and patience, six dirhams of acacia, twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Pound all together finely, sift, and knead with water or egg white, then apply.
Description of a plaster for joints and bones which have slipped from their places, and to relieve the pain accompanying them, as well as headache due to bone fracture; take purified wool and soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, then apply to the area. This plaster has no power to set.
But it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and expelling pain, especially. Description of another plaster which sets broken bone; take the leaves of common fig and wild poppy and pound them all together, then apply them damp. Description of a shorter plaster used when a bone is set.
And if you want to analyze the rest of the swelling, take from the root of henna, chamomile, violet and carnation seed, one part of each; pound all together and knead with plaster if the member is not inflamed; but if it is inflamed, knead with coriander water or with water and apply.
Description of another plaster stronger in resolution than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs after bone setting; take from the root of henna, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, marjoram, violet and chamomile, one part of each.
Everyone pounds and kneads it with water of rue or with fresh water or with plaster, all according to the heat of the member and its quiescence. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed upon the fractured member when it is set.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil alone, and he described it as being intermediate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he saw that things with hollows and a little heat should be placed upon the fractured member when setting it, such as myrrh and mastic and frankincense and what we have mentioned.
Description of a poultice that is beneficial for weakness and pain: take equal parts of flaxseed, sesame seed, human hair from a person who has recently recovered from illness, or bird feathers, mastic, and salt; pound it, sift it, knead it and apply it as a poultice.  As for the amount of time the bandage should remain.
Then it is removed; you should observe if the patient does not experience pain or irritation, nor any movement of the broken bone from its place, do not remove it for many days. If there occurs in that place severe irritation or disturbing pain or swelling.
Immediately remove it at once and do not delay that, nor leave the poultice on. Then take a soft rag or a sea sponge moistened with lukewarm water and wash the area until the irritation subsides and the pain calms down, then let the member rest for an hour.
Then place upon it wool that has been moistened with vinegar and oil or rose ointment and bind it for a night so that you are safe from the hot swelling, and the limb's swelling subsides and its pain goes away; then return it to gentle tension and a light poultice and do not tighten it as you did at first.
And be gentle with it until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain and swelling, and all the symptoms have completely gone and required a poultice and strapping, then repeat as you did first, whether or not anything of what we mentioned occurred in the limb.
Do not set it until after three, four, five or seven days; or even twenty days may be left, all according to the state of the limb as we have said, until if breaking is necessary and the flesh is about to unite upon it.
Then also increase the tension more than your initial tension altogether and also increase the strengthening of the patient's diet as previously mentioned, for if you see that the place of the fracture has dried up and become flabby more than is proper, know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you dress it, and let this be done every three days, and slightly reduce the tension, for by this action nourishment is carried to the limb and it heals quickly; but what ignorant binders do, breaking the limb again,
If it was not first bound as it should be and is set crooked, is a great mistake on their part and a deception; for if it were right they would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and you would not see any trace of it from anyone among them whatsoever. And the correct course is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On Fractures Encountered in the Head.
There are many types of accidental fractures in the head, with diverse shapes and intricate causes. Some fractures may result from a sword blow, either cleaving the bone entirely down to the dura mater beneath the bone. As a woodcutter does to wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "woodcutter's" fracture. Or the sword may cleave only part of the bone, and its surface alone, without penetrating to the end; this type of fracture is called a "complete avulsion." The wound from these fractures can be either large or small.
Other fractures may be a crush or contusion, caused by a blow with a stone or a fall onto a stone or similar object. This type of fracture can either penetrate nearly to the membrane beneath the bone or occur on the surface of the bone.
The wound of these two fractures will also be either large or small, and the fracture may be hidden in the bone in the thinness of hair, which is a slight crack.
That is why this type of fracture is called hairy, and there is a fracture that occurs from a fall or a stone blow and the like, which enters the surface of the bone inside and makes the place concave, as it appears in copper when it is struck by a blow, part of it enters inside.
This is more common in the wet head bone, such as the heads of boys, and all these types of fractures may have free fragments and non-free fragments, and we will come to mention the treatment of all this in its place, and we recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examine it with probes and remove the corrupted flesh from it, and as for the hairy type, it is known by exposing the bone, touching it, and smearing it with ink, then the fracture will appear black, and as for the treatment of the fracture, you first look at the patient's symptoms.
If you see from his symptoms an indication that is clearly indicative of fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of mind, interruption of speech, fainting, and a severe fever, redness and inflammation of the eyes, and other similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient or treat him. For death will befall him with these symptoms in most cases inevitably.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for his safety, then take him in treatment; for if the wounded man comes to you immediately after the injury, and this was in the winter days.
You should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is in summer days, you should hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone deteriorates, exposing those symptoms that we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is fragmentation and crushing, then it is necessary to cut off the crushed portion as I will describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way you are able, according to the shape of the wound and what relieves the patient; if, upon exposure, blood flows or a hot swelling appears on the bone, treat it accordingly.
This is done by stuffing the area with cloths soaked in a decoction of roses and oil, cleansing the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops; then proceed to grind the bone and extract it, which can be accomplished in one of two ways.
One way is to cut the bone with a delicate, narrow-bladed instrument, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter, slightly wider than the first, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter, also wider than the second; you should have several cutters of different widths and lengths, all with extremely sharp tips, made of Indian iron or good steel.
Use gentleness when striking the cutter so as not to shake the head and cause him pain. If the bone is strong and hard, you should drill around it before using the cutters with awls called "non-piercing" awls.
This is because they do not penetrate beyond the skull bone; their head has a circular shape below the sharp point, resembling a collar or small circle, which prevents them from plunging and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should have several of these awls, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that you are prepared to drill every skull with a sharp tip length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull; this is an image of three types: From large trephines, medium and small:
As for how to drill around a broken bone, you should place the trephine on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then move the trephine to another location and make the distance between each hole the thickness of a probe or thereabouts.
Then cut with the saw between each two holes and do this as gently as you can, as we have said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from some of the instruments that were prepared for that purpose.
Such as forceps and fine pliers, and you should be very careful that the trephine or saw touches nothing from the peritoneum. And if you have strengthened the bone and separated it from the peritoneum if it was attached to it and removed it, then it should be dressed.
And smooth any roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument that resembles a saw, but it should be thinner and finer than all other saws. If there are any small bones or fragments remaining, take them gently with the instruments you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with bandages and ointments that will be mentioned, and as for the other side of the work, it is very easy and far from danger, something Galen mentioned and praised greatly. This is what he said: You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and prominent, so that once you have exposed that part, you place the tip of this lens-shaped saw underneath it. And this is its image:
The lens-shaped part of it should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and the sharp part of it is on its sides that extend in length as you see, so that the lens-shaped part rests on the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the saw is in the bone, then you strike the saw from one side.
With a small hammer, until you cut through all the bone gently, as it rotates and you are safe from the membrane where nothing terrible will happen at all, even if the healer is the most ignorant and cowardly of men, yes, even if he were asleep.
If anything of the membrane remains adhering to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lens-shaped cutter itself and gently free it, for it will come away without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone has not extended to the membrane and what has been broken off from the bone has a rough surface and fine splinters, then you should remove that roughness and extract those splinters with fine scrapers, several of which have been made in different sizes so that you can use whichever is most suitable and best for each place according to what your work dictates.
And the same broken bone and its shape, you should use in your scraping first the widest of those scrapers then use one finer than it and continue doing so in this manner until you arrive at using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for all the other small, hair-like fissures and fine fractures, treatment suitable for each one should be used according to what leads to its healing; this is something that is not hidden from someone who has even the slightest experience in this craft and has considered what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after you have removed the bone, then you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth folded in two or three. Soak it in syrup and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth; do this as lightly as you can so as not to weigh down the peritoneum. Then use a broad bandage over it and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the hot swelling is safe, then dissolve it and use afterwards medicines that have a drying effect, such as the root of asphodel and the powder of caraway and cinnamon and costus and the like. This means making a poultice from these medicines.
Apply it to the wound as is, dry. In general, use a medicine that will cleanse without stinging. Make every effort in treating the wound to keep it clean, with neither fat nor dirt in it, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
For pus, if it collects on the membrane of the brain, will spoil and corrupt it, and from that there will befall upon the patient a great calamity. It may also occur in the periosteum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if his treatment is neglected.
If you see blackness on its surface and the patient shows the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is inevitably doomed. If the blackness occurred from a medicine applied to it, and that medicine had the power to do so.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, beat them together well, soak a cloth in the mixture, and place it on the periosteum. Then treat it with the types of treatment that are appropriate until it is healed.
Chapter Three.
On setting a broken nose.
Know that the only part of the nose that can break is its upper section, either both or one, because they are bones. The lower part is cartilaginous and does not break but may suffer bruising, distortion, and displacement.
If one of its sections breaks, you should insert the little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from the inside with your middle finger and thumb from the outside until the nose is returned to its natural shape. Do this gently and carefully so as not to cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and no finger has been joined to it, it should be straightened with the tip of a rod slightly thickened. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same, and hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert a wick of linen cloth into the nostril opening if the fracture is on one side or insert two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril opening, and some of the early setters mentioned soaking the wick in ghee and changing it every day. I do not see that, but it should be soaked in egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the wick until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens, and you may insert goose feather tubes in place of the wick after wrapping them with soft cloth, so their retention for a nasal fracture is firmer and so that the patient does not refrain from breathing.
This is not necessary if you wish to do it, and if you wish make the wicks. If during your work the nose swells with hot inflammation, bandage the nose with qirtayus or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or something from the diachylon plaster.
If hot inflammation does not occur, it should be bandaged from the outside with fine semolina and powdered benzoin mixed with egg white, then a soft bandage should be placed on top, and the nose should not be tied with anything at all. If the nasal bones are fractured in a small break.
Or if they are shattered, it should be incised and removed with the instrument suitable for that, then the incision should be sutured and treated with ointments appropriate for healing and closing, and if a wound occurs inside the nose, it should be treated with a wick and lead tubes should be used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
In setting the lower jaw if it is broken.
If the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, and if the fracture is external only and has not been broken in two and pushed inward, then recognizing it is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, insert the index finger of your right hand and raise the prominence of the fracture from the inside gently to the outside.
Your other hand, from outside the bone, controls its alignment. If the jawbone fracture has been split in two, a spreader should be used on both sides to straighten it so that it can be set. If it has occurred in the teeth, they will have to be extracted.
Or separate and tighten what you intend to keep with a thread of gold, silver or silk; then place the plaster on the broken jaw, followed by a folded cloth and then a large, tight splint or a piece of leather sole equal in length to the jaw. Then tie it securely from above, as convenient for you and conforming to its compression so that it does not loosen, and order the patient to refrain from speaking and keep still, and give him soft, liquid food. If you think that something has changed in any way in the shape of the jaw, proceed immediately to correct it on the third day.
Then tie it securely from above, as convenient for you and conforming to its compression so that it does not loosen, and order the patient to refrain from speaking and keep still, and give him soft, liquid food. If you think that something has changed in any way in the shape of the jaw, proceed immediately to correct it on the third day. Then fix what you have compressed with flour mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the plaster, and place a soft bandage on top of the dressing. As long as the bandage adheres to it and the bone's condition has not changed, leave it alone.
Do not loosen it until it heals and the fracture consolidates, for this fracture often consolidates in three weeks. If swelling occurs during this time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to relieve it until the swelling subsides.
If the fracture is with a wound, and if it has become free of bone fragments, extract those fragments carefully in a manner suitable for removing them with an instrument. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel to the extent necessary.
Then, if you have extracted all those fragments and nothing remains, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. And allow the wound to heal until it is fully recovered.
Chapter Five.
On setting a broken clavicle.
The clavicle most often breaks in front of the end at the shoulder, and the fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits into two without fragmentation, which is the easiest to set;
or fragmentation occurs in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture occurs with a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants: one to hold the arm next to the broken clavicle and the other to extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then straighten the fracture with your fingers until it assumes its proper shape, without any bump or angulation; if you need more extension, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
The ball will provide the necessary extension, and you will extend and raise the clavicle while pressing down on the ball with your hand until the fracture is set properly. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outward because it has sunk deep, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a medium-sized pillow under their elbow, and have the assistant press down on the elbow until the deep clavicle bone rises upward. At that point, correct the fracture and straighten it with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken and is moving, it should be incised and that fragment carefully removed. If the fragment is lodged in the bone, proceed to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for that purpose. After placing the instrument under the clavicle, which preserves the peritoneum and is made of wood or iron, this is its form:
It resembles a spoon without a handle, and its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. Its length should also be according to what you are able to work with. Let it have two ends, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the incision made when removing the bone fragment is wide and you are confident there will be no hot swelling, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures. If the incision is small or you fear hot swelling, treat the wound with dressing and bandages according to the size of the incision.
If hot swelling appears, soak the dressing in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. To fix the bone if there is no wound or incision, apply a poultice made of rye flour mixed with egg white to the bone. Place a soft bandage over it, then put a ball under his armpit if necessary. Take a long turban, about a span wide, fold the bandage and poultice over the bandage, and then take a splint from a thin board. It should be three finger-widths wide and long, then wrap it in cloth, place the cloth with the splint in the part of the turban that lies over the fracture, and tighten the turban around the fracture as it goes around. Tie it around his neck and under his sound armpit, then under the injured armpit. Wrap it several times on each side and adjust the tightness so that the fracture is firmly setâ€”this will be clear to you.
The principle is that the splint should not be removed from the fractured bone. Therefore, the patient should be examined every day; whenever the ligament relaxes and you see that the splint has become loose, fix it and tighten the ligament. Then, make the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit at night while he sleeps.
This will raise his arm away from his side, raising the clavicle fracture with the raising of the elbow, or his arm is tied to his neck; and do not loosen the ligament unless there occurs in the place an incident of rubbing or swelling for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a need for it. Continue tightening and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and unites, and becomes stronger during twenty-eight days. It may be in some people in less time than that.
Chapter Six.
In setting a broken shoulder. The shoulder rarely breaks in its wide part, but rather its processes; whenever it breaks in a place or breaks in the middle, this is known by touch, and according to the shape of the break, a mold is made.
Set it and return it to its natural shape in every way you can, then apply rye flour powder mixed with egg white and a soft bandage; place a folded cloth dressing on top, then a wide splint made of wood.
Make it thin enough to cover the whole shoulder or a little wider; if there is swelling in any place under the splint, even it out with a soft bandage so that the splint lies flat, then tie it tightly from above with a long turban.
Tighten it securely and make sure the splint does not move from its place, and check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved, let the patient lie on his right side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
On the twenty-fifth day, if this period is completed, release the ligature and you will be safe, for it is one of the bones which there is no fear of its breaking or crushing; but if a fragment of bone should protrude and lie under the skin, make an incision over it and remove it. Do what I have mentioned in fractures of the clavicle to relieve a hot swelling if any such thing should occur.
Chapter Seven.
On setting a fracture of the breastbone.
The breastbone may break in the middle, which is rare; but its extremities are more frequently fractured and comminuted. If it breaks in the middle, it inclines downwards, and is attended with violent pain, difficulty of breathing, and coughing.
Sometimes blood is coughed up and the depression in the broken bone becomes apparent and unmistakable to the touch. To set it, let the patient lie on his back and place a cushion between his shoulders; then press his elbows together and bring the ribs into apposition with the hands on both sides, and adjust it as carefully as possible in every direction.
Continue this until the shape of the bone is restored; then apply a bandage and splint, placing over it a light splint made of thin wood, such as boxwood or holly, after wrapping it in linen. Adjust the bandage carefully over the broken bone so that it will not become loose; pass the bandage around to the back several times and tighten it firmly; then examine the bandage frequently and whenever it becomes loose, tighten it again.
If necessity requires the bandage to be removed, when pain, swelling or inflammation occurs in the part, hasten to remove it, take off the bandage and correct what has occurred by appropriate treatment; then replace the bandage if you deem it necessary. Direct him to wear a support until he is healed.
Chapter Eight.
In setting a broken rib, know that fractures in the ribs occur mostly in the thick places following the back and their ends from the front. This is because they are cartilaginous, and this knowledge is not hidden when examining with fingers.
Set the fracture by straightening it with your fingers as much as possible until the shape is restored, then bandage and constrict the broken bone with a splint if needed. If the rib fracture is angled inward, it will cause the patient severe pain.
And a collapse similar to that which occurs in those with pneumothorax may occur because the bone collapses the diaphragm and also causes him shortness of breath, coughing, and expectoration of a large amount of blood. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients have tried many tricks for it.
Some say that the patient's diet should consist of foods that produce flatulence and gas so that the abdomen swells and expands, pushing the fracture outward. We dislike this lest it aggravate the formation of a hot abscess. If it has already occurred, it will increase and strengthen it.
Some said that a cupping glass should be placed on the area and sucked forcefully, similar to drawing out pus. However, they fear that the cupping glass may draw further into the weakened area. Others said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
Padding is placed between the ribs until it is full, making the bandage even when wrapped around it. Then, treat the patient like one with pneumothorax with diet and medicine. If the patient is experiencing extreme hardship they cannot endure... â€¦and the bone collapses the diaphragm in a harmful way, and we fear for the patient's well-being. So, we should cut open the area and expose the broken rib, then place the instrument that preserves the peritoneum underneath it (a description of which is provided later). We cut the bone carefully and remove it.
