Then we bring together the lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling should appear during this time, hasten to soak lint in rose oil and place it on the area and treat the patient with something that will soothe the swelling from within as well.
He should lie on the side that is more comfortable for sleep until he is healed.
Chapter Nine.
On setting a dislocated vertebra of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, this rarely occurs, and what is more common is a sprain;  the same applies to the vertebrae of the back. If someone suffers from this and you want to know whether it will heal or not. Observe whether his hands have become relaxed and numb, lifeless, and unable to move, extend, or contract them; and if you prick or stab them with a needle he does not feel it nor experience pain in them, then know that it will most likely not heal, for he is lost.
But if he can move them and feels the pricking and stabbing, know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will heal with treatment. If a vertebra of the back has been similarly dislocated and you want to know whether it will also heal,
Observe his legs and see if they too have become relaxed, and what happened in the hands has occurred in them; then, if he lies on his back, wind and stool pass involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine passes involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and tries to urinate but is unable to, know that he is lost and do not concern yourself with treating him. If none of these things occur, the matter is less serious. The treatment for what has happened is to apply something to reduce the hot swelling.
Apply rose oil alone to the diseased vertebra, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then, place one of the strengthening bandages on the area and tighten it with a bandage. Order him to rest and remain still.
He should not sleep except on the side where he does not feel pain until he is healed. If a fragment of bone or something else that has become healed has occurred at the time of the injury, it should be cut open and that bone should be removed. Then the lips of the wound are brought together if it is large, by stitching, and then treated with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, the thumb of the left hand should be inserted into the anus.
The broken bone is then straightened with the other hand, as much as possible and straightening is attained, then a dressing and splint are applied if needed, and then it is tightened.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, cut it open and remove it, then treat the wound with the treatment previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On Setting a Hip Fracture.
Rarely are the bones of the hip fractured. If they are fractured, the fracture is usually one in which they crumble at their extremities and split lengthwise, tending inwards. This will cause the patient pain in that spot and a limp, as well as numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
To set it, pass your hand over it until you determine the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the extremities, straighten that fracture as best as possible so that it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has inclined inwards, lay the patient on their stomach so that you can set the fracture. Once it is straightened, apply a dressing and then place a splint made of wood or leather over it, and tighten it securely.
Do not worry about the fracture shifting or the splint coming undone. Straighten any hollows in the waist with padding to ensure an even tightening. Order the patient to sleep on their back or right side, and if they develop a hot swelling:
Stop extending it and dress the wound until the hot swelling subsides, then apply what will soothe it as previously mentioned. Then return to dressing the wound and tightening it as needed. If there are fragments or any part has broken off from the bone, they should not be removed.
Do not touch them but smooth them out externally as we have said and leave it tightened until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it breaks, set it in one of two ways: one is to take a smooth, curved stick of medium thickness in this manner:
Tie ropes at both ends and hang it from a high place. Then have the patient sit on a chair and lay his broken arm on the stick so that his armpit becomes stuck in the middle of the curve of the stick, then hang something heavy from above.
Or a servant extends it downwards, then the doctor straightens the fracture with both his hands until he returns the fracture to its proper place; the other way is for the patient to lie on his back and hang his hand from his neck with a rope.
Then order two servants, one to hold what is above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each one extends it towards his side. If you want the extension to be stronger, tie a rope under the fracture and another above it, and have each of the servants extend them toward their sides.
If the fracture is near the end of the elbow, the middle rope should be placed under the armpit and the other below the fracture towards the elbow; likewise if the fracture is near the elbow, the ropes should be placed at that location and on the elbow itself.
Then gently straighten the fracture without violence until it is aligned as needed and a good union occurs, then tighten it if no hot swelling appears; but if hot swelling appears, leave it untightened until the seventh day and place on it a poultice soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Even if the swelling subsides, then bind it tightly, and the method of binding it is to hold the dressing on the fracture, then hold a bandage of new cloth over the dressing, then gather the arm against the upper arm itself and place his hand open on his elbow.
Hold the cloth and bind it to the upper arm and arm so that the arm acts as splints if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes for you from the broken bone, then if you fear that something will change for you from that, use splints.
It is to place on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than all other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side.
Then tighten on the splints the binding that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is for your binding to be tightest on the place of the fracture.
And the farther from the fracture, the looser the binding should be; if you see the placing of the splints and the binding as we have said at the time of your setting of the limb, then do so.
And if you fear hot swelling, leave the binding and splints until the seventh day as we have said, then examine the bandage every three days so that no roughness or bloating occurs in the place, nor is food prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness; then correct all of that according to what we mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of that will happen, do not loosen the bandage except after many days, and let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a bolster stuffed with wool placed moderately under the upper arm.
And check at all times, night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be displaced or the bandage relaxes, and correct all that with your utmost effort; make the patient's diet according to the rank we have presented, so that the food is mild at first until the bone begins to strengthen.
His food should be strengthened, for it is customary for the arm and leg to become strong in forty days. Then it should be set and he should use the bath and be treated with ointments suitable for that, if the fracture is severe and crushed.
The bandage should not be removed from it for fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelfth.
On Setting a Fracture of the Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius and ulna, one is small, which is next to the thumb, and the other is large, which is located below the smaller one. Sometimes the larger radius breaks alone or the smaller one breaks alone, and sometimes they both break together.
So when the smaller upper radius breaks, its setting is easy and its healing is faster. And when the lower ulna breaks, its fracture is bad and its healing is difficult. The worst of all is if both bones break together. If the bone that has broken is the smaller upper radius...
The physician should, when setting it, make the extension gentle so as to straighten it. If the larger ulna is the one broken, he should make the extension stronger. And if both radii are broken, he should make the extension much stronger indeed.
The shape of the hand should be placed when setting it and extending it on a pillow, with the thumb of the hand raised higher than all the fingers, and the little finger lower than all the other fingers, and the ring finger based squarely on itself.
Let the pillow be raised in front of him so that the patient is not troubled.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends it from above. Then the physician straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape possible. If there are fragments in the fracture, they are removed.
Try your utmost to put each fragment back in its place. If a sterile fragment appears and it is undermining the skin, do not attempt to suture it. Instead, make an incision over it and remove it in the manner we have previously described. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, we have devoted a separate chapter to it.
You will find treatment for that in the aforementioned chapter. If swelling appears immediately after your dressing and it is hot, apply a cloth poultice made with rose ointment and white wax. Let it be of medium thickness and bind it gently over the swelling until it subsides.
Then remove the rose ointment poultice and apply a dressing prepared from powdered barley mixed with egg white. After that, apply splints; let the splint placed directly on the fracture be slightly wider and stronger. Be aware that the number of arm splints is usually six.
Whether the fracture is in one bone or in two together, make your binding tightest at the site of the fracture. And the more you move upward or downward with the binding, the looser it should become. This follows what we mentioned earlier in the first chapter.
Let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft and damp, not overly firm. The thread used for binding should be linen, of medium thickness as we have described. Examine the limb and its ligaments after a few days.
If something requires correction, such as a rash that appears on the limb, immerse the limb in warm water until the rash subsides. Then leave the limb unbound for a night so it can rest, and afterward resume binding.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted, or something similar has occurred, correct it to the best of your ability. Also observe whether the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness. If so, loosen it slightly and leave it unbound for a few days until nourishment flows freely, then resume binding.
If nothing mentioned above presents itself to the patient, then he should not be disturbed except after twenty days or so. Then his hand should be hung from his neck, with his arm kept straight, and care should be taken that he avoids agitated movements and sleeps on his back.
Know that this arm fracture will set in thirty days or in two and thirty, and perhaps even in twenty-eight, all depending on the temperaments and strengths of the individual cases.
Chapter Thirteen.
On Setting a Fracture of the Hand and Fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but they are often bruised.  Whenever a fracture or bruise occurs in the hand...
The patient should sit squarely with a chair directly opposite him.
Then he should place his hand on it, extended. The bone-setter's assistant then extends the fractured limb and straightens it; the doctor continues until a good union is achieved. If no hot swelling occurs, a bandage and splint should be applied. Then a cast should be placed over it, according to the size of the area.
If it has been wrapped in a soft cloth, and if the fracture is towards the bottom, near the palm, make a half sphere from cloth and have the patient grasp it with his fractured hand, then tighten it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of supple leather so that it conforms to the bend of the entire hand, and tighten it as needed. If the fracture is outward, a splint should be placed on top and another below in the hand so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then the tension should be held as the hand turns and a ribbon should be tied between the fingers. If a fracture occurs in one of the phalanges, and it is the thumb, straighten it as needed and then bind it with the hand. If you wish, you may make a small upright splint to support the fracture.
And it does not move if the fracture is to all the fingers like the middle finger, index finger or ring finger or little finger, then straighten it and bind it with the following healthy finger, or bind them all on a splint; this is best, or put a small upright splint on it.
As we said in the thumb and examine it while splinting it and keeping it away from hot swelling, then treat it as necessary when something of that occurs, as its description has been repeated.
Chapter Fourteenth.
In the Setting of a Femur Fracture.
The femur often fractures and is evident to the touch because it displaces forward and backward. Its setting is done by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another below it, with the patient lying on his stomach.
Then extend each bandage holder to its side in accordance with this if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, but if it is near the base of the thigh, a soft bandage should be tightened, either from wool or something similar.
At the base of the thigh towards the groin, the tension should be upwards and the other bandage below the fracture; likewise if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the tension goes downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both his hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone then unites well, at which point the bandage and tightening should be maintained unless swelling occurs in the limb. If swelling occurs, leave it for several days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for tightening it, a wide, firm bandage should be wound twice or three times over the fracture, with any excess remaining. Then the leg is wound until the heel is at the base of the splint and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below. The ends of the thread are brought upwards on both sides.
Then turn the remainder of the bandage upon the leg and thigh, and place splints upon the thigh in the very seat of the fracture, making one splint of them for the bone of the leg; then stuff the interval between the thigh and the leg with soft linen, so that the tension may be equal.
Then begin to bind firmly in the middle, over the seat of the fracture, three or four turns; and the farther you carry the bandage from the seat of the fracture, let your binding be looser and less tight.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread which you had introduced between the thigh and leg, and tie them to the splints above; then pass the ends of the thread downwards until they reach the ankle, and tighten them there also to the ends of the splints on that side.
So that the bandage may not slip from its place, and leave it tight as long as the limb does not complain of pain, swelling, or distension, or anything of that kind; but if any such thing should happen, hasten to undo it and rectify whatever has occurred as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there are splinters in the bone which project, it should be leveled if possible; otherwise, cut them out and remove them. Treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may sometimes be set without binding the leg to the thigh with splints.
As we have said in regard to the humerus and arm, but setting it in this way does not cause the patient to limp; if, however, you set it alone without binding it to the leg, the patient will always limp.
Know that the thigh takes fifty days to consolidate, or a little more or less according to the difference in constitutions and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteenth.
On Fracture of the Patella.
The kneecap is rarely fractured, but it is often bruised. Should a fracture occur, it will be either a crack or a shattering into fragments, and this may occur with or without a wound.
You rely on feeling to judge this, and you force it to straighten any separated parts with your fingers until they come together and unite as best as possible with care, skill, and precision. Then apply a dressing and a circular splint if needed.
Tighten the bandage above it accordingly, and in all cases you must follow the procedures we have described for other fractures, such as hot swelling, and so forth. Meet each occurrence with what is suitable until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Setting a Broken Leg.
The leg has two bones: one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula. These bones are subject to the same types of fractures as those in the arm. Therefore, setting them is done in the same way as setting an arm, and the process is one. If both bones are broken, the leg turns in all directions. If only the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns forward. And if only the thicker bone breaks below the knee, this will be clear to you.
Use traction and alignment, and bind splints accordingly, except that if the fracture is a severe one with many fragments, traction should be less forceful and gentler, and you must exert great care in setting it.
In addition to the work done on the arm, in setting a leg you must take two pine slats which are used for leveling rooms and place them between the splint's gaps, or use palm leaves or similar material.
Choose those that are slightly thick and not too thin, and let their length be the length of the leg from the knee down. Then wrap a cloth twice along the length of each slat and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let it be from the knee to below the foot, then bind the splints in three places at the ends and the middle; for by this binding, the leg is prevented from inclining right and left, and it is properly fixed. It may also be used with a trough of wood along the length of the leg.
Place it in this to keep it from moving, and it is best to do this in a fracture if there is a wound with it, then examine the leg every day and take great care of it for swelling or bloating or anything else. So when something like that happens, meet it with what is necessary until it heals, and the bone of the leg will be set in thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On Fractures of the Leg Bones and Fingers.
As for the heel, it is not subject to fracture at all. As for the bones of the leg, they may be fractured, and the fingers are rarely fractured, but they are more often bruised.
If the bones of the leg are fractured and you see that those bones are overlapping, let the patient place his foot on the ground upright as if walking, then put your foot on what has risen from those bones and step on it and straighten them.
Until it returns to its place, then carry a bandage and splint over it and put under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat heads, then tighten the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloths and anything else it needs.
If three or four days have passed, untie the bandage, for you will find the bones straight whether they were broken or dislocated; do as you see. As for if some fingers are broken, force and straighten them according to what I have described to you for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then put a splint for the broken finger along the length of the finger and let it be slightly wider than it, then put under the foot this board that I described to you and tighten it firmly, if the number of broken bones is two or three or more.
Place a splint on each finger, fitted into soft cloth and secure the board at the bottom of the foot. Let the head of each splint project outwards from the sole of the foot for proper alignment.
You should remember all that we have mentioned to you regarding the symptoms of fractures and treat each accordingly.
Chapter Eighteenth.
Fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's pelvis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her in a squatting position and then lean her slightly backward while supporting her from behind. Then have the midwife fill her vagina with cotton until it becomes like a ball.
Then shake the woman and raise her pelvis slightly, so that the cotton comes out until it forms a ball at the vaginal opening. This will reduce the fracture. Then apply a bandage to her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can urinate.
Replace it in the same way it was initially inserted and then return to her original bandage. Do this for seven days or so, as it will heal. If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, stretch a reed tube over its opening, and insert the whole bladder into her vagina.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vagina; this will reduce the fracture. Then pack it with cotton and keep it for several days as we described until it heals. As for a fractured pubic bone in a man or woman, the same method is used to set it.
And treat it as we described for a fractured hip bone. It will not be hidden from you the correctness of these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book of mine, yes, because the fracture itself guides you in most cases.
On the right way to heal and tighten it, understand. As for a man if his thigh is broken, take a goose's throat and insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth on it and bandage it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteenth.
On setting fractures of bones when there is a wound.
Whoever has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the thighbone or humerus or similar, should hasten to bleed him when conditions for bleeding permit as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound bleeds blood, you should hasten to staunch it by sprinkling powdered alum on it if nothing else is available. Then proceed with setting the fracture that same day and do not delay it.
If no hot swelling occurs, but if a hot swelling does occur, leave the setting until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides and do not approach it at all on the third or fourth day for it is subject to bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is protruding onto the skin exposed, you should try to return it and set it straight with your hands gently and slightly stretching. If you cannot return it and set it straight with your hands, return it with this instrument which is made of iron seven or eight fingers long. And its width according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should acquire three or four of them according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture and let it be rounded with a slight thickness so that it does not bend when pressed upon during work.
And it should have a sharp end with a bend at its end, the top being thicker and from the middle to the bottom very thin. And this is its form:
It is called in Greek Periosteum, meaning a small awl. The sharp bent end should be placed on the protruding bone and pushed with it at once so that when the bone returns and becomes somewhat straight, you then align the ends of the fracture upon each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument has not grasped it well, the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can take hold of it. If you cannot reduce the bone at all as we have described, cut it according to its shape from the sections we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the instruments as much as possible, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone. If you have reduced the bone and the patient still experiences severe, painful distress afterward, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you are able to return it to its natural place, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. Once you have finished setting the bone, dip a cloth in a dark astringent solution, especially if it is summer, and do not put anything greasy on the wound.
So that decay and putrefaction do not occur, then use splints when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors to fit the size of the wound.
Beware of tightening the wound with a fracture, for ignorant physicians often do this and cause their patients either death or gangrene. Let your binding be soft and loose, unlike the binding of other fractures.
But if the wound is poor or a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he finds worrisome pain in the area, do not put splints on it. Make him bandages from strong cloths in place of the splints and tighten them around him.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you have placed on it with the solution and then use the bandages and ointments that we are accustomed to treating wounds with, such as the quadripartite ointment and the like. You should also loosen the bandage.
You should inspect the wound every morning and evening until it heals and closes. The limb should be positioned so that pus can easily flow downward. If, after many days, the wound has not closed and the pus continues to flow, know that there are small bone fragments present.
You should then probe the wound with a surgical instrument. Any fragments that are clean and free of infection should be removed. If any fragments are infected and causing pain or swelling, they should be cut into smaller pieces and carefully removed in any way possible.
If the wound shows signs of cold, a bad odor, or any other type of corruption or decay, you should treat each symptom according to its description in the relevant chapter.
It is important to remember that if a large bone, such as the femur or humerus, is broken and you are treating the limb, you should not attempt to pull it or remove it from its position. Attempting to do so often results in death. Instead, leave it until it decays and it may then fall away on its own after twenty or thirty days.
At that point, you can treat the wound if there is a suitable area for treatment; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On Treating Complications That May Arise Following Some Fractures.
This complication often occurs after a fracture has healed, particularly those near joints.  It can result in a deformed limb and may prevent the limb from functioning normally.
If the complication is soft, use astringent medications such as frankincense, myrrh, tamarisk, acacia, or similar herbs. Combine some or all of these with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply the mixture to the complication, bind it tightly with a bandage, and leave it in place for many days. Then remove the bandage and apply a fresh one until the complication disappears, God willing. Alternatively, you can secure a tight lead plate over the complication.
Lead has a property that removes everything that is implanted in the organs. If the implant has become hardened and intense, and necessity compels its removal, cut it from above and remove the excess that results, or scrape it with some instruments until it is gone, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it heals and the limb afterward remains weak and unlike its original state.
If a bone fracture heals and the limb afterward remains weak and feeble, then it is due to many reasons. One of them is either for the excessive loosening and tying of ligaments in a way that is not proper, or for the excessive tightening of ligaments until food is prevented from flowing to the limb, or for excessive immobilization, or for excessive movements at an improper time, or for a lack of blood in the body of the patient and its weakness.
And the treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that blood increases in it, use the bath and introduce joy and happiness to him, and then apply plaster to the limb so that the plaster attracts a great deal of nourishment to it, or continue immobilizing it with lukewarm water. So that nourishment flows into it and it returns to its natural state.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of broken bones that heal crookedly and prevent them from functioning properly.
When a limb, after healing from a break, shows crookedness or a bony prominence or complication that has distorted the shape of the limb. Unless the limb has been prevented from its natural function, then one should not accept the opinion of someone who claims to break the limb again from the beginning, for many ignorant physicians and bone-setters used to do this in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great deception, the easiest of which is the defect itself. But if the crookedness and complexity are soft, it should be straightened with water in which relaxing herbs have been cooked, such as marshmallow leaf and its root, rosemary and the like.
It is bandaged with relaxing compresses such as well-made diachylon, or saliva from the marshmallow root is taken and beaten with chicken fat and shear butter, and bandaged with it. Or, a ripe fig is taken and pounded with pigeon dove dung, and other remedies called anti-contraction agents.
And the complexity may be dissolved by continuous gentle massage with the hands, using the movement of the limb in all directions at all times. If the crookedness has been present for a long time and intensified and hardened, and necessity dictates treatment with iron.
It should be incised above and the bone connection released, and what remains of the crookedness or bone should be cut into delicate sections. Great gentleness and care should be used in doing so. Then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
Dislocation is the separation of a joint from its place, hindering movement and deforming the limb. It causes severe pain to the sufferer. Whenever someone suffers a dislocation, they should be promptly treated to return it to its proper position.
Do not delay treatment at all, for if it is delayed, the area will swell and become difficult to relocate. Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved or stretched while swollen because this often causes the sufferer severe cramps and pain.
But if this happens, the patient should be bled immediately and then left until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then, the limb should be softened with hot water and oil, and gently relocated. Each joint should then be treated as described in its respective place.
And I have also arranged the chapters on dislocations according to their progression from the upper part of the body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Lower Jaw.
Dislocations of the jaws are rare, and their separation occurs in two ways: either a slight displacement where they relax a little, or a complete dislocation where they become fully relaxed. Towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he is unable to hold it back, nor can he clench his jaw or speak clearly; as for slight dislocations, they often return on their own quite easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced quickly without delay; the assistant should hold the patient's head, and the surgeon should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side.
Or he should insert both thumbs if the dislocation is on both sides, and with the other fingers outside, align them; he then instructs the patient to relax his jaw and let it go in every direction, while the surgeon aligns the jaw and pushes until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to reduce them, especially if both jaws are dislocated, apply a compress soaked in hot water and oil to ease the reduction; do not delay the reduction as we have said. Once they are back in place, aligned, and the patient's mouth closes without relaxation, then place bandages of cloth over them with splints made of wax and oil.
Then tie them gently with a relaxed bandage; the patient should sleep on his back with his head elevated between two pillows so that he does not move it right or left and is not required to chew anything, but rather have soft, liquid food.
Even if the pain goes away and the jaw is set, let him eat what he pleases, but let him use it gently and not force open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If difficulty arises in returning the jaws to their place when they have been dislocated and do not return to their positions, it often results in fevers and persistent headaches, and sometimes the patient's abdomen will swell and he may vomit repeatedly. If you see this, know that it is a serious injury.
And often those who suffer from this die within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Returning the Jaw of the Shoulder and End of the Collarbone.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate inward due to its connection to the chest, but it may dislocate outward, which can be determined by touch. Force the patient to lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press on the area with your hand forcefully, and it will return. Then place a bandage and splints on it and tighten them. As for its end which joins the shoulder blade, it rarely dislocates. If it does happen to dislocate one day, it should be returned and straightened as we have described and what is suitable for you.
Then place a bandage, splints and tightening on it, and order the patient to observe rest and quiet until he recovers. This same treatment is used to return the end of the shoulder blade if it has also been dislocated.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On Returning a Dislocated Shoulder Blade.
Know that the shoulder blade can dislocate in three ways: one is towards the armpit downwards, the second is towards the chest, and rarely it can dislocate above the shoulder blade.
And it does not break off behind to the shoulder place, nor does it break off in front to the nerve place, and more often than not it breaks off and comes out below towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh because it goes out quickly and enters quickly in them.
But as for those with much flesh, it is the opposite, meaning that it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty, and perhaps some people may experience a blow or fall, so the elbow swells and becomes hot, and they think that it has broken off, so it should be examined until its soundness is determined.
Then you proceed in treating it, and you recognize the fracture if it is below towards the armpit; if you compare between the fractured elbow and the sound elbow, you will find an apparent difference, and you will find the head of the elbow therein dented, and under the armpit upon touching the head of the elbow as if it were an egg.
And the patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear, nor can he move it with all movements; likewise, if it breaks towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent upon touching and it is not hidden.
And this fracture may be easy to return if it is soft or if the patient is a boy, and returning it is done by having a servant raise his hand upwards; then you place your thumbs under his armpit and raise the joint strongly upward to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upward.
Then he puts it down; it will return quickly, if it does not return as we mentioned and the fracture has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use a poultice that relaxes and softens, such as cooking the root of marshmallow and fenugreek and rosemary in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back and you place under his armpit a ball of wool that is moderate between softness and hardness, then the doctor puts his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downward.
And he will push the ball with his foot, for it will return to its place. And if you wish to return it in this way, it is that you prepare a man taller than the patient and stop him from the side of the flank and insert his elbow under the patient's armpit and raise his armpit upwards until the patient is hanging in the air. And another servant will pull the patient's hand down to his stomach, and if the patient is light, something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, for the dislocation will return from its time. It may also be returned in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long wooden beam whose head is round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, then place it under the patient's armpit after placing a soft rag on the head of the wood and the patient standing upright along the wood.
Then he extends his hand down from the other side and also extends his body from the same side with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it by all that we have mentioned, use this treatment: take a wooden beam about two arm's lengths long. And its width is the size of four fingers and its thickness is the size of two fingers, it has a round head to make it easy to enter deep into the armpit in this way:
Then a soft rag is tied to the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient, then it is placed under the armpit and the entire hand and arm are extended on the wood and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm and end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a horizontal ladder beam and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side, for the joint will enter its place quickly. And if it enters in any way possible, then it is necessary to put a moderate-sized ball of wool under the armpit.
Then a dressing is applied, prepared from flour dust with frankincense and egg white, to the whole shoulder as it turns upwards; then the ball is firmly tightened from under the armpit and bound over the dressing from above, and his hand is hung around his neck.
His hand is left unmoved for seven days, and the patient's food should be kept light so that the limb may become strong, for this is quicker for its healing; then after seven days or five it is unbound and exercised by movement. If it remains firm and does not relax, it is healed.
But if the joint dislocates frequently due to dampness or some other cause, cautery should be used with three white-hot irons as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint does not remain stable...
...the dressing and binding are repeated several times, but it does not hold, falls, and relaxes, and you cannot raise it upwards, know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed or stretched or relaxed; then know that the joint will never remain fixed in its place.
As for dislocation towards the chest, the breast and behind, it is reduced by pressure and extension with the hands until it returns, and all bandages and treatment are used until it is healed. If stiffness occurs in the limb after healing and movement is slow...
â€¦let the patient frequently use the bath so that the stiffness softens and returns to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Treatment of Elbow Dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty. It dislocates in all directions, especially forwards or backwards. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it lies under sight and touch in whichever way it has dislocated.
And if you join the dislocated joint to the sound one, it will become clear to you, by the obvious depression of the joint, that he cannot bend his arm nor touch it to his elbow, and you should hasten to return the dislocation from your hour before a hot swelling occurs for it.
For if a hot swelling occurs for him, it will be difficult to return it and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward, for it is the worst of all types of dislocations, the most painful, and death often follows with it.
And if his fracture is something that can be returned, let a servant extend his hand with both of his hands and arms outstretched, and the doctor's hand from above the elbow and below it, while he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, it may be returned by bending the hand at once so that its base strikes the elbow opposite it; but if the dislocation does not return, use extreme force, which is to extend the arm with two servants.
And let the patient also be held by two servants so that he does not move during the extension, then the arm is rotated in all directions after a folded cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped around it. And if the doctor touches the joint with his hands, he should rub it with oil to make it easier for the joint to slip.
Then the joint should be pushed hard until it returns, and after it returns, a bandage with a grip and drying with egg white should be applied to it and tightened firmly, and the arm should be hung from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is loosened, and if the joint remains in place, the bandage is removed from it and left alone. If you see that the joint has not become strong, reapply the bandage and binding and leave it for several days until it strengthens and then remove it.  If he experiences pain after the joint returns and is released
If there is stiffness in movement, let him use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and pressure until it softens, or place on the joint a sheep's bladder then tie it and leave it for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in the bath, and when he sweats, rub the joint moderately.
Then repeat the bladder two and three times with entering the bath until it softens. And if you wish to place wet, softened cow dung mixed with butter and bind it on, do that several times for it will soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Wrist Dislocation.
The wrist of the hand is often dislocated, and its reduction is easy unlike other joints; however, it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the hour it is dislocated before swelling or a hot tumor appears in the place.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board and have an attendant extend his hand; then the physician places his hand on the prominence of the joint and pushes until it returns. However, he must look to see if the dislocation has moved toward the palm of the hand.
Let the patient place the back of his hand on the board when extending and reducing it, and if the dislocation is prominent toward the back of the hand let him place the palm of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, or else bind it with a bandage that relieves swelling.
Leave it and do not let it reoccur, for it cannot bear it, and you will not be able to reduce it after several days have passed; however the joint remains on its place and does not harm the patient at all except if the hand becomes inactive and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve has been cut or become diseased; there is no remedy except to bind it tightly, perhaps it will be of benefit and perhaps not. If the wrist has been reduced, apply the bandage we have described, then bind it tightly and leave it for five days.
Then it is softened and trained, for if its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness in it, soften it with warm water and rub it repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction, so when a finger dislocates towards the back of the hand or its palm side, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then tie the tip of the finger and hang it towards the side to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight that day. If it is at night, tie it in the same way, but do not stop loosening it during the day and train it with movement and tie it at night. Do this for days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it dislocates towards the palm side, tying it towards the same direction and repeating your initial actions until it heals. Likewise, do this for all other directions of dislocation.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Vertebrae.
When a vertebra from the vertebrae of the back or neck becomes completely dislocated, or when many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, as death hastens to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool comes out involuntarily, and they are often unable to hold it back. Often some of their limbs relax, either both legs or both arms, or one of them. If only one vertebra is displaced, it often returns to its place.
The dislocation can occur in four directions: the one that dislocates backwards is called a hump. As for its treatment, if the hump occurred in childhood, there is no treatment and it will not heal at all. But if it occurred from a fall or blow, etc.
The ancients have mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy discourse, most of which is not very beneficial. I have abridged this, so that a little suffices for much of what they have brought forth in terms of approximation to the meaning and its explanation, and I have depicted the instrument differently from how they have described it.
I say that the hump which occurs in front of the chest is neither curable nor treatable, nor is that which occurs on either side. Only that which occurs in the back can be treated, particularly as I will describe: by laying the patient face down.
On a level bench near a wall, spread out a damp mat underneath so that his chest is not hurt; then place an upright plank set in a hole in the ground at the head end of the bench and another plank toward his feet at the other end of the bench.
One attendant holds the plank, which is not fixed in the hole, and another attendant holds the other plank in that position; then a soft, tight bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under his armpits. The end of the bandage is extended to the plank at his head and tied there.
Then another bandage is tightened above his thighs, above his knees and at his ankles; all the bandages are gathered together and tied to the other plank at his feet. Then each attendant extends their plank with the bandage, and the planks do not move from their fixed positions.
However, they are not fixed as we have said, and the physician places their hands firmly on the hump until it subsides or places a board on it and then leans on the board with their legs until it subsides. If it does not subside with this treatment, take a board that is about three cubits long.
And a notch is carved into the wall we mentioned should be near the patient, into which the end of the board is inserted; then the middle of the board is placed on the hump and the physician places their feet on the other end and pulls tightly so that the hump is compressed.
Return it to its place, and if you wish to make it with a screw that is twisted by hand, which is to insert at the head of the patient in the last part of the stretcher two pieces of wood, each being an arm's length long, and at his feet also in the last part of the stretcher, two pieces.
Let the distance between each piece of wood be a span, and let there be made in each piece of wood a hole through which the screw is passed and all the wood is firmly secured to the ground so that it does not move at all, and insert a round stick which is the screw in which the ligature is twisted in the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end there is a hole in which a stick a span long is fixed, with which it is twisted, and the same is done in the other two pieces of wood; then tighten the ligature that you tightened on the patient's chest in the screw at his head and the ligatures that you tightened on his legs in the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands by each screw and twists his hand with the wrench with which the screw is twisted, and the surgeon straightens the bump as we have described, and this is a picture of the screw, the stretcher, and the patient.
Then, after the hernia has returned and the area is level, you should apply a dry dressing with egg white then a bandage, and place over the dressing a splint of wood which is three fingers wide or thereabouts, and its length should be as much as takes the place of the bump.
And on top of some correct beads, then it is tied with the ligature which should be used; the patient should use soft food until he recovers. If there remains some swelling in the place at the end of recovery, you should apply medications that relax and soften, while using the board we described for a long time.
You may use a lead plate for this; and it may happen that a swelling appears at the end of the dorsal vertebrae, which is mistaken for a dislocation; this is an extra bone that has grown, so do not treat it with this method, for death may occur.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of luxated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and subsidence that affect all other joints. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward. Dislocation is most common inward and least common forward or backward. The sign of inward dislocation is that if you align the healthy leg of the patient with the injured leg, the injured leg will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend their leg at the hip. The area below the hip will be warm and swollen, clearly indicating that the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outward are the opposite of these signs. The sign of anterior dislocation is that the patient can fully extend their leg, but cannot bend it without pain. Pain will be present in the knee. If they try to walk, they will not be able to move forward.
Urine retention and swelling of the groin will occur, and when walking, they will land on their heels. The sign of posterior dislocation is that they cannot extend the knee, nor can they bend it before bending the hip. Their leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin will be relaxed and the head of the femur will be clearly visible near the waist. As for reducing these dislocations, if it is an old dislocation that has been present for a long time and the patient has not attempted to reduce it, then there is no treatment at all.
It should not be treated. But if it is a recent dislocation of any type, one of the four directions, then hurry to manipulate the joint, extending it inward and outward, and moving it right and left; perhaps
If the dislocation returns and does not require further treatment, it is sufficient. If it does not return in this way, a strong servant should be employed to support his leg from below, either with their hands or with a splint tied above the knee, and another servant to support him from above.
He should insert his hands under the armpits and then tighten a soft splint around the base of the thigh, with another servant holding the end of the splint. This servant should extend the leg either from the front from the groin to the shoulder or from behind to the back.
All of them should extend simultaneously until the patient is raised off the ground and suspended. This type of extension is a common one for all four types, if the dislocation returns as we said, or else each type requires its own specific treatment as I have mentioned.
As for the specific treatment if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their right side and a splint should be applied to the base of the thigh between the head of the femur and the area below the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the shoulder. Another strong servant then takes hold of the thick part of the affected thigh with their arms and extends it outwards forcefully, causing it to return to its place.
This type is easier than all other types of treatment for returning this limb. If this proves difficult and you cannot proceed with this type of treatment at all, then both legs of the patient should be bound together with a strong soft bandage on the heels and knees.
There should be a distance of four finger widths between each leg, with the affected leg extended more than the other by two fingers. The patient is then hung from their head from a beam in the house, suspended at a distance of two arm lengths from the ground.
Then order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh and let another boy cling to the patient; then have the latter boy push hard against the thigh, for the joint will quickly return to its place. As for returning a dislocated jaw that is out.
The patient should lie on the plank as we described in Al-Hadba's work, and the bandage should be tightened on his affected leg, especially on his chest; then two planks should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; then an additional plank should be placed in the middle of the plank, securely fastened.
Wind a damp cloth around it so as not to hurt the patient, so that the plank is between his thighs lest he be pulled down during stretching; then each attendant stretches towards his side and the physician straightens the hip with his hands; if it responds by returning.
If not, place the board on it and press it down as we mentioned in Al-Hadba, except that the patient should lie on his right side; and if the dislocation is forward, stretch the patient's leg once while he is in this exact position on the plank.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected thigh and then presses it with his other hand, while extending the pressure downwards towards the knee; if the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be extended downward.
Rather than being raised off the ground, he should be placed on something solid; and it should also be the case that if his hip is dislocated outward as we mentioned regarding his position on the plank while on his face with bandages tightened.
As we said earlier, pressing with the board should also be used on the location to which the joint has dislocated. If the hip joint returns properly and the sign of its return is not hidden from you, it is that you extend the patient's legs.
If you see them level and the patient draws up his leg and extends it without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper position; then join the thighs together, apply a bandage and tighten with a turban so firmly that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then the bandage and ligature should be removed and the leg compared to the other. If they are equal in size, know that the fracture has set and allow the patient to walk. If you see any laxity.
Redo the bandage and tighten it according to your initial tightness, and leave it for another three days. Then remove it and allow him to walk slowly on it for several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter the Thirty-Second and Thirty-Third.
On the Treatment of a Knee Fracture.
The knee fractures in three ways: inward, outward, and downward - meaning backward -  and it never fractures forward. A sign of a fracture is to order the patient to bring his leg up to his thigh.
If it does not touch the thigh, know that the knee is fractured.  To set all types of fractures, have the patient sit with his legs extended if he is strong enough, and have an assistant stand behind him to grasp his waist and twist him slightly backward. Then you should sit on his thighs.
Press your back against his face, place his leg between yours, then grip his knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers on his knee. Then squeeze the sides of his knee firmly with your hands while another assistant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign that it has returned to its place is that the leg will touch the thigh easily and without force. Then bandage it, pressing the leg against the thigh, and bind them all together with a bandage for three or four days, then release it. Only allow a little walking for several days until it strengthens.
If it is impossible for you to return it in the manner we have described, otherwise use strong traction with bandages which I have previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter thirty-three.
In the treatment of a fractured heel.
The heel may separate slightly or completely fracture, and the fracture can be either inward or outward. The sign of a fracture is that you will see the heel swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has fractured.
As for the treatment of a slight separation, it is easy to return it by gently pulling with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it fractures completely, the patient should sit down and be held by a strong attendant from behind his middle back.
Then hold the patient's foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below at the ankle, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand toward the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg in the third attempt while pulling on the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts with this method and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the process because it will return.
If it refuses to return as we have described, otherwise lay the patient on his back on the ground and insert a strong peg firmly into the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so as not to hurt the patient, then let an attendant hold his thigh.
Then another attendant extends the leg, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around the neck of the leg; then each attendant pulls contrary to the pull of the other and the peg remains fixed between the patient's thighs to hold him so that his body does not slip down during the pulling; then the doctor straightens the fracture with his hands.
Another attendant should hold the sound leg down; then, the jaw will return quickly. When the jaw returns and it becomes clear to you that its return is sound, apply a dressing and bandage, tightening it with strong ligatures, and tie the foot to the bandage below. Care must be taken of the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not tight on it and cause it pain, then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day, and the patient must refrain from walking for forty days.
If he wishes to walk before this period, you cannot be sure that the jaw will not dislocate and become spoiled and no longer accept treatment. If a hot swelling appears, one should use what has been previously described in another section of the treatment and bandaging until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Fourth.
On the Treatment of a Fracture of the Fingers.
One should straighten what is fractured with a slight extension without force; this is not difficult but rather easy. If the fracture is in some of the phalanges of the toes, the patient should place his foot on a level surface.
Or on a plank while standing as if walking, then you should place your foot on what is above those joints and then step on it forcefully until it returns and you see that it has become straight and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that will take the whole foot and has two heads, then tighten it firmly for three days, then loosen it and keep it from walking for many days until it becomes strong and you are safe from its return, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Fifth.
On the Types of Fracture that Occur with a Wound or with a Break, or Both Together.
When something of this kind occurs and it is treated with medication and bandaging, death often follows.
Therefore, only one who is skilled in the craft, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, neither hasty nor reckless, should undertake to treat such a condition. He should begin by using medicines that soothe only hot tumors and then leave the patient alone.
By God's will, only what I hope for safety from affliction with the lightness of the disease, and if you see some hope in it, return it to its initial hour before a hot tumor occurs.
If the member returns as desired, use a remedy that soothes hot tumors and treat the wound with drying ointments suitable for it. If the fracture is accompanied by bone splinters that have become detached, remove them. Follow what we have mentioned regarding simple diseases previously in their respective places.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of risk as I have previously advised you, for that is most lasting for your reputation and safest for your appearance, God willing.
